The RED PAGE
Billy —Paul Methven (Sydney Book Club).
Billy, of course, is a girl, strong, tall,
and fond of golf. The Hon. Jerry is a
feeble little man with an interest in
fossils. Both regard matrimony with
horror, and are inspired to enter into a
formal marriage contract, for offensive
and defensive purposes. Two difficult
characters are steered cleverly through
an entertaining comedy, in which there
is a capital divorce trial. . . . This is
Methven’s second book, and he looks
like a good trier for a place next to
W. J. Locke.
The Lass with the Delicate Air —A. Goring
Thomas (Sydney Book Club). A bright
and witty yarn by an ex-Sydney man.
The Hicks family and Mr. Goe, the ar-
tist, who said proudly ,“I am the new
movement,” are worth knowing. *
The Window at the White Cat —Mary Rine-
hart (Geo. Robertson and Co.). Mur-
der and disappearances neatly wrapped
in mystery, which is unfolded without
the aid of the usual detective. Miss
Rinehart employs Munsterberg’s psy-
choteraphy in order to extract evidence
from an unwilling witness. Cleveland
Moffit did it more effectively in Through
the Wall.
A Woman on the Threshold —Maude Little
(Chatto and Windus). A woman who
wants to write a novel is stopped by her
stodgy husband ; later on her son shows
the characteristics of her hero, and acts
the unwritten story. There are some
unusual characters in this readable
book. The story is told in episodes; the
time and place of each being given at
the chapter head —which is a conveni-
encei
The Best Australian Prose
Some time ago a Red Page competition
brought out many good lines of Australian
verse not generally known before, and illus-
trated the quality of the authors fairly well.
It may not be easy to apply this method of
exhibiting “form at a glance” to our prose
writers; but it is worth trying.
Will readers look up their Australian
books and magazines, and send in the prose
passages (not more than 200 words each)
which they consider the best in style. Aus-
tralian authors are taken to be those natives
or immigrants who have written in Australia
about anything. Thus Marcus Clarke,
born in England, is an Australian author;
Gilbert Murray, born in Sydney, is not.
A prize of £1 Is. is offered to the first
sender of the prose passage adjudged best
by the editor of this page. Last date for re-
ceipt—3lst May.

IRONY.
; I called on Fame: the office boy
Said : “Please send in your name.”
“Indeed I have none,” I replied;
< ' And so, away I came.
I called on Wealth: I Was required
To pay an entrance fee:
“I have no money,” I replied,
And left immediately.
I called on Love: it was decreed
That first I show my heart.
“She has it,” was all that I could say:
And then I did depart.
O. Henry (The American Magazine) .
O. Henry
Since the death of O. Henry, in the middle
of last year, two new collections of his
stories have been published— Strictly Busi-
ness and Whirligigs (G. Robertson and
Go.). “An Adventure in Neurasthenia,”
the last story he wrote, is to be issued as
Let Me Feel Your Pulse ; and some stragglers
may be rounded up for yet another volume.
Of the 11 books so far published, all are
collections of short stories save the first,
Cabbages and Kings, and that is really a
number of episodes loosely strung together.
Their quality is very even; in fact, to those
who read only for amusement, the O.
Henry stories are like beer—and, as you
know, there is no bad beer.
This large amount of work was written in
a small number of years, for he did not
begin until late, and he died at 43. Born
William Sidney Porter, in North Carolina,
he spent his youth in Texas, and, later, be
knocked about in Central America and New
Orleans. He tried various occupations, in-
cluding journalism, qntil the editor of
Ainslee’s Magazine invited him to New
York with an offer of 1200 dollars for 12
short stories. Eventually he got as much
as 1000 dollars per story paid in advance.
There was no inducement to do anything
better, though he said that he wanted to
write a real novel, which would startle by
telling the naked truth. If he had written
such a book, the chances are that it would
fall flat after the hundreds of startles he
had got into his short stories.
The unexpected ending was the feature
of his plots, as it is of most detective and
mystery stories. The O. Henry yarn closed
so often with a snap that the unexpected
ending came to be expected. Only in a
few cases did he depart from the formula,
and it required no little invention to vary
the surprise. Like Poe, he built his story
with that end in view; but when Poe had
exploited one kind of story he passed to an-
other. O. Henry, like the cat in the adage,
had virtually only one trick, which paid
better than nine of other men’s. Paid in
cash, of course, for enduring fame was not
to be expected. Nevertheless, it is no mean
thing to have amused, as O. Henry has
done, so many readers of his own genera-
tion.
In reaching his end he made use of slang,
incongruities of speech which are generally
humorous, and the boisterous perversions
usually associated with the stage Irish-
man. Here is a representative passage
from his first book:
“Monseer,” says I, “I don’t know where
your country is, but me heart bleeds for it.
The heart of a Clancy was never deaf to
the sight of an oppressed people. The
family is filibustered by birth, and for-
eigners by trade. If you can use James
Clancy’s arm and his blood in denudin’ your
shores of the tyrant’s yoke, they’re yours to
command.”
These are random examples of plain English
translated into O. Henry:
“Segregate yourself from your pseudo-
equine quadruped and come inside.”
“Mrs. Brown,” says I, “I am going to ex-
tend my feet alternately one after another
in such a manner and direction that this
tenement will recede from me in the quickest
possible time.”
“For 15 years I was the ruling power
behind old Sambo Benavides, the Royal
High Thumbscrew of the republic. You’ve
seen his picture in the papers—a mushy
black man, with whiskers like the notes on
a Swiss music box cylinder, and a scroll
in his right hand like the one they write
births on in the family Bible. Well, that
chocolate potentate used to be the biggest
item of interest anywhere between the color
line and the parallels of latitude. It was
three throws, horses, whether he was to wind
up in the Hall of Fame or the Bureau of
Combustibles. He’d have been sure called
the Roosevelt of the Southern Continent if
it hadn’t been that Grover Cleveland was
President at the time.”
But, besides the visible mechanism, and
the easily acquired style, there is the epi-
sode, the adventure, the romance—which is
the body of the story. O. Henry got that
from life. He mixed with all sorts of men
in many conditions of life, and he gathered
material from them all. “So long as a
story is true to human nature,” he'’ said,
“all you need to do to make it fit any town
is to change the local cdlor. You can make
all the characters of the ‘Arabian Nights’
parade up and down Broadway.” And of
the same Broadway he said, in another
place—“ The third Jungle Book was there
waiting for somebody to put covers on it.”
O. Henry’s reflections on life were not
very deep and serious; but he scattered a lot
of horse sense in his fun:
“When ye run down politeness ye take
the mortar from between the bricks of the
foundations of society.”
“The key to Liberty is not in the hands
of License, but Convention holds it.
Comity has a toll gate at which you must
pay, or you may not enter the land of free-
dom.”
“I have found out the biggest hazard in
the world, a game of chance that never is
concluded, a venture that may end in the
highest heaven or the blackest pit. It will
keep a man on edge until the clods fall on
his coffin, because he will never know—-not
until his last day, and not then will he
know. It is a voyage, without a rudder or
compass, and you must be captain and crew,
and keep watch yourself day and night, with
no one to relieve you. I have found the
Venture ... I married her yesterday at
noon.”
“Let’s have the moral first and be done
with it. All is not gold that glitters, but
it is a wise child that keeps the stopper
on the bottle of testing acid.”
The moral of many of his stories, such as
“The Cop and the Anthem,” was that the
Obvious is often missed. The moral of O.
Henry’s success is that the story-writer may
find romance and advepture in his own
neighborhood if he has eyes to see it.
His quality is that of smart journalism,
which appeals to a particular audience for
a limited time. It never equalled that of
Maupassant at his best, nor are his stories
to be compared with those of Balzac, Steven-
son and Kipling. Amongst living writers
in America Ambrose Bierce gave a deeper
thrill with his tricky endings, and Leonard
Merrick has a gayer, more spontaneous hu-
mor. On his own level, however, O. Henry
was supreme.
Hermann Sudermann
Very little of the literature of modern
Germany has any international value. The
influence of Nietzsche has stimulated a num-
ber of poets who have thrilled home readers
with strange weird notes; but none has found
a translator capable of carrying him across
the border alive. In drama, though there
does not seem to be much imaginative
beauty, there is power, and here Sudermann
is still supreme, with Hauptmann, Schnitzler
and Wedekind as his only serious rivals.
German novels are comparatively unimpor-
tant, and Sudermann’s triumph in this de-
partment was easily gained. He is the only
German author with a foreign reputation of
any size.
Sudermann’s fame abroad is mainly due
to “Magda,” which caught the Ibsen tide
before it turned, and went the round of the
world’s theatres. Other dramas —“John
the Baptist,” “Three Heron Feathers,” “Fires
of St. John” and “The Joy of Living”—have
been translated, and, occasionally, acted.
They are, however, unsuited for the average
British audience.
Neither in drama or novel does Sudermann
seek to amuse. His satire is not light, but
deep. His main concern, however, is to por-
tray life. He has plumbed many depths and
recesses in human nature, and the psycholo-
gical material with which his characters are
built is fuller than usual in English fiction.
He presents phases of life as completely as
he sees them; wit’ out reticence, but without
any of the sickly zest in erotic sensations
which yellows the pages of D’Annunzio, and
without the specialism in filth which de-
stroys some of Zola’s work.
Dame Care, The Undying Past and Re-
gina, appeared in English some years ago.
They are rather sombre studies—the first
of bashfulness, the others of heredity—
treated with masterful directness and vigor.
The Song of Songs (Das Hohe Lied) is
much longer than its predecessors, and more
varied in interest. Published in Germany
in 1908, a translation by Thomas Seltzer ap-
peared in America in the following year, and
edition after edition was demanded there
before John Lane printed it in England.
The British Libraries Committee promptly
banned it, together with the harmless Cottage
Pie of Neil Lyons.
It may be said at the outset that The
Song of Songs is not a novel for crippled or
feeble intellects. It does not belong to the
order of buttered muffin fiction, which is
served up for maidens’ breakfasts, nor is it
a seasoned kickshaw for the rouri’s lunch.
It is a full meal for a healthy adult —or, to
the food simile—a story of a repre-
sentative woman told dispassionately.
Lilly Czepanek is the daughter of a music
master who disappeared in her fourteenth
year, leaving, besides some debts, the score of
an oratorio—-“ The Song of Songs.” She started
her life badly, through no fault of her own,
and fell, rose again, and again fell.
At length she met one man who awakened
the best in her, and for him she wished to
slough her old self, and live a new life. He,
a student, could not marry without the con-
sent of his uncle, and this tough old warrior
visits Berlin to inspect the bride-elect.
The scene in which the uncle gets Lilly to
betray her past unconsciously, is a remark-
able one. Next day he tells her that the
marriage is not to be.
“Because he is a rising man. He wants
to climb to the top. He is still blessed with
faith, and you no longer possess it. . . I’m
not laying so'much stress on last night’s
eye-opener. It’s not the appearance of a
coastline that counts. It doesn’t matter
whether it’s covered with palms or sand.
The important thing is the interior. And
in the interior I see steppes—scorched, waste
land—no birds flying across it —a desert
where confidence will not strike root. Crawl
into whatever shelter life offers you, little
one. Cling to those who brought you to
the pass you are in. But let the boy go.”
Suicide is the natural end for Lilly. She
decides to throw herself into the river, and
takes with her the score of “The Song of
Songs,” to which she had clung all through
her checkered career.
“Now one. leap and the thing was done.
‘Yes, I’ll do it, I’ll do it,’ a voice within her
called. But she had to send ‘The Song of
Songs’ ahead. It would be a hindrance to
her as she climbed over (the railing). She
threw it —a bit of white flitted by-—a
splash below —sharp and distinct, which
made her tingle all over like a slap in the
face.
“When she heard the sound, she knew
she would never do it.”
She didn’t, and the edd comes with a rush
—unexpectedly cynical.
If any adult is any the worse for reading
a full-bodied book of this kind, which cuts
across a big section of life in a modern capi-
tal, then he or she is past curing.
The Chronicle of Turner
“It’s an historical work,” said the book-
seller to Sylvestre Bonnard in Anatole
France’s story.
“In that case,” replied Bonnard, “it would
be very wearisome to read, for historical
works that do not lie are exceedingly dull.”
The same objection might be offered to
The First Decade of the Australian Com-
monwealth, by H. Gyles Turner (Mason,
Firth and McCutcheon). It does not pre-
tend to be anything more than a chronicle,
and the author has tried to make it a com-
plete one. His bias against the Labor
party, however, is manifest from the begin-
ning, and his account of events is somewhat
discolored by Tory prejudice.
The members of the respective Ministries
are summed up from that point of view. J.
0. Watson receives praise for his moderation
and good temper; Hughes appears something
like a Robespierre; Deakin has disappointed
the author; Bruce Smith is his only hero.
Bruce the Mirabeau of a decaying pluto-
cracy ! Smith the last hope of Anti-Sosh! 1
One can imagine how he will shine in the
pages of the Japanese Carlyle of the future.
How eloquent his-last words will be! And
a vision comes of Bruce Smith sitting on
the ruins of North Sydney bridge, shaking
his large wise head, and saying—"If they
had only listened to me!” j
H. G. Turner seems to favor the olid gag
that history is philosophy teaching by ex-
amples. A truer phrase for chronicles such
as this is that of Coleridge—history is
like the stern-lights of a ship, it illumines
only the path that has been travelled. In
this case the light shines through a pea-green
glass.

The velvety-eyed heroine of The Black
Pearl, by Noel Aimir (G. Robertson and
Co.) is a not-too-Frencb French girl, who
corned to Australia and conquers at first
sight. No. lis a parson, and they are
engaged ; but, because the gay Parisienne
cannot swallow the whole 39 articles at
once, he, with fine abandon, sacrifices his
love and goes to the leper settlement at
Molokai. No. 2 is the reincarnation of a
Rajah, a masterful chap, but, unfortunately,
already married. That does riot matter to
him, and he carries the little fortress by
storm. The situation is interesting when
Theosophy comes in to save it, and the spas-
modic story closes in a blaze of the seven
mystic colors, with the Divine Triangle ap-
proaching.

“V.V.V.” supplies information: —
Duckworth issues a translation in slightly
archaic English of La Premiere Tentation
de St. Antoine by Flaubert, first edited
from the MSS. (1849-56) by Louis Bertrand
(Paris, Fosquelb, 1908). Though scorned
by Bouilhet and Du Camp, this first form
was loved by the author, and only the
Bovary prosecution prevented its publica-
tion in 1856. As literature the book is of
secondary interest, compared with the 1874
version, but valuable for the light it throws
on Flaubert himself. Unlike the later
form, it is subjective and personal, diffuse
in style, and the dramatic element is more
pronounced. The gorgeous language, that
in places has a cadence as of verse, is very
effective in scenes like that where Simon
Magus and Helen describe her successive
transformations, or where Apollonius tells
of his many wanderings. The misguided
English editor omits a great part of Ber-
trand’s useful introduction, and includes a
negligible preface by Sir G. Maspero.

Gaston Leroux has a talent for the
tangled detective story, unsolved till the
last page. He now writes Le fan-
tome de VOpira, a long-winded mystery
of an invisible presence, unrelieved by his
crude attempts at humor. Meantime what
is happening to Rouletabille, who two years
ago went to Russia on secret service, after
his Yellow Room and Lady in Black Adven-
tures. Leroux’s earlier book, La double vie
de M. Longuet, now available in a six-
penny edition, is only an indifferent shocker.
The popularity of Lindsay Gordon’s verses must
be growing abroad, as one often sees quotations
from them in books and newspapers. Dorothea
Conyers in The Strayings of Sandy (1907) prints
bits of Gordon at the head of nearly every chap-
ter, So does Louise Gerard in The Golden Centipede
(1910)—without adding the poet’s name. “Life
is mostly froth and bubble,’’ etc., was quoted by
one of the characters in Galsworthy’s A Country
House as his rule of life. Lines from Gordon also
adorn the chapter heads of Dolf Wyllarde’s The
Riding Mastei —which, by the way, is a readable
yarn,

London Truth took a vote of its readers,
to ascertain the most popular novels of
1910. The result is surprisingly good:
I. The Dop Doctor —Dehan.
2. Clayhanger —Bennett.
3. Howard's End —Forster.
4. Let the Roof Fall In —Danby.

THE GRAY NORNS.
What do you bring in your sacks, Gray Girls?
“Sea-sand and sorrow.”
What is that mist that behind you whirls?
“The souls of to-morrow.”
What are those shapes on the windy coasts?
“The dead souls going.”
And what are those loads on the backs of the
.ghosts?
“The seed of their sowing!”
Edwin Markham (in Vedenta Magazine) .

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
Senlac.
Oh, the stifl shone down on Senlac, and it filing the
mists before it,
And it warmed them through their armur as they
Waded in the sea,
And it flashed upon their standard so the mail-
clad might that bore it
Hurled his war song on before it
Till the sea cliffs caught the clangor and gave
birth to melody,
Iron melody.
Then aloud cried Norman William, through his
ruddy great beard laughing,
As he shook aloft his double-headed death-axe
joyously:
“Lads! Here’s England lies before us, full of
ale for each man’s quaffing,
Full of lasses, lithe and laughing,
And a throne that ■ Saxon Harold, foresworn
Harold, warms for me ;
Shall we back to sea?”
Then a shout rang, that rebounded from the hol-
low vault of Heaven,
And there flashed along the sea’s face, the long
swords, silverly ;
Sospied William far-off armor. “ Lads,” he cried.
“ Here’s welcome given,
Let us send our hosts to Heaven !
Give them rather Heaven than England; that
we’ll hold, as anciently
We did Normandy.”
By the short-grassed hill-top, Harold, on his
twisted bull-hide bending,
Eyed the blood-rust on Ins war-sword, and he
eyed it wearily ;
Yet his slackened sinews tightened; “Saxons,”
called he, “make an ending !
See the Norman bow-staves bending !
Let us drive them as we drove the Danes, to
drink the bitter sea ! ”
And they cheered him, cheerily.
So the swift shafts sang together like a flight of
war-birds whirring,
That through shield and helm and byrnie thrust
keen beaks eagerly.
And the grim war-eagles gathered where the
crushed corpse yet was stirring,
And the grey wolf watched their whirring,
And the Valkyrs reaped their harvest in the
meadows by the sea,
Choosing silently.
Thrice the Saxon war-wedge thundered, crashing
through the sons of Rollo,
Thrice the Norman death-axe turned it, bursting
passage free.
“Blood of Christ!” cries Harold, helmless, “Men
of S.>xon England, follow,
Will, ye have the breed of Kollo,
Bastard pirates, France-fat Northmen, to subdue
us utterly?
England and victory ! ”
As along the windy heaven flares the full-moon,
stormy-wreathed
Flared he helmless through the foemen, fair-
haired Harold, furiously,
And the winged-helms flashed and faded as the
stars in Heaven are sheathed
When the yellow moon’s unwreathed.
For his battle-edge through bone and brain bit
deep and deathfully.
Gods ! What a sight to see !
Quoth then Norman William’s cunning to his
archers “ Aim ye higher,
Let us give them rain from Heaven an’ they fight
so thirstily ! ”
And the Saxon shield-ring shivered in the flaming
hail of fire,
And to Harold, towering higher,
Soft and sweet the Valkyrs whispered, and a
feathered death found he,
Swiftly, and silently.
Oh, the harvest moon glows yellow, on the vultures’
harvest peeping,
And the grey wolf snarls beneath the moon where
red-toothed slinketh he,
For along the gleaming battle-wrack there floats a
woman’s weeping,
’Midst death-grey faces peeping—
What’s that grim thing her unbound hair falls
over, goldenly ?
O God ! That let this be !
n.s.w.
H. M. GREEN.
God and I.
There is one great bond between us that is neither
worn nor frayed,
Though He reigns supreme in Heaven and I till
the Earth He made ;
Though He is the great Creator —I the smallest,
trampled clod.
Yet there is this bond between us—we are weary,
I and God!
By the stern Decree of Eden, I must wring from
barren lands
Food for my toil-racked body, whilst He sits with
folded hands ;
Low and softly in these ages the celestial choirs do
sing.
For a palace sinks to silence when in slumber lies
the King.
In the primal days of kingship, so the hoary legend
says,
He created this vast plaything in six mighty
working days ;
But the task no doubt distressed Him, and He
sought a life of ease ;
Now unheeded spins the plaything through the
careless centuries.
Now I see the temples totter, and I hear the
chanting cease
When with those first days of vigor we contrast
this deathly peace;
For the prayer remains unanswered and the weak
and helpless fall.
And the tyrant goes unhindered whilst His faint-
ing servants call.
And now, having hurled this pebble, to my glass-
house I’ll retire,
To continue my earth labor and reluctantly
perspire;
And I bid you when I perish carve above this
head of mine:
“Yes, he sinned both much and deeply—but his
tiredness was divine.”
W.A.

THURSDAY, APRIL 6, 1911.
Prick 6d.
VICTORIA’S MARRIAGE BOOM.
The marriage-rate was largely increased last year, for the first time in the history of the State reaching the 10,000 level. The exact figure is 10,240. Double
this, and we find that 20,480 people have been made happy or otherwise during the year which has recently become a memory. Out of each thousand people in
Victoria 15‘66 celebrated the ceremony. —Daily Paper.
HYMEN (to Cupid): “See here, I’m worked to death. If this is the result of your Labor Government, do you expect
me to vote ‘Yes’ at the Referendum?”

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper {and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters {with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
therej the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the cyntents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 ivords, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are, en-

ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
lurther, those sending MS. to distinctly understand

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any pic-
ture which has to be re-drawn —-will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, APRIL 6, 1911.
THE REFERENDA AND THE
RAILWAYS.
E. F. Mitchell, K.C., a Victorian hard-
shell Tory, supplied “Liberalism” with a
text after its own heart the other
day. He argued that, assuming the Refer-
enda questions are answered the way the
Australian Government desires, “the Federal
Labor Caucus will have power to make laws
with respect to practically every matter con-
nected with the management and control of
every State railway; it could regulate all
freights, the times and conditions of running
of trains, etc.” This, in Mitchell’s opin-
ion, will produce that nrnst unthinkable of
national disasters, Disquiet in the Pawn-
shop. “If,” he says, “the creditors realise
that the whole control and management of
the railways of each State is entirely at the
mercy of the Federal Parliament —which at
present may mean the Federal Labor caucus
—is it likely that the States will be in a
position to borrow money in the future?”
It is a subtle appeal to the many who be-
lieve implicitly that the nation which does
not live by pawning its clothes and keeping
a permanent bill of sale on the furniture is
Lost. But, though it is subtle, it is bogus.
On a matter of this sort the foreign Cohen
will take the opinion of the foreign railway
expert; and here is the foreign expert’s
opinion, as already impartially stated in the
English Railway Gazette (16/12/TO) —a
journal issued in the interests of Private En-
terprise,' as represented by the leading Rail-
way Companies oi the United Kingdom: —
To say that the Federal Court or Government will
in future control the railways is, of course, not ac-
curate All it [the Court] will do will be to
say, when appealed to, what are and what are not
fair rates of pay and conditions of service. The in-
dividual States would merely stand in the same posi-
tion as an} - other great employer, the condition in
whose employ are already controlled by the Fee.oral
Arbitration Courts. These Courts are very impartial
and highly respected, and are further removed from
political intrigue than the State institutions. The
MOVE WOULD APPEAR TO BE A WISE ONE, even If it
did tend to weaken State railway control, which it
will not do.
No doubt Cohen would have reason to be
alarmed if he assumed that the Australian
people chose lunatics for their legislators,
and gibbering idiots for their Judges. if
they were in the habit of doing that, and if
the lunatic legislators chose their wildest
maniacs for Ministers, the Australian Gov-
ernment might no doubt have all millionaires,
liars, tide-waiters, auctioneers, and remit-
tance-men warned off the State railways for
life. Holders of first-class tickets might
be ordered to occupy horse-boxes, and wear
dog-chains; also the Commissioners’ cars
might be set aside for the use of undesirable
aliens, and any (die found travelling under a
seat without a ticket could be rewarded with
a purse of severeigns. In short, the Com-
monwealth Government, if it ivere mad,
could run the railways on madhouse lines —
till the country got its opportunity to ex-
press an opinion on the efforts. If, then,
Cohen believes that Australia is going to
hand over its business to its madmen, and
that its madmen will do such things, he will
be alarmed. For the present, however, his
technical, railway advisers evidently refuse
to believe any such nonsense. On the con-
trary, they assume that sane electors will
elect sane Parliaments, and sane Govern-
ments will appoint sane Judges. There-
fore, “the move,” they say, “would appear
to be a wise one.”
* * * *
And these advisers have very good reason,
based on actual experience, to conclude that
the Commonwealth will not work on mad-
house principles. For the past 10 years the
Commonwealth, though it has not had con-
trol over railway services within the States,
has had the fullest control over all traffic
between the States, inasmuch as Section 98
of the Constitution declares that “the powers
of Parliament to make laws with respect to
trade and commerce extend ... to railways,
the property of a State. Thus, though the
Commonwealth Parliament possesses no
power over railway traffic between (say)
xielbournq and Wodonga, or Sydney and
Bathurst, it has had, ever since its inception,
the fullest power to regulate freights and
fares, and all conceivable travelling condi-
tions between (say) Melbourne and Sydney,
or Brisbane and Adelaide. It has never ex-
ercised, or attempted to exercise, that power ;
and it never will. As Lowell, Low,
Bagehot, and the rest of the commentators
on the working of the British Constitution
have repeatedly insisted, Ministerial respon-
sibility in no wise signifies Departmental
management.
* * * *
W. E. Gladstone spoke a truth, which
is extremely pertinent in these days of ex-
cited Tory talk about Laborites driving law-
abiding folks to revolution and bloodshed,
when he said that “all Constitutional Gov-
ernment is founded on compromise and com-
mon-sense.” The average Australian Minis-
ter has at least , the average amount of com-
mon-sense ; wherefore the nation may rely on
its Fishers leaving the management of
railway details —such as equipment, regula-
tion of freights and fares, train services, and
so forth —in the hands of the Railway Com-
missioners. These are matters with which
the Commonwealth is as unlikely to inter-
fere as it is to crowd into the Army Bar-
racks with the object of revolutionising the
drill system. But the question of the
hours of pay of the railway staff stands on
a different footing. There are few, even in
Mitchell’s party of false-alarm Liberals,
who question the desirability of granting
railway employees a right of appeal to the
Federal Arbitration Court—the Supreme
Court of Appeal in the land. The great ma-
jority of them confess that the-" are not
alarmed about that. They know, moreover,
that it is no use to even profess to be
alarmed, inasmuch as that would amount to
a ilseless reflection on the High Court —and
that cry, besides being dangerous, would cut
no ice. So they fall back on the fatuous
assumption that the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment and Government will do mad things
which they know it never will do, and hasn’t
the least intention of attempting.
So Cohen, rattling his money-boxes 12,000
miles away, will— fortunately or unfortun-
ately—continue to be at home to Australian
customers at the old address. The bogey-
talk of Mitchell, K.C., won’t affect him in
the least. His argument will be quite sound
and quite simple—“lf I have to assume that
the people will elect their madmen to do
their business, I should also have to assume
that they would elect madmen to manage
their banks and other companies; and I
haven’t seen any particular disposition on
the part of people anywhere to do any such
thing.”
SOME PLAIN SPEECH ON A GHASTLY
SUBJECT.

the tongue that Shakspeare
fa o i d . maide nly reluctance to face
1 physiological facts. The
which was said to have driv en
chronic chapel . goers in the United States to
dothe the legs of their pianoSj impelled Brit-
ish critics to damn “Ghosts,” the British cen-
sor to put his veto on “Mrs. Warren’s Pro-
fession,” and Mudie’s and the rest of the li-
braries to ban “Cottage Pie.” The West
End of London provides a standing text for
visiting sociologists and others, as well as a
permanent menace to the youth of the na-
tion, not because the Englishman likes the
main streets and chief music halls of his
capital city to hold the world’s record as
havens for every class and color of Women
who have Done, but because he prefers even
that to the unpleasantness of legislating on
a question which, he considers, should not
be mentioned in respectable society. At
the time of the Dargai Campaign, an en-
tire British regiment was debarred from go-
ing to the front owing to disease. It was
so ill that it couldn’t march, much less
fight. To-day, despite the devoted efEorts
or regimental and medical officers, between
30 and 40 per cent, of the rank and file in
India are diseased; and the figures for the
Home battalions are little better. It is an-
other manifestation of the English-speaking
person’s insane habit of treating, as though
they did not exist, horrors which shriek for
close attention.
* * * *
Here, in Australia, assuming that Japan
lies obligingly low till our army of small
boys grows up, and that nothing happens to
Britain’s sea-power before the Common-
wealth’s belated navy develops into a factor
to be reckoned with, we shall in time achieve
a considerable body of defenders. Even if
there were a prospect of this country hav-
ing soldiers and sailors to spare, it would be
a blunder and a crime to waste them. But
there is no such thing in prospect. One of
the things Australia is not going to be
threatened with for a number of generations
io come is a surplusage of warriors. Demon-
strably it behoves the Commonwealth to pro-
vide that its defenders shall, amongst other
things, be in the best possible physical con-
dition when they grow old enough to be
effective. How does Australia stand in this
regard at present? The answer is that,
despite the various Pure Foods Acts, Boards
of Health, and so forth, in the matter of
combating that disease which Fournier has
classed wdth tuberculosis and alcoholism as
‘‘the most dreadful of modern plagues,” prac-
tically nothing has been either done or at-
tempted—in most States, nothing whatever.
It is not that medical men have failed
to din into the ears of all and sundry the
urgency of getting an immediate strangle-
hold on the evil; nor that legislators fail to
appreciate the tiling for the blasting curse
it is. It runs riot unchecked, with never a
word of protest from a public platform, or
in the columns of the daily press, because,
with their British blood, Australians have
inherited a full measure of British snuffle
and false shame. In addition to Siiak-
speare’s tongue, and Milton’s faith and
morals, a big class in this country possesses
the social outlook of the calamitous British
folk who opposed the C. D. Act towards the
end of last century, on the preposterous
grounds that “the Law should not operate
to encourage sin, or protect sinners.”
* # * *
Just how deeply Australia is scored with
one of the grimmest diseases known to man-
kind, it is impossible to say. In the nature
of things there are no reliable figures avail-
able. A line may possibly be got from
Maoriland, where, in the words of Dr. Find-
lay, Minister for Justice in the Dominion: —
According to the best calculations that can be made
on the statistics and other figures obtainable, 60 per
cent, of all males contract sexual disorders before
they reach 30; while from 65 per cent, to 80 per
cent, of the abdominal and pelvic operations on
women are due to the infection that ensues.
Australia may not be in such a bad way as
Maoriland ; on the other hand, it may be in a
considerably worse way. In the Universi-
ties of Germany, where sexual troubles have
been handled with the gloves off, it is on
record that 25 per cent, of the students be-
come infected every term; and according to
Havelock Ellis, in the German Army—
where supervision is particularly careful —
“the yearly number of men invalided equals
a third of the total number wounded in the
Franco-Prussian War.” Let it be again
insisted that the trouble by which a vast
number of these men are laid low is no
comparative triviality like small-pox or ty-
phoid fever. It bears a fairly close analogy
to leprosy. A great authority has said that
without it there would be no nervous diseases
in the world. It is certainly responsible for
95 per cent, of cases of general paralysis
and locomotor ataxia. A big proportion of
cases of brain-softening and dementia,
and innumerable skin, nose and eye com-
plaints are due to it. In its final manifesta-
tions it is indescribable. The highest order
of Right Thinking Person may contract it.
It may be inherited. A son may unwit-
tingly transmit it to his mother by kissing
her on the cheek. Doctors and nurses stand
in daily peril of it. It may lurk on the rim
of any glass or cup in any hotel or tea-
room ; bn any towel or fork or roll of bread
or bunch of grapes or tram or pawn ticket.
And each person who is infected becomes
immediately a distributing-focus for the
ruin of innumerable others. In Manchuria,
at the present moment, Japan is pouring
out money like water, with the object of
suppressing pneumonic plague. Sydney did
the same, a decade ago, in order to banish
bubonic plague. All dver the Common-
wealth hundreds of thousands sterling are
expended yearly in the fight with the White
Death. Yet against a disease which is
more loathsome than plague, and perhaps as
disastrous, nationally speaking, as tubercu-
losis itself, not a finger is being raised.
Australia, with an army and navy in the
making, is inert in the presence of that
which has for centuries done deadly damage
to armies. And for no more rational cause
than that which precludes the really genteel
Yankee from mentioning the word “stomach”
in the hearing of his lady friends.
* * * *
For years reformers were discouraged in
their efforts to check the most appalling
variety of venereal disease by the hopeless
knowledge that there were no means of
swiftly dealing with it once it had been con-
tracted, and consequently no possibility of
preventing it from spreading. The best of
evidence is available that that state of af-
fairs is ended. In the Australasian
Medical Gazette (20/3/’U), the Hon.
Pathologist to the Sydney Hospital, and the
Medical Superintendent of the N.S.W. Coast
Hospital state the effects of salvarsan, or
“696,” on the 19 Coast Hospital cases
treated by this drug since its arrival from
Germany "a few months ago. If it produced
no other result than that of rendering the
patient powerless to infect others, after four
days’ application ,(and that it has this
power the scientists have definitely proved),
it would stand justified as one of humanity’s
priceless discoveries. To narrow a diseased
person’s potentialities for harm from two
years down to four days, means decreasing
the public’s chances of acquiring this mis-
fortune to an incalculable extent. But more
than this is proved. Two of the least re-
volting cases that are cited in the Gazette
will do:-
Case 4. —Before injection: Multiple ulcers of
tongue, ulcer at angle of mouth and of lower lip,
extensive ulceration of both tonsils and of posterior
pharyngeal wall, causing much pain in swallowing.
In seven days ulcers of tongue, of angle of mouth
and of lower lip are healed; ulcers of throat are
healing, and he can now swallow food in comfort;
weight has increased from 1341 b. to 1381 b.
Case s.—Before injection: Ulcer at each angle of
mouth and numerous ulcers on tongue; much sali-
vation, causing difficulty in speaking. In seven davs
ulcers of tongue quite, and of mouth almost, healed ;
salivation has ceased; weight has increased from
1191 b. to 1221 b.
In short, a means has been found of check-
ing, if not of absolutely scotching, a dis-
ease which, one way and another, kills more
people in a week than cancer does in a year.
What is Australia —the country with the
infant army, the embryo navy, the birth-rate
problem, and the unsatisfactory immigration
tally—going to do about it?
* * * «
The numerous experiments that were tried
during the past century to mitigate what
has always been recognised as a national
menace—the Stale regulation of the “social
evil,” and so on —all failed, more or less.
They were bound to, in the nature of things,
because none of them got near the heart of
the trouble. It is certain that the medical
men of Australia, with Professor Ehrlich’s
new discovery at their command, can show
results as satisfactory in the grosser form
of venereal disease as any that have been
shown in diphtheria or plague. All they
require is a fair sporting chance. A first
essentiul to success is the formation in
every State of Lock Hospitals, or of Lock
wings attached to existing hospitals. All
sufferers should be legally compelled to notify
themselves, and to undergo proper treat-
ment and supervision. A howl of protest is
bound to arise when such a scheme is under-
stood. The Wowser will inveigh against it
as a plot to render sin safe, and the Sinner
damn it as a gross assault on the liberty of
the subject. But the vast majority of the
unprejudiced must recognise that it is but
a corollary of legislation which has done
magnificent work for years past. Victims
of leprosy, small-pox and bubonic plague are
already compulsorily segregated—as one re-
sult of which these once almost-universal
complaints are practically unknown among
us. Compulsory notification exists in re-
gard to diphtheria, scarlet fever, typhoid
fever, typhus, etc.; and the principle has
ceased to arouse protest in their regard.
Side by side with the proposed reform would,
of course, go the quarantining of venereal
subjects from oversea. The work would
be painful and costly, but the reward \vould
be worth it. It would mean the acquisition
by the Commonwealth of the only physically-
sound army and navy that the world has
had for four centuries. Australia would
become known, not only as the only White
Continent, but as the only Clean Continent.
It is an ideal worth striving for.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The State Frights Railway Swindle.
Railway Commissioner Johnson,
-while under examination by the Decentral-
isation Commission recently, gave away the
whole case for the cont rol of the railways
by the States. The business under the Corn-
mission’s eye at the moment was the River-
ina traffic and the vast, unnecessary haul-
age to and from Sydney. Said Johnson :
The SU gg es tion to grant rebates on railway rates
f- 0 induce shipment from ports other than Sydney,
would be absolutely at variance with established
railway principles. Under existing conditions it
would be absolutely illegal.
Having thus laid it down that it would be
sinful to give a preference to any N.S.W.
port except Sydney, he went on to say that,
of course, so far, as Riverina is concerned,
Victorian ports would be much nearer. But
the idea of encouraging the traffic to go to
the nearest port was not to be thought of!
So N. S. Wales cut in with special rebates,
He quoted freights as follows: —
Finley to Toeumwal. — 163 miles to Melbourne,
466 miles to Sydney; wheat, per ton, 13s. 6d. to
Melbourne, 11s. lOd. to Sydney..
Thus, in order to prevent Finley wheat go-
ing the 163 miles to Melbourne, a rate is fixed
at which it is dragged the whole 455 miles
to Sydney for _ Is. Bd. per ton less than it
would pay if it went the short way. In
other words, assuming that 13s. 6d. per ton is
a fair rate for the 163 miles, then the
N.S.W. taxpayer drags the wheat 310 miles
for nothing, in order that Sydney shall
have the handling of it, and the pecking
at it, instead of Melbourne. You can t
drag wheat, or anything else, 310 miles for
nothing; and, as the wheat-farmer doesn t
pay, the money has to come out of general
revenue. Or the suburban or other railway
users have to pay their own fares and
freights, and, in addition, something to
make good the loss on the haulage of Some-
body else’s wheat a wholly unnecessary 300
miles. _ Nor does the farmer benefit at,
all. His wheat is none the better for the
unnecessary haulage; and unnecessary haul-
age is all he gets out of the deal, for the
Is. Bd. per ton is too small to count. In
fact, if there were none of this shocking
waste—if there were none of this unneces-
sary haulage—the railways could afford to
carry his goods at a cheaper rate to the
nearest market. The Victorian railways,
on account of the competition of the jS.S.H.
lines, don’t have the traffic they should:
consequently the Finley man has to pay
more for freight to his nearest port than
he should. Also, the Victorian railways,
like those of N. S. Wales, struggle to
make traffic flow out of its natural chan-
nel; and that means waste, and waste
means that the Finley man pays more
than is necessary, even to reach his nearest
port. And what applies to the Finley man
applies to 10,000 others. The whole busi-
ness is artificial; and it never will be any-
thing else so long as there are rival State
railway systems, one _ competing for the
trade which geographically belongs, and.
therefore, ought to go, to the other, and all
struggling to feed their bloated capitals.


Song of the People.
[Fob The Bulletin.]
We are the People Omniscient! At word of
our might
Mountains fall riven to dust;
Or the shuddering night,
Scared by our power into horror o gasp
ing affright,
Bursts into rivers of blood, while ppies
sion and Lust ,
Flee to their burrows in fear, and w i 1
crawling disgust
Curse us and die!
We build our gallows on hilltop and p am,
aud near by
Tyrants crouch watching our beacons t a
blaze up on high,
Jewelling minster and steeple!
Terrible, beautiful, clear is the truth that
we cry —
We are the People!
We are the people All-Loving! We reckon
aright
Gibes of the insolent rich and the piteous
cry
Wrung from the breasts of poor babes as
they hunger and die!
We scorn the gods that they trust-
Pander, and dotard, and vampire, and
minion, and wanton, bedight
Still with the gems that we dig for and
bleed for; and bright,
Bright as the flame of our scorn, is the hope
we espy:
Soon all the barbarous wrongs that now
fester and creep ’ll
Rot, and as carrion lie!
We are the Lords of the Time. Kings may
squirm, but they must
Bend as whipped thralls to the gods we
have made by-and-bye —
We are the People!
We are the People Uniting! We fling to
the sky
Challenges fierce as we waken and spring
from the dust!
We toss aside pale “ You may" for terrific
“You must” :
Splendid and piercing and strong is our
ultimate cry !
Soon the starved helots that suffer and
struggle and weep ’ll
Spring fully-armed to the glorious banner
we fly !
Out on you, tyrannous horde! For the
battle you light
Ends in damnation for you now our torch
is alight!
Out and to Hell with you, prelate and
courtier and spy,
Fools that have trusted in fools and have
built on a lie!
We are the People!
We are the People Unsparing! Your doom
is a-nigh— , ,
Law-cats and snufflers and pilferers greedy
and sly!
We shall live fatly in peace while you
munch on a crust
Flung by our pity, that you shall have
leisure to die!
Red glooms the sun of our rising, our man-
date is thrust
Into your womanish hands that so tremble
with fright! .
Back to your owner and master, vile owls
of the night!
Torments you’ve made at your livers shall
batten and bite;
Pits you have digged for our feet, so grim-
yawning and deep, ’ll
Serve to hide your flaccid clay from our
noses and sight!
We are the People!
MAORILAND. FRANK MORTON.
The Brotherhood of Fat.
When you get right into the heart of the
present Tory uproar against the Referenda,
you find that it is almost wholly due to
the fierce rage of the Fatman at the pros-
pect of losing some of his profits. The old
Scripture-writers, when they wanted a simile
for desperate wrath, used to refer to a
lioness robbed of her cubs. If they wrote
to-day, they would picture a Fatman in dan-
ger of his profits. It is the same the world
over. One issue of the morning papers
recently had these two items:
Calcutta. —With the support of all the provin-
cial governments, the Viceroy’s Council yesterday
passed a Factory Act, establishing a day of 12 hours
for all adults, and six hours for children. . . It was
passed in the face of continuous opposition from
the Bombay cotton and the Calcutta jute firms.
London. —In the House of Commons yesterday
the Prime Minister announced that a Licensing
Bill, on the lines of the 1908 Bill, was
urgently needed. The Bill would be introduced
immediately the Parliament Veto Bill was passed.
I The 1908 Bill was passed in the House ot Com-
mons by a. majority of 23/, and was rejected by
the House of Lords by a vote of 272 to 96.1
So necessary was the Indian Factory Act
that “all the provincial governments sup-
ported it. Fat, however, continuously op-
posed it. The House of Lords’ action over
the Licensing Bill was just about as bad.
The Bill provided some splendid reforms;
but, as it happened to interfere with vested
interests, it went out on its head. In Aus-
tralia, the last stronghold of Fat is the
Legislative Council, especially where, as in
Victoria, it is elected on a restricted property
franchise. If the Referenda are passed,
Fat will be shorn of much of his remaining
power, so that he is fighting with his back
to the wall. It is all very intelligible; and
it is all very ignoble. But the sorriest spec-
tacle is the support which Fat is getting
from alleged Democrats. It cannot be t<po
strongly insisted on that “State Rights, ih
this contest, means really “Fat’s Privileges
—the privilege to block humane legislation,
the right to continue sweating, and the li-
cense to stop the progress of civilisation gen-
erally. If the electors grasp this true in-
wardness of the situation, the battle is won.
The Australian National Spirit.
One day lately the Sydney Telegraph de-
plored the cult of Australianism. It com-
plained that there was no need for any pro-
paganda of the sort. As to people banding
together to foster an Australian spirit, it
was “as purposeless as a French natives’
association in France.” The analogy is, un-
fortunately, false, and the conclusion, there-
fore, rotten ; and there is plenty of evidence
to prove it so. A week or so back the Com-
monwealth destroyers—which, largely owing
to Australia’s old-time craze for foreign im-
ports, had to be built abroad— developed defects
in their steering-gear. No one questioned
that the blame rested with the British au-
thorities, who had supervised the building ot
the vessels, tested them, and given them a
clean bill of health. Anyhow,, and in what-
ever light the occurrence was viewed, it didn t
amount to an occasion for rejoicing or merri-
ment. To persons who are threatened with
any imagination at all, there must be some-
thing inspiring about these small ships, the
lonely forerunners of the mighty fleet that is
to be —provided abways Australians aren t.
the crassest incompetents and the worst
parasites that the world has known. Yet,
Sydney Evening News, which gets its com-
fortable living out of this country, voicing
quite a large body of “opinion,” greeted the
mishap with the delighted guffaw of the
small boy who sees his hated enemy flung
off a tram. The dreadful publication hailed
the fact that Britain had supplied the Com-
monwealth with some defective steering-
gear as a humorous proof of its an-
cient contention that Australia shouldn't
be trusted with a navy of its own. A car-
toon depicted a crowd of low-comedy mar-
iners steering, with a wooden oar, the sort
of craft which J. Cook, M.P., when “Lib-
eral” Minister of Defence, imagined a
“River” destroyer was. Had the half-nigger
Republic of Chili —a country vastly inferior
to Australia in wealth and population —suf-
fered a mishap to one of its war-ships, Aus-
tralian “loyalists” wouldn’t have been di-
verted. The intimation that Peru —whose
public revenue is about 24 millions, as
against Australia’s 144 millions —had found
defects in one of the formidable cruisers
which it owns, would not have wrung gibes
from members of the British Empire League
of Australia. It is only when an Australian-
owned war-vessel goes wrong that the as-
tounding “loyalists,” whose views are faith-
fully expressed by papers like the News, are
moved to behave as though they were in the
presence of a Lion Comique. Which goes
to show that patriotism does need to be
fostered in Australia. In Prance there is
no necessity for anything of the sort. But,
then, in France, aW. E. Johnson —the type
of politician who accuses his native land of
wanting a navy in order to destroy its chief
benefactor —would be regarded as a particu-
larly libellous traitor: and the editor of a
paper which habitually belittled the nation’s
fleet would stand a fair chance of decorating
a lamp-post.

Some things That Were Never Contemplated.
Ma’s Minister for Education Beeby has
now definitely signed on to join the forces of
Fat, in the attempt to hamstring the nation
as it passes through the gateway of the Re-
ferenda. Making excuses for himself, lie
uses that old familiar wheeze, “It was never
contemplated by the framers of the Constitu-
tion, or by the people when they accepted it,
that,” etc. Probably the . most concise an-
swer to this undemocratic argument was
given by that sound Constitutionalist, Jus-
tice (then Mr.) Higgins, many years ago,
when the smiths of the Federal Constitution
were busy riveting the hobble-chains. He
put it thus :
I contend that we have no right to dictate to the
future Australian people what shall be the machinery
for dealing with those subjects which are admittedly
Australian, or to interfere with the control of all-
Australian questions by a majority of the people of
Australia. To judge from some advocates, one would
think that in the convention of 1897-8 the duty of
members was to guide and restrain the people for
centuries to come as to their own concerns—as the
Biglow Papers put it:
That wat we hed met fur was just to agree
Wat the people’s opinions in futur’ should be.
Beeby agrees that all powers should primar-
ily be vested in the National Parliament, and
tliat the limits placed on the power to amend
the Constitution must be. removed, “in. order
that expression can be given at any . time to
the will of the majority of the citizens of
the Commonwealth” ; which is practically an
elaborate overture to the very tune the Re-
ferenda propose to play without an overture.
The peculiar complaint of Beeby is that these
Referenda proposals are a short cut to unifi-
cation ; yet, if he is not misreported, he holds
that unification is the proper form of govern-
ment for the Commonwealth. He sets out
his conception of that “proper form of gov-
ernment” this way:—
All powers should primarily be vested in the
National Parliament. The re-subdivision of the Con-
tinent into States should follow, and then the
■National Parliament should declare what functions
it intends to exercise, leaving the residue in the
hands of the States, with the necessary simplification
of the forms of State Government. So long as un-
trammelled expression can at any time be given to
the wishes of the majority of the people of the Com-
monwealth, I would not hesitate to heartily endorse
this re-organisation of governmental powers.
So that, despite the open-armed welcome that
Fat’s newspapers have given to Beeby, as
another prominent deserter from the people’s
cause, even the Sydney Telegraph has to
admit that he is “a unificationist on a demo-
cratic basis.” But, whatever particular spe-
cies of unificationist that may be, Beeby’s
present position is clear and plain—and sus-
picious. He is standing high in the light,
pointing with one hand to the national des-
tination, and with the other to the longest
and most perilous way round. He is no
comfort to either party. To Fat he says,
in effect: “I’ll fight with you, so that I can
get a stronger grip on your throat —some
day.” To Labor he says: “I don’t believe
in half a loaf, so you can starve, as far as
I’m concerned.” The point he overlooks is
the reply both parties tvill probably make to
him. It is fairly safe to say that the reply
from one, at least, will be such as Beeby
“never contemplated” vffien he first got a
foothold on the shoulders of the Labor giant.
The Prophet Who Doesn’t Deserve Any
Honor.
Not many months back the columns of the
Sydney Daily Telegraph resounded with
a riot of epithets, warnings and outcries
against the Labor candidates for seats in the
Ma Parliament, while the mere suggestion
that Wade should be passed out, to make
room for a “Caucus Ministry,” was sufficient
to set all King-street trembling with rage
and indignation. Nothing seemed clearer
than that a Labor victory would.be the fore-
runner of general, wide-spread disaster, mal-
administration, class oppression, incompet-
ence, confiscation, and all. the ills, that could
arise from diseased political tissue. But
what happened? The Wade-Would-T waddle
party was emptied into The rubbish-bin, and
the Revolutionaries took office. Five
months have passed since then, and the
Telegraph has not published the where-
abouts of one marriage tie busted, nor of a
solitary bank harassed into bankruptcy.
Instead, the paper trots out, every now and
then, dopes of adulation of one Minister or
the other. Now, it is the “business-like”
Assistant-Treasurer Carmichael who gets
the glad pen, for “playing the game of poli-
tics as it should be played by conscientious
men”; next, it is Flowers’s arguments
which “are backed by common-sense” ; next,
Chief Secretary Donald Macdonell is
lime-lighted as one who “must be commended
for moving in the right direction”; then
Minister for Works Griffith is boomed
through the paper on his northern tour, in
a way to make poor old “Solemn” Lee’s
mouth water, and is saluted from the leading
article in this way : —
The frank impartiality and independence with
which the Minister for Works administers his Depart-
ment promises to be the most reassuring feature of
Labor rule to those who on one score and another
have their doubts about it.
After so many frank admissions of its
rank failure as a public prophet, it is no
surprise to find the D. T. reversing the view
it took of IIolman when he was opposed to
the calamitous Coercion Act, and describing
him, the new Attorney-General, as a man
“who is picked out by reason of his conspic-
uous fitness to legally advise the State.”
Altogether, the Telegraph proves itself the
same old incompetent adviser, bawling con-
demnation before the evidence is presented,
and immediately afterwards cheering in a
loud voice to cover its confusion, and drown
the echo of its ridiculous hoots.

Fizz!
The Champagne Riots in France appear to
have raised the question whether one district
can or should stop other districts from apply-
ing its name to the goods they produce. Firstly,
the small vineyardists and laud owners of
the original Champagne country—also pre-
sumably the oldest and most conservative
firms— for a Bill to prevent unscrupu-
lous manufacturers from importing wine
from outlying districts and trading it off on
the world as genuine champagne. While the
Bill was dawdling along, the malcontents
seized a few thousand vats of the spurious
liquor and emptied the contents into a river.
Thus they scared the Government into passing
a measure that gave to the original Cham-
pagne district the sole right to the title; but
the big exporters were not forbidden to deal
with other sparkling wine, provided they
shipped it as something else. The limita-
tion of the word “champagne” to the Cham-
pagne country has, however, produced riots
in other districts. The departments of
Aube and Upper Marnay are in a state of
ferment —vineyard workers are burning the
Premier in effigy, and wanting to know what
will become of them if the wine of their dis-
tricts is deprived of its bogus title and ren-
dered almost useless for exportation to for-
eign countries, “Champagne” and “Moselle”
being the only two French light sparkling
wines that count in foreign markets. So
the French Senate is up against the fact
that, in trying to content the genuine cham-
pagne grape-growers, it is threatening to
destroy a large French trade, which prospers
under false pretences. Hence, the second
series of Champagne Riots is pretty certain
to result in the amendment of the Govern-
ment’s virtuous bill. From this recent re-
velation of the truth, or some of the truth,
about the frauds of the French wine trade,
Australia may at least gain a fresh incen-
tive to the manufacture of its own sparkling
wines, which are much better entitled to be
called “champagne” than the vast bulk of
the stuff that comes here from Europe.
Nothing seems to have been done by the
State Governments to create a solid public
respect for the choicest native brands, and
to counteract the antagonistic influence of
the Stinking Fish party —which claims to be
the only party with an educated taste in
liquor. After the Referendum the Federal
Parliament will be in a position to do some-
thing worth doing. Already it ought to be
considering the advisability of a Bill to safe-
guard fools against the temptation of buy-
ing bogus and probably harmful French
champagne at preposterously high prices—
the price for the name being the charm of
the temptation. The Bill passed by the
French Senate would prove a safeguard if it
were put into operation, but the odds are
that it never will be. The duty of stopping
mock champagne from entering the Com-
monwealth is therefore pretty certain to be
left to the Customs.

State Rights and Private Wrongs.
From a well-known Sydney investor:
As, nearly every newspaper in New South .Wales
is barracking for “No” on the Referenda, on Apiil
26, and a number of them refuse to publish any-
thing that may prejudice what they term State
Rights,” the time is opportune to draw public at-
tention to some of the laws passed by the State
Parliament in the interests of a certain class. 1
will refer to one only, the Companies Act, but there
are a number of others quite as bad, and they were
all placed on the Statute Book by the so-called
Liberal party. _ ,, T * +
In 1892 the Dibbs Government passed the Joint
Stock Companies Arrangement Act (Dibbs’s Recon-
struction Act). That Act enabled the wealthy
shareholders in banks and other financial institu-
tions to plunder their N. S. Wales creditors unmerci-
fully. I know a wealthy institution, also a wealthy
family of millionaires, who both held a large number
of shares in a pastoral company that took advantage
of that Act several times. The former got rid of a
liability of about £60,000, while the latter got
rid of a liability of about £190,000.
This Act, after being renewed and extended sev-
eral times, finally lapsed in 1899. In 1906, at the
instance of certain financial institutions, the then
Liberal Government passed an Amended Companies
Act. That Act practically resuscitated and per-
petuated Dibbs’s Reconstruction A,ct. There was
serious opposition to the bill, and, in order to allay
it. provisions were inserted,, compelling all foreign
companies trading in N. S. Wales to register a list
of debenture holders and shareholders in Sydney,
the object being to enable the creditors of these
reconstructed institutions to combine for the pro-
tection of their interests, as previously these con-
cerns had almost invariably refused to supply a
list of their creditors when they contemplated a
fresh reconstruction. A large number of companies
complied with the law, and registered lists of de-
benture holders and shareholders with the Registrar-
General ; but a number failed to do so, and included
in the latter were those companies which had re-
constructed or compromised, and some of which had
defrauded their N. S. Wales creditors. Instead of
compelling those companies to comply with the law,
the Wade Government hurriedly passed an amended
bill through Parliament (it was passed through the
Lower House at one sitting), entitled the Companies
(Amendment) Act, 1907. This Act repealed the
provisions in the Act of 1906, compelling these
companies to register in Sydney a list of debenture
holders, and practically of shareholders also, thereby
conclusively showing that the interests of N. S. Wales
citizens were of minor importance to the interests
of the wealthy shareholders of these reconstructed
concerns.
It is significant that for some time past a large num-
ber of companies, some registered elsewhere, have been
opening brant ' cs in Sydney. As the law at present
stands in N. S. Wales (thanks to the so-called Lib-
eral Government), when the inevitable reaction ar-
rives, the crisis of 1893 will be repeated—probablv
with intensified results —and the citizens of N. S.
Wales will again have to submit to the plundering
process unless the Commonwealth Parliament is
given power to prevent it.

An Instance of How an Ass is Made
Doubly an Ass.
The protest of “Common Law Side” :■ —-
A suit tried in Sydney last week exemplified the
way the Law in regard to breach of promise is
misused. The plaintiff was an ex-liotel employee
who owned to 30, who had, as she put it, “lost all
she had ever loved,” and whom the Chief Justice
described as a user of “coarse language” and “a
woman of no great refinement.” The charmer, whose
fickle ways had, she claimed, damaged her to the
extent of a thousand pounds sterling, was a 47-year-
old up-country clerk. In her evidence-in-cliief the
lady swore that defendant had assailed her virtue,
but had been beaten off. The clerk left this aspect
of tile trouble alone till he was tackled about it in
cross-examination; then he declared that there had
been persistent impropriety. The lady went into
the box and lodged what the papers next day termed
“an indignant denial.” And “the jury, after a brief
retirement, found in favor of the plaintiff, and
assesses damages at £5.” If the good and true
lieges who pronounced upon this case believed that
the defendant had promised to marry the plaintiff
and had then gone back on his pledge, thus causing
her to lose her position, break her heart, and feel as
though a death had taken place—all of which catas-
trophes she claimed had smitten her owing to his de-
fection —a solatium of five sovereigns was grotesquely
inadequate. Five hundred would hardly have met
the situation. If, furthermore, the jurymen believed
that she was a virtuous woman, and that the de-
fendant when he swore to the contrary was lying,
a five-pound note would no more recompense her for
the intolerable wrong she had suffered than a sick
headache. As to her slanderer, in that case the con-
fiscation of his entire real and personal estate, fol-
lowed by a thorough public boiling, would scarcely
avail to purge his guilt. Either, then, the jurors
who considered this case were stark mad, or
they paid no particular attention to the evi-
dence of either of the romanticists. The factor
which chiefly weighed with them was, evi-
dently the consideration that the defendant
had played the part (to borrow barrister Broom-
field's phrase), of “a sort of wayback Lothario.”
In other words, they fined him £5 and costs, for a
lapse of which the Law takes no cognisance. In so
doing they were not acting differently from a thou-
sand other juries. The custom is quite familiar;
wherefore the Supreme Court is habitually used as
an engine for punishing citizens for being Lotharios,
and the breach-of-promise law is turned into a
species of matrimonial agency, to force reluctant
gentlemen into the arras of ladies who have “lost
all.” Some day a body of sensible legislators will
provide that unless a philandering female has been
seduced under a promise of marriage, or has been
strung on, year after year, and has thereby lost other
chances of desirable marriage, she shall have no
cause for a breach-of-promise action against any
philandering male. Till that is the lav/, absurd suits
like the one just discussed will continue to arouse
the gratification of Costs and the derision of the
public.
The loss of the inter-State liner Yongala,
with something like 120 lives on board, will
remind a good many thousand people, on
April 26, that, though they are Victorians,
or South Australians, or Sydneysiders, they
are vitally interested in what happens on
the Queensland coast. When one of these
inter-State carriers goes down it is an Aus-
tralian disaster —not merely a Queensland
disaster, or a South Australian catastrophe
—and the laws that govern the traffic should
be Australian, too. Queensland may be
doing everything that it ought to do to safe-
guard life on its coast, and to see that the
dependents of the seamen who are drowned
get fair insurance money; but, in the light
of a disaster such as this, it is easy to see
that the matter should not be left wholly
to Queensland. The other Australians who
are vitally interested should have a say in
the matter as well. That means, of course,
the extension of the powers of the Common-
wealth —such an extension as the people will
be asked to sanction on April 26.
At the annual meeting of Australian Cham-
bers of Manufactures, held in Tasmania,
Ludowici, of Sydney, backed by Pratten,
of the same swollen village, moved for the
encouragement of closer bonds, commercial
and national, between Australia and Amer-
ica. But the parochial majority fought shy
of “embarking on the turbulent sea of inter-
national politics,” as one or two delegates
put it, and Ludowici moved in vain. Yet,
as the A.N.A. is silent on this matter, and
as the Commonwealth Government is ab-
sorbed between the Coronation and the fight
with the Insects, the Associated Manufac-
turers might have made a name for them-
selves by taking the lead in promoting an
entente which all far-sighted Australians
have in mind, and which all Australia will
be discussing to-morrow or the day after.
The Federal Progressive Land Tax is getting
in first-class work in Victoria. In the
Western District, “Salmon” Wilson left
his children a cluster of big stations, includ-
ing Ercildoune, Mount Bute, and Coranga-
mite. Ercildoune, 20 miles west of Bal-
larat, is reckoned the finest estate and home-
stead in Australia. It is the show place for
visitors to Ballarat, and has been tenanted
by Governors. One has it just now. Yet
the Wilsons have always lived in England,
only returning once every three years to
fulfil a residence stipulation in their father’s
will. (Old Wilson was a good enough
Australian to say in his will that his chil-
dren should not be away from Australia
more than three years at a time.) So the
three fine estates were just worked for
profit, and the profit was religiously
’Ome. Now, however, the Federal Land
Tax, with its absentee provision, has fallen
with a heavy thud on these places, and they
are all in the market. The whole of Cor-
angamite (near Colac) has been subdivided,
and a lot of it is already sold. Mt. Bute
(near Ballarat) and a big slice of Ercil-
doune, are going the same way. The cut-
ting up of these big estates, (over 100,000
acres altogether) is a splendid thing for the
State. Until recently, the Wilson and
Brydges estates made the stretch of country
between Ballarat and Beaufort a wilder-
ness. For mile upon mile you saw nothing
but sheep, and an odd boundary-rider. Now,
there is going to be a whole army of farmers,
for the land is of the best. And Ballarat,
which needs it badly, will be a great gainer

Digby Denham, Premier of Queensland,
has supplied 11 reasons why the electors
should vote “No” on April 26. Each of the
11 is as bad as the others. The culminat-
ing “reason” is this—“ The proposals simply
mean that the Commonwealth assumes com-
plete control of all State borrowing.” If
that is so, then the State has already as-
sumed complete control of all private bor-
rowing. Denham’s idea is that, since the
Commonwealth Court may fix wages and
conditions of employment, the Common-
wealth will really decide whether money
may be raised or not. Already, however,
the State Courts and Wages Boards fix
wages and conditions of labor in private
employ; so that, according to Denham, the
State has assumed “complete control” of
private borrowing. Yet if Digby Denham
were told that Denham Brothers, the
buttermen, couldn’t get an overdraft with-
out asking the State, Digby Denham
would certainly reply that his informant
was a liar. This paper is much too polite
to carry the argument any further.

Thus Melbourne Argus, snorting at the
thought of anti-combine legislation:—
Nor can any combination in the world have more
than a temporary influence on prices of the great
staples. The world’s gold supply is one great factor;
and behind all there is the inexorable law of supply
and demand, by which the price of bread and the
price of diamonds are equally determined. Not in-
trinsic usefulness, but desire to have things, and the
supply of the things that are desired, fix exchange
value, which is price.
What does the Argus mean by “temporary”?
The fish rings of certain countries have
lasted just about as long as anybody can re-
member. So also the middle-men of the
meat trade are always in combination more
or less to keep up the retail price and squeeze
illegitimate profits from the consumer. The
Yankee Meat Trust, if unchecked, would soon
be in a position to make its own law of sup-
ply, without any fear of losing its grip of de-
mand for a staple article of food. The de-
mand for bread likewise continues, utterly
regardless of price up to a certain limit; anil
a gigantic wheat combine could raise the
price of bread for all time with a stroke of
the pen. Workers must eat to live and
work; they can't reduce the prices of the
necessities of life by refraining from buying
them, whereas people can reduce the price of
unnecessaries like diamonds by spending no
money on them. When the Argus talks of
“the inexorable law of supply and demand,
by which the price of bread and the price of
diamonds are equally determined,” it is clos-
ing its hundred Tory eyes to the fact that
there are two sorts of law of demand, and
only one of them —the law of Demand to
Live —is inexorable.
Yested Interests—White-vested interests.
Brilliant piffle of Jokeruthers, dug up
from somewhere, to urge people to vote “ No” on
April 26 :
If a poor plumber wanted to mend a water-tap in Pad-
dington he would have to go to the Federal Government
for a license.
Well, even that is not nearly so bad as a poor
squatter having to part up thousands of his hard-
earned money to various agents before he could
get a State department to do a small land job.
The Six Hatters lie has come home to
roost. Owing to the scarcity of labor in Sydney,
a meeting of city manufacturers was held last
week to discuss matters, and one of the doleful
party told the meeting that recently, when he
was in England, he found it useless to attempt to
induce suitable factory workers to emigrate to
Australia, because they feared being clapped into
gaol “like the Six Hatters.” By-and-bye even
Fat will open his eyes to the fact that honesty
pays best —sometimes.
Attorney-General Hughes has stated that,
if the Referendum vote goes “ No,” the Naviga-
tion Bill will hardly be worth passing. Following
recent High Court decisions, it is evident that,
unless the Constitution is amended, the Federal
law will be inoperative in respect of any vessel
trading wholly within a State. A steamer trad-
ing from Brisbane to Richmond River would be
under Federal law; from Richmond River to
Sydney—a much longer run—she would be under
State law. It is clearly in the interests of the
large part of the population which is vitally in-
terested in the passage of an up-to-date Federal
Navigation Act to see that Australia votes “Yes”
on April 26.
Dick Sleath, one-time M.L.A. in the Ma
State, recovered £IOO damages last week from the
S. M. Herald for libel, and, strangely enough, it
was over his old colleague. Ferguson, with whom
he was associated in the Broken Hill strike trials
of ’92, that the trouble occurred. Last October
Sleath was speaking on behalf of the Labor candi-
date who was opposed at Newtown by Ferguson,
now a “ Liberal.” Someone interjected : “Fer-
guson’s a rat”; to which Sleath retorted: “I
won’t abuse Ferguson.” According to the Herald,
Sleath went on to say that Labor’s abuse of its
opponents would lose it the election. Sleath
denied having said anything of the sort, and com-
plained that the published statement had been
the cause of his losing lucrative employment in
Labor circles. The jury sympathised with him
to the extent of £IOO. That is the second time
the Herald has fallen in over Sleath. Last
time it was a £SO verdict.
From P. R. Allen, long in business in
Port Darwin :
A newcomer to the Territory gets some angry words
into The Bulletin of MarchS. “Local merch mts,” he
says, “buy the most inferior stuff imaginable,” and
“ goods condemned in the South are considered good
enough for the North.” There is not a particle of truth
in this, as I know my people (and I feel that I can say the
same for the co.’s European competitors) handle
only leading and best brands of groceries. The
wet season is a very trying time for storekeepers
in the Far North. Currants and raisins quickly
breed worms, and bacon will in three days’ transit to
country parts develop maggots. I have had to condemn
bacon time after time, and have had it sold for pigs’ food
or destroyed. Every care is taken to ensure only good
goods being sent out, and in the instance stated by the
angry correspondent, I think the supplies must have
been some time in transit. Darwin merchants are also
handicapped by the irregularity of the steamer service.
The Burns-Philp line is the only one whose boats run to
a regular time-table. The consequence is, three steamers
may arrive at Port Darwin in the same week from the
south, and then not another for from three to four weeks;
so storekeepers have to keep unnecessarily large stocks
on hand, which, as mentioned above, isn’t calculated to
improve them.
