“The Woman in the Case,” a drama by
Clyde Fitch, was produced at Sydney Cri-
terion on Saturday night. It is more har-
rowing than a Liberal forecast of the re-
sults of “Labor domination.” Why theatre-
goers should so love episodes which tear
their emotions to shreds it is hard to say.
Yet so it is. A dramatist who chose as his
theme the winning of a Tattersall’s sweep
by an indigent suburban dentist, who por-
trayed the exultation of the family and the
envy of their friends, and who finally showed
them happily packing for the Coronation,
would do no good for himself. It is in
people’s ghastliest experiences that the boom
lies. Bearing this in mind, the American
playwright has, in the work under review,
laid on the agony with. a trowel, and the
box-office returns of the past year show his
wisdom.
The plot hinges on a pre-marital flirtation
of Julian liolfe. In the days of his youth
Rolfe thought he loved a member of a musi-
cal-comedy sextette. It took him 10 days
to find that he didn’t. lie then backed out,
leaving some undated love-letters in his
wake. Instead of feeling grateful to the
lady for violating all the established conven-
tions of the game by not writtiug him for
breach of promise, he proceeded at the
earliest opportunity to blast her character in
the eyes of his friend, Philip Long. The
girl loved Long, and he loved the girl so
much that, having given Rolfe his word not
to marry her, he shot himself rather than
face life without her. Just as Hell is re-
ported to hold no fury like a woman scorned,
so earth holds none like a show-lady who
has been robbed of her lover by an officious
male chatterer. Claire Forster, despite the
calamitous shade of her hair and various
other unpleasant attributes, wins a certain
sympathy in her efforts to get Julian con-
victed of murdering Long. He was the in-
direct cause of that lamentable weakling’s
decease, anyhow. He is a congenital prig
and fool; and her failure to remove him
from a world which he does not adorn is in
no sense due to his own efforts. His irre-
sponsibility and incompetence in the trouble
which he has brought on himself are com-
plete and exasperating. Not till the situ-
ation is absolutely howled in his ear by a
lawyer does he realise that he is in any real
peril at all. And then he has no suggestion
whatever to offer. His salvation is due to
his wife, who traps the inebriated Claire
into an admission that Long committed sui-
cide.
* * * *
Margaret Rolfe (Miss Mabel Trevor) is
a queer mixture of hysteria, and cool resolu-
tion and courage. It is hard to credit that
the capable lady who masquerades suc-
cessfully as a demi-mondaine, and cheerfully
endures the company of Claire Forster’s ap-
palling “gentleman friend,” was liable to
such an uncontrollable outburst as overtook
her when her husband was arrested. That
being so, the onlooker conceives the irritat-
ing suspicion that his feelings are being
butchered to make an actress’s tour de force
of emotionalism. Miss Elinor Foster pro-
vides a likely picture of the dreadful Woman
in the Case. She might be more alluring;
but the fact that not one of those whom she
enslaves was a difficult mark goes to justify
her characterisation. Julian, the pain-
fully futile and self-righteous hero, does as
well as can be expected. G. K. Souper
plays the unpleasant part. Miss Nelly
Mortyne is excellent as Margaret’s mother.
So also is Hugh C. Buckler, the lawyer; and
there are some well-played minor parts.

By the time these lines impact the public
eye, “Via Wireless” will have fied Melbourne-
wards, and the stage carpenters at Her
Majesty’s will be toiling overtime to get
“Jack and the Beanstalk” ready for presen-
tation on Saturday night.

While this issue is, as it were, hovering
between earth and heaven —-or, to be less
poetical, but more precise, between the Re-
ligious Editor and the Printer’s Devil —George
Marlow, Ltd., is having a house-warming at
the new Adelphi—the big theatre which pro-
poses to thrive where Sydney’s Paddy Mar-
ket used to bloom. Marlow’s intention is
evidently to get in ahead of Bruce Smith,
with Blood, and his first thrill is “The Bad
Girl of the Family,” one of the bedroom
dramas which have lately come along in a
muddy flood.
** * *
At the Palace Theatre, on Saturday and
Monday, Sydney Stage Society, with the
valuable help of the only Titheradge, did its
best with Ashley Dukes’s four-act comedy,
“Civil War.” To a certain extent it was
civil war. On the opening night, some fool-
ish gods interrupted to such an extent that
Titheradge threatened to retreat, and leave
the field to the enemy, unless they behaved
themselves. With a mixed professional
and amateur cast, it is almost impossible to
get an even rendering of a play; and this
version of Dukes’s comedy wasn’t the glori-
ous exception to the general rule. A few
passages were admirably handled, and won
generous applause; in one of them Tither-
adge was concerned, and Lily Titheradge
was partly responsible for another. For his
plot, A. Dukes takes two violently antagon-
istic people—one the usual obstinate old
nobleman, and the other a “Socialist.” The
son of the first, and the daughter of the
second, regardless of anything but the course
■ —or curse —of true love, get married, and
hopelessly complicate the situation. As Sir
John Latimer, the obstinate old bart., Tither-
adge was excellent. S. Scarlett acted vig-
orously as John Shannon, the honest but.
accursed “Socialist” ; Lily Titlieradge played
sympathetically as Margaret Shannon, the
capturer of the bart.’s son; and Leonard
Willey, as the son, scored well several times.

Third Act Curtain. Mabel Trevor (as Margaret

“The Grey Kimona,” billed as Australia’s
pioneer musical comedy, was played at Syd-
ney Criterion on two nights last week. It
proved to be faithful and true to type. The
charming numbers of F. Wynne Jones might
be run in to any Williamson or Clarke-Meynell
musical production without a solitary critic
knowing the difference: and D. H. Souter’s
libretto is as good as the average imported
“book.” The piece was handicapped by a
mostly-amateur interpretation. The comic
Irishman of R. B. Orchard, for example,
was very trying in spots. This particular
variety of Celt is so familiar to playgoers
that, unless he is portrayed by a Master, he
stands in chronic peril of having clods flung
at him. Miss Amy Murphy sang delight-
fully as the Daimio’s daughter, and so did
Sydney Gordon, baritone and lover. Miss
Pressy Preston was thoroughly alluring as
Comet Bright. The hit of the show on the
comedy side was scored by Arthur Woods,
whose Executioner was one of the most hil-
arious Japanese products the writer has met.
The chorus sang excellently, even if it didn’t
always know what to do with its feet.
m» * *
Leipzig, a conjurer who makes a success
of the unsensational business of card-
juggling, stood at the footlights of Sydney
Tivoli on Saturday night, and spoke to the
audience in the usual kind, patient manner
peculiar to conjurers. The chief interest
of the show, however, is still supplied by
R. A. Roberts, who quick-changes his
clothes, his speech and liis manner, and
rattles through the Dick Turpin play for
live characters. The rapidity of Roberts
is almost uncanny; one has the uncomfort-
able suspicion that he owns five fully-
dressed bodies and a transferable soul, which
changes trains behind the scenes like a
seasoned commercial traveller, and leaves
four silent forms huddled in the dark whilst
it acts and enjoys life in the fifth out where
the limelight shines. The commonplace ex-
planation, probably, is that Roberts has
merely learnt how to do without buttons.
Other names that lend enchantment to the
bill are Florence Challis, Alexander Prince
and Morris and Wilson.
** * *
Each year Wirths’ Circus finds it more
to unwind its programme to a Syd-
ney audience in lime to permit everybody to
reach home at a reasonable hour. Every
visit sees at least one more big turn added
to a list which already seemed to have
reached the limit, until to-day the Wirth
Bros, can claim to have discovered the use-
ful art of squeezing a quart of entertain-
ment into a pint-pot of time. On Saturday
night, when the Sydney season was com-
menced at Prince Alfred Park, 25 items,
many of them requiring the most intricate and
careful fixings, raced against the clock, and
beat it. The “star” turn was provided by
the Flying Jordans, and the claim of the
management that it is the greatest act of
its sort in the world is near enough vertical
fact to be accepted. There was more thrill
to the square inch of that performance,
most of it away up in the air at the top of
the tent, than there would be in 10 civil
wars led by Bruce Smith and “Solemn” Lee.
Perhaps the most graceful wire performance;
ever seen in Sydney was given by The Adelia
sisters and brother George, while as acrobats
the Frantz party needs only to be artisti-
cally dressed to challenge anything in its
line of revolving business ; and if Danny
Ryan's Americans, Miss Millie Dagmar’s
dogs, Philip Wirth’s horses, Kitchen and
Foy’s horizontal bar work (an alteration
in their costume, too, is urgent), Marco’s
wild animals, and Quira Meers’s horseback-
ism, cannot keep the ringside crowded for
the. nest six weeks, it will be because a sus-
picion is abroad that the blood-sweating hip-
popotamus is a State Fright in an advanced
stage of the disease.

West’s, at Sydney Glaciarium, is booming
with Fergus Hume’s success, “The Mystery
of a Hansom Cab.” Ever since the drama
has been running across the screen, money has
had to be turned from the doors. In addition
to the Mystery there is the usual varied
programme; but the items are changed so
frequently these nights that it is hardly
worth mentioning them. Anyhow, for the
present they are completely eclipsed by the
Cab story.

George Marlow, Ltd., will produce a picture
drama at the Palace Theatre on, Saturday
night. It is the film which recently at-
tracted such crowds to the Melbourne Glaci-
arium, “The Luck of Roaring Camp,” as
played before the machine by Marlow’s own
company. There are 4000 feet of thrills
and nerve bumps in it.
** * *
One of the most charming features of
Spencer’s recent programmes at the Lyceum
was “Historic Spots of Old London”; the
man who superintended the taking of those
pictures knew the old place, and loved it.
Exactly what it is no man knoweth, but the
energetic and versatile Spencer has some-
thing extra unique up his sleeve, and hints
darkly at a coming announcement of gigantic
tonnage.
** * *
The Hawaiian singers and dancers will
commence their Sydney season at the
Y.M.C.A. Hall on Saturday night. A new
thrill is promised by the lady who performs
the weird hula —the national dance of
Hawaii.
** m *
“The Belle of Brittany,” late of Sydney,
met her predestined young man under the
Trysting Tree at Melbourne Royal last Sat-
urday night, when the shrieks of a. great
multitude welcomed the “Arcadians” Co.
back to their old camp. The Belle in ques-
tion, as was recently remarked by this jour-
nal, has nothing of Brittany about her, nor
is daffodil time in the 18th century sug-
gested by the fact that the maid to an alleged
marquis of the old noblesse dances in a some-
what-hobble skirt. There is no air of by-
gone romance, no color of ancient probability
in the manner of the opera. But the ma-
terials for a satisfactory entertainment are
there. The dressing is charmingly decora-
tive, Goatcher’s two scenes are first-class, the
music is sweetly graceful as a rule, and, al-
though the chorus ladies are not strong in
beauty and vivacity, they sing like good Aus-
tralians.
* * * *
What there is in the story might very
well have been elaborated. Percy Clifton,
who appears to be a miller with money in
the bank, has a jolly wife (Miss Kavanagh),
and an only daughter (Miss O’Connor), and
the daughter is under orders to marry a dis-
tinguished cook (Charles Stone), who comes
from Paris to claim his tender morsel. The
strange chef from the. French metropolis
doesn’t* impress the maiden, however. She
kisses him, but, as he feelingly -complains,
there is no chutney in her caress. The
miller holds a mortgage or something on the
neighboring chateau, and, unless the giddy
old marquis “turns up” at once to redeem
the miller’s document, his chateau will be
lost to him. So the marquis, who is Tom
Walls, does turn up, with Miss Gilliam as his
maid-in-waiting, and he takes possession of
“the whole dam show.” Things are mostly
dam or dev’lish with the bottle-nosed noble-
man. His allusions are apt to be horsey,
and his accent sounds as though he must
have been running a two-up school at Ver-
sailles. He forgets his connection with the
plot, and introduces an illegitimate variety
“turn” of modern design, humorous for a
while, but unpardonably long and interrup-
tive. Otherwise the marquis is an amusing
anachronism. He pelts the other characters
with gags, and wants to know why a cold,
deserted sausage should be surrounded with
parsley, to “kid” the poor devil that it is
living in the country. And so forth. In
spite of the marquis the plot hangs together.
Ilis son captures the miller’s lovely daugh-
ter, and thus saves the family estate, whilst
the accomplished chef reverts to his jealous
former sweetheart (Miss Gilliam), iu con-
sideration of the malevolent genius she dis-
played in tampering with his noblest dish.
Also, a local heiress, who was intended for
the marquis’s son, pairs off with a falsetto
johnnie in a tall hat.

new-comer shows no special cause for her

Motoring in New Zealand.
A well-known motorist in New Zealand, when
writing to a relative in Sydney, says the British
car, Wolseley, a product of Vickers, Son & Maxim,
is fast displacing its foreign competitors. Such
news as this should be welcome to every ardent
Britisher, as it is about time a British ear should
command a good market in a British country.
According to the latest English paper, the same
make of car is scoring with equal success in South
Africa, India and the Argentine.*
David Jones, Ltd., have issued an autumn cata-
logue which satisfactorily answers the inevitable “ What
shall I get for Easter?” No woman of spirit ever admits
she has a rag to her name. Four times a year she goes
through the same agonising catechism. The autumn
plunge is into coats and skirts j and David Jones, Ltd.,
are prepared to meet the demands of a record rush of
customers.*

** * *
At the Princess’s the cheerful little moral-
ity comedy, knoAvn as “A Message from
Mars,” offers milk of human kindness to
every heart that is taking any. Melbourne
is rolling up freely to get the milk, but the
audiences probably forget to take it to heart.
They just drink it, and rub a little on their
complexions—-or their boots, maybe, lhe
Message is rather crude in composition, and
too direct to be artistic; all the same, it in-
terests the many that objected to being
preached at by the supernatural stranger in
“The Third Floor Back.” In the present
revival Gregan McMahon is the well-to-do
young Horace, who dreams that a bluey-
brown man from Mars doubles, him up with
electric currents, and orders him out into a
cold world to shovel snow. And Gregan is
always likeable —a trifle too dry in his sel-
fishness, uncommonly funny in his dynamic
shocks, and forcible in his regeneration. The
Minnie of Miss Harvey is gushingly art-
less in her manner, and labored in her speech,
yet she does very nicely. Aunt Martha
supports the burden of social dignity Avell
enough. John Hesford is fairly convincing
as the Tramp, and two or three of the slight
character sketches escape unfavorable com-
ment. Among the conspicuous delinquents
the Policeman is awful, and the rather podgy
impersonator of young man Arthur Dicey
invites disrespect. Also the tall, command-
ing person from Mars amounts to a calam-
ity, because of his great importance to the
piece. In the present commentator’s eye,
there is something wrong with his unearthly
elocution, and everything wanting in his
conception of impressive deportment. His
height, however, counts for righteousness in
the easy spectacular part, and the stage ef-
fects which attend him are neatly managed.
“Mars” finishes the present season sometime
next week. On Saturday the Williamson
Dramatic Co. comes along .with “Paid in
Full.”

Students of Australian juvenile precocity
and infant phenomena will find 100 young-
sters performing “The Geisha” at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.) just now. The Juvenile
Opera Co. has the stage until Friday only,
and will not appear in “Floradora,” as was
threatened. On Saturday the adults who
dealt with “A Waltz Dream” last year, are
to repeat that melodious German opera ;
or, rather, Florence Young, Roberts and
some others will sing again in their old
parts, and the rest will be changes in the
cast.
At the King’s on Saturday, there was a
passable revival of “In London Town.”
This is the drama of Sims and. Shirley,
made irksome by ponderous comic relief,
which keeps one thinking of mammoths at
play. The mammoths are Miss Russell and
Messrs. Brown and DeVine, who have one
whole,' large, burly scene entirely to them-
selves, besides being pushed into other situa-
tions. The sportive trio leave less than the
normal space for the recreations of Virtue
and the- riots of Vice. Still Virtue, in the
persons of Walter Baker and Miss Beatrice
Holloway, has sufficient room for self-indul-
gence. Walter, as a heart-broken million-
aire in search of a daughter, performs sun-
dry good actions, with all the magnificence
of the very, very rich. Incidentally, also,
he attracts the eye by his wintry hair, ex-
pensive suitings, and a hat reminiscent of
artistic Bohemia. Miss Holloway is a. lady
who emerges from a vulgar slumdom with a
repertoire of sobs, and a filial .yearning. . In
due course she finds her parent, and gives
offence to her blind protector, Arthur Styan.
Styan is the heavy rascal of the show, who
bangs his boy attendant Avith a stick, while
Richard Bellairs and Miss Vera Remee are
cast as a couple of beauties who adopt com-
mon sins as domestic accomplishments. The
scenery is Bruntonic, and the story reeks
of the popular odors of beer and pawnshop.
And, being one of those dramas in which
anything may happei\ its incidents are
many.

At the Opera House the bill has lost
weight in some respects, and the Football
Dogs were playing a very ragged game the
last time The Bulletin saw them. The
gaps left by recent departures are filled by
such old familiar items as Brown and Robin-
son, and Levinsky. But the. bald-headed
vedding guest is now breathing his last
farewell; therefore Saturday is expected to
bring fresh faces and pastimes new.
* * *
Last week Spencer showed in Melbourne
a film that was the nicest bit of pictorial
pastry. This picture, “The Lad from Ire-
land,” told a story of love in a hearty
fashion, and, besides giving some incidental
snapshots of Irish scenes, was run off to an
accompaniment of* Irish tunes that made
every Hibernian present throb with emo-
tion. There were other pictures, of course,
but for the moment “The Lad from Ireland”
was It.
m* * *
The Talts, at the Melbourne Glaciarium,
have been uncurling a film bearing on “The
Luck of Roaring Camp.” The picture,
which was taken under the supervision of
Mr. “Willie” Lincoln, depicts tbe principal
incidents in the incredible drama. The
fabrication is all right in its way, though
this paper prefers Taits’ occasional displays
of color subjects. Which reminds the writer
that a tinted film of particular beautv Avas
dropped into the programme on Saturday
night. The item referred to some spots in
Maoriland, and was the artistic work of
Rathe FrSres.
** * *
The Joe Blascheck whom Melbourne
knew of yore is back from a long absence in
Britain and elsewhere, ivith a polished plat-
form manner and a tall lady colleague in
“drawing-room entertainment.” The lady
—Miss Wrighton—restricts herself to the
piano stool, where she plays accompaniments
and sings on her own, or in duet Avith J. B.
She is a telling vocalist, with a turn for
humor, and a useful voice. Blascheck, for
his part, is a character actor in a small Avay
—a musical sketcher, a storyteller. In the
Athenaeum Hall, where Mel. B. Spurr used
to give a monologue entertainment, the com-
bined efforts of Blascheck and Miss Mildred
Wrighton should draw the patronage of those
persons Avho take their pleasures mildly, and
are not prone to hearty laughter during
Lent. The two performers have a mile of
items in their several and joint repertoires,
and they vary their selections.

KING’S.
Adelaide’s shows have roused up suddenly.
The Royal wakes from one of its rare
closures with “The Cay Gordons” ; and, as
nothing except melodrama has been seen
there for a long time, the music, gaiety and
glitter are specially welcome. The Troca-
dero has found it time to get out of the
open air, and will presently start again un-
der cover. The Empire has been starring
“Captain Midnight,” and West’s house has
made a feature of the classic tragedy of the
Di Rimini. These pictures go on to Moonta,
Wallaroo, and those parts afterwards.
Yorke’s Peninsula is being looked after by a
Mabel Hardinge Co., which stages “Hands
Across the Sea” and “East Lynne” at each
township. To get back to Town shows, The
Tivoli has a return visit from the biped
dogs, and the inebriated pu- gets his usual
ovation every time he totters into an en-
trance marked “Saloon.” Fasola, too, adds
his illusions to a strong programme. Hap-
pily, he has not brought his lion along. The
front door of the Stock Exchange looks, for
reasons which have never been satisfactorily
explained, right on to the stage door of the
Tivoli, and if a lion hau got out among the
bears and the Bullfinches there might have
been a horrid scene.
An Adelaide police-court case last week
walked straight out of a Crockett story. Husband
and wife, over 70, had been married only three
years, and lived with the lady’s son and his
family He spent his time in running a convent-
icle, and she, apparently, in getting up domestic
rows. When the rows got particularly bad, the
Rev. Newly-nailed called a meeting of his church
and had his lady formally expelled from it on the
ground of Scandal ! Then she sued him for
maintenance ; but when she accused him in Court
of an “unholy intimacy’’ with her son’s wife,
the P.M. shook his head and dismissed the case.
It even had an underclothing joke, in,Crockett's
sprightliest manner. The lady was shown to
have incurred a debt of £2 14s. for “ combs,” arid
the Bench could not see how hair ornaments
could come to such a sum. Then counsel bash-
fully suggested that he “thought it was an abbre-
viation.” (Laughter.)
It is gravely stated, as though the news
were quite satisfactory, that the Customs “be-
lieve that they have closed up one of the breaches
in the immigration restriction policy of the Com-
monwealth,” which has provided means of ingress
for 400 or 500 Chows within the past few years.
Also it is mentioned that the “ full story ” of the
law-evading practices will probably never be told;
but the Minister of Cu6toms “ regards the period
covered by the offences as one of the darkest on
the records.” Too much or too little is said. The
500 Chows who have come into Australia through
a breach in the legislative wall didn’t walk here.
They had to be shipped, and the discovery of the
means whereby they evaded the Customs suggests
a knowledge of a shipping company’s complicity
in the fraud on White Australia. Why is the
story of “one of the darkest periods in the
Customs records” to be kept dark?
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Parisine” : Williamson regrets he cannot persuade
Sarah Bernhardt to come to Australia. This proves
she is good yet, at 66 —and her enormously successful
American tour, just ended, is evidence on the same
point. This visit to Monsieur Sam was a brilliant
defiance of Frohman and all the Rings. At New Or-
leans they absolutely shut her out, and she per-
formed in a tent. Before leaving New York she
charmed critics and audience alike with a perform-
ance of Rostand’s “Woman of Samaria,” in which
Christ and the Apostles are introduced. For some
years she has regularly acted this in Paris, in Holy
Week, and sometimes on Good Friday nights. The
play is in the Melbourne Public Library—all in
verse. It would be a fine piece of work for anybody
but Rostand, but is far below “Cyrano” and “Chanti-
clgre” as a work of art. Sarah these days is a
great-grandmother. Her son Maurice is a grand-
father —at 47. He it was to whom the lady was
pleased to refer as “My little accident.” Of course
he is very strict, like Dumas Fils. Maurice was
nearly a man when Sarah married Damala. There
have been plays of the Prodigal Daughter, and Prodi-
gal Father. But nobody has tackled the Prodigal
Mother.
Melbourne is to get its first experience of a “Reper-
tory Theatre” under business-like conditions. Gregan
McMahon, who ran “The Players” in their palmiest
days, and gave promise of establishing a school of
modern comedy acting until he threw up the job to
take a touring engagement, has formed rational
plans. He asks for no Government endowment or
backing from private guarantors, but is practically
starting a school for amateurs with a Repertory
Theatre purpose. Plis pupils will as soon as pos-
sible be put into rehearsal for three plays to run for
four nights each. Ibsen, Galsworthy and St. John
Hankin are to be tried in the first place; and the
best professionals available will be asked to do what
work is beyond the amateur strength of the organi-
sation. The business management is to run on
the lines of the Glasgow Repertory Theatre. The
price of tickets for each performance will be 65., 3s.
and Is. at the doors, but ardent supporters will be
invited to take parcels of coupon tickets at reduced
figures. There are said to be fully 4000 persons
hungering for repertory plays in Melbourne.
McMahon reckons that if he can get 100 of them to
buy each a parcel of tickets, he will have all the
assured support absolutely necessary for each season.
He is concluding his present engagement with the
“Message from Mars” Co., and will then devote all
his energies to the new scheme.
The “revue,” a form of entertainment supposed
to be confined to Paris, is getting a hold in London
at last. A revue is a kind of pantomime, with
all the topical allusions left in, and all the fairy-
story and children’s element left out. If one were
presented in Australia just now, it might show
test-matches with Japan, or Douglas Mawson depart-
ing for the Pole by airship; and the comic songs
about the Referenda would be sung by someone made
up in the likeness of King O’Malley, or Rupert
Clarke, or Judkins, instead of by a red-nosed Dame,
who is the improbable mother of a fairy prince.
The background of Girl and Glitter is the same in
both cases. The latest London revue runs about an
hour, and is cheekily entitled “By George!” its
author being “young George” Grossmith.
The Sydney Brough whose death was cabled last
week had no interest for Australia, apart from the
fact that he was a cousin of the late Robert Brough,
and a brother of Percy Brough. Percy is dimly re-
membered as a tall and rather lumpish young actor,
who appeared in the first Australian production of
“The Liars,” 13 years ago.
Howard Chambers, who first took up professional
singing with John Fuller in Auckland (M.L.), died
lately in ’Murka. He was with Rickards in Aus-
tralia for a time, and for eight years or more
was chief baritone with a Boston co. and other
American combines.
It was bound to come! The Yic. A.N.A. has
passed a resolution that the Government be asked to
appoint a censor for moving pictures. It has been
brought home to the A.N.A. that thousands of girls
with brief frocks and hair in plaits visit picture-
shows in Melbourne and suburbs, and it has been told
with bated breath that some of the views are calcu-
lated to bring the Blush of Shame to the Cheek of
the Young Person. The cheek of the young person
of to-day is a matter of public concern.
H. P. Lyons, the patriarch of Australian advance
agents, is again, at 70, in harness. . He has gone to
Westralia, to meet Nicola and Company, and pilot
them through the States.
Photos of the singers whom Melba has engaged'
for her grand opera venture in Australia support the
statement that they are expensive persons of artistic
distinction. The women show good looks and style.
Wayda, a dramatic soprano, has appeared at Covent
Garden. A pretty. Russian lyric soprano, Marie
Axarine, is known outside of Russia. The principal
contralto, Marie Ranzanberg, is a mighty fine woman
with a big repertoire of principal parts, including
Carmen. Another contralto is Elenora De Cisneros,
an Italian ; and it is to be hoped the ladies will get
along nicely together. Melba, anyhow, is not the
star to permit any serious disturbance which she
doesn’t herself originate. The tenors of the co. seem
sure to fill the bill. John McCormack, the Irishman,
has a renowned voice and a merry eye, whilst Albert
Quesnel, the second tenor, is said to be generally
adored in suffragette circles. As for the conductor,
his name speaks for itself —-Angeline! No orchestra
or chorus could fail to be inspired by it.
Kitty Campion, sister of the better-known Gertie
Campion, formerly of the Pollards, has left
Maoriland for two years’ training in Europe. Calve
enthused over her voice, the Taits took her up,
Auckland gave her benefit concerts, and enough
money has been supplied to pay fees and exes, for
awhile.
“0.K.”: Ethel Irving seemingly has scored an-
other notable success in “The Witness for the De-
fence,” wherein she is associated with George Alex-
ander at the St. James’s (Fogville). Her part has
a wide range of histrionics, and she is said to
go higher in tragedy than she ever went before.
“Beautiful and truthful,” the Times calls her per-
formance. Ethel Irving is as intensely natural in
dramatic effect as Margaret Anglin, and withal she
is more powerful and more decorative in style than
the American actress. There is a grave doubt
as to whether the pieces she is to bring to Australia
will appeal to the multitude that stayed away from
(say) “The Thief,” and is rarely attracted to “so-
ciety” comedy-drama; but there is no doubt that
the star will captivate her audiences. A great
actress is not necessarily interesting all the time.
Miss Anglin, who was almost great, lacked personal
magnetism. But Ethel Irving gives fascination to
the character she creates. She is the finished ar-
tist, as many are, in vocal exnression, facial ex-
pression, and the capacity of suiting the action
to the word, as well as in the hundred and one
little details of silent effect necessary to a full in-
terpretation —or elaboration —of the “author s
meaning.” But beyond this, she has the genius
for losing herself in her work. , The lines of her
part and all its stage directions are telegraphed from
her head to her soul, and her arts become impulses
as she proceeds. The audience is not so much
magnetised and deceived by the actress as convinced
by the woman.
“0.K.”: The musty traditions of pantomime char-
acterisation are getting on the nerves of even the
lads and lassies. They complain of the monotony
of the annual hotch-potch. Boys want to know
why the “principal boy” is always a girl, and girls
remark the fact that every panto, has a “dame’ m
the foreground, and that the stereotyped aged fe-
male is invariably “a man dressed up.” Also the
young of both sexes seem to bo mutually tired o.
“the baron” who pairs off with the dame. His title
worries them. “Why is there always a silly old baron
in a pantomime?” they ask. And the answer is:
“Because there always was.” Our pantos, contain
the germs of nursery tales, which came mostly from
Germany, where every old romance had a baron in it.
So the baron is almost a fixture in Grand Christmas
Annualism. You get him, or something very like him,
in even “Dick Whittington” and “The Forty
Thieves” sometimes. As for the dame with
the sugar-loaf head, and the heroic boy, who is
invariably a girl, there is never a rest from
them. The Meynell and Gunn production of “Cin-
derella” was the least conventional treatment of an
old panto, subject that has been seen in this country;
nevertheless, the prince was a girl, and the heroine’s
father was a baron, and the dame-baroness was a
man dressed up. Pantomime manufacturers —stage
manager, librettist, ballet-mistress, scene painter,
property man —are the cringing slaves of tradition.
They do what has been done before, with the least
possible variation. The chief difference between one
panto, and another is in the character of the star
variety turn. This is interpolated, and has nothing
whatever to do with the compilers of the rest of the
entertainment. We hear a lot about the brain force
which is expended in devising novelties for the em-
bellishment of the annual panto., but where is any
sign of novelty in the construction of the framework,
or the arrangement of the fooling ? The people
responsible for the eternal repetition of she-boys, he-
dames, bottle-nosed barons, and long-drawn-out
marches, may not be actually incapable of original
ideas, yet they are evidently unable to make new
moves. Why not introduce the thin end of a
wedge of human interest into panto., and try also
the novelty of bringing on a wandless fairy, with
mystical effects? The fairy queen, who just walks
on with a long stick, and sings a cheap, sentimental
ballad, is as foolish an anachronism as _ the gnome
king, who tries to look like the Devil in “Faust,”
and is finally lined up with the rest in a medley
chorus. There is no work of imagination in a
modern panto, show. Everything is traditional,
matter-of-fact.
Among the very few stock players who performed
with G. V. Brooke, and are still living, are Mrs.
Gladstone and Mr. and Mrs. William Ryan. Mrs.
Gladstone lives in the Old Colonists’ Homes, near
Melbourne, and, at 81, is a bustling little lady, to
be found mostly in her garden. Another old lady,
not an actress,' in the Cottages, arrived in Victoria
in the early ’so’s. One of her vivid stories is about
the Queen’s Birthday ball, at which Governor Hotham
gave his guests “Colonial” beer out of bedroom jugs,
instead of champagne and spirits, and caused a fear-
ful commotion thereby.

What a lesson in platform deportment for the
man Hughes and his anarchical following !
The mind of the Tory Fatman runs
naturally to jobbery. The Argus, forecasting
what will happen when the Federal Parliament
has full power to Socialise industries, sees how
everything will be nationalised and all the good
billets—managerships and so forth—will be
handed to Labor politicians. Well, Labor-man
Tom Price got into power in S. Aus. in 1905, and
he and his Labor successors have been in office
ever since, with full power to nationalise as many
things as they pleased. That is nearly six years—
and what Labor man has been given any fat billet
in all that time? Also, McGowen has been in
power in N. S. Wales for a good many months,
and the only fat job he has found for any-
body has been for S. W. Moore, who, instead of
being a Laborite, was a Minister in Wade’s
Government.
WAS OTHELLO MAD?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two Melbourne experts in lunacy have respectively
stated that “Othello was irresponsible for his
action when he killed Desdernona,” and “Othello was
suffering from the variety of mental disorder which
is now termed paronoia.”
Was Othello mad?
When, deluded by lago re the gentle Desdernona,
He believed he had a rival in the favors of his donah,
Was the gory Moor of Venice compos mentis in his
fury?
Would a modern Judge have hanged him with the
sanction of a jury?
Or would some learned members of the medical pro-
fession
Have attributed the murder to a lunatic’s obsession?
There’s an up-to-date suggestion
In the hearing of the question:
Is green jealousy a brain disease —-a malady like
cancer ?
Quite a lot of pretty wives are interested in the
answer,
Was Othello mad?
Was Othello mad ?
Say, was he irresponsible for all his words and actions
When he dreamt of danger lurking in his lady-love’s
attractions ?
Or was it Moorish temper that excited him to
slaughter
On the strength of mere suspicion, as he hadn’t even
caught her
Making eves at his lieutenant under sinister con-
ditions?
Could a Judge accept the dictum of a dozen grave
physicians
Re the question somewhat knotty
Whether jealous men are “dotty,”
Like the cranks in the asylum who are glum or
feather-headed,
And forgetful of the missus, if they happen to be
wedded ?
If those wife-forgetting husbands are insane, and
rightly dreaded,
Was Othello mad?
Was Othello mad?
When a man suspects his angel of intention to
deceive him,
And behaves in such a manner as is apt to make her
leave him ;
When he rolls his eyes like Johnson at each pleasant-
spoken stranger,
And snaps and snarls his hatred like the doggie in
the manger,
He is too absurd to live, and, as he couldn’t be
It were best to kill him gently ’ere he’s guilty of a
murder;
He may wish (it’s only human)
To monopolise the woman,
But if her nature craves for the society of others—
Well, there ought to be an inquest on the hubby
whom she smothers,
And the coroner should put it to the doctors —“Oh,
my brothers,
Was Othello mad?”
VICTORIA.

SOCIETY
“The Philistine ” on the Pharisee :
John Longstaff, talking to a Vic. Artists’ Society crowd
about tlie keen pleasure that a painter derives from
travel, “ remembered that while passing through the
glory of a beautiful Mediterranean day in the Straits of
Messina, a fellow-passenger sat reading a novel. His
(J.L.’s) pleasure in the fine color about him moved him to
say to the reader, ‘That’s fine, isn’t it?’ The other
man raised one eye for a moment, replied ‘Great!’
and went on reading as before.” Longstaff’s little
story seemed satirically humorous to the audi-
ence of artists and art-lovers, genuine or make-
believe ; and apparently they didn’t realise that there
could be any other point of view. Yet, to an audi-
ence of habitual booklovers, it would have sounded like
an artist’s unconscious confession of his inability to think
of anything as being possibly more interesting than the
study of his own trade. Nature, from the artist's point
of view, is to be considered only as an education in color
lines, curves and all sorts of “effects.” And,
since Nature is all around him, he is usually talking shop
and almost invariably thinking shop. Moreover, in
Longstaff’s case, the artist was enjoying a comparatively
new scene, whereas the man who just raised his
eyes and said “Great” was possibly a commercial
traveller who had looked at a terrible lot of fine colored
scenery and had often been through the Straits of
Messina, and was more fond of exercising hisimagination
in book-reading than of feasting his limited sense of
color. Besides, the spectacle hud no commercial interest
for him ; from the novel on the other hand, lie was
possibly getting a good business idea —just as Longsiaff
was from the scenery. Why, therefore, the implied note
of superiority in Longstaff’s remarks ?
DRESS—AND LESS.
THE HUSBAND WITH A BAST: “ Hmp! It was
very different with my first wife; she dressed
solely to please me!"
HIS WIFE OF THE PRESENT: “ Indeed; then
that explains why her dress bills came to
next to nothing.”
Those parts of the world where the Chow
is welcomed as a brother are in for trouble
shortly. All through the Chinese winter (which
is our summer) the inhabitants of vast tracts of
country have been dying like flies, of pneumonic
plague. The bubonic horror, with which Aus-
tralia has had some slight acquaintance, is a mere
circumstance compared with pneumonic plague.
Bubonic can only be acquired by injection ;
pneumonic may be borne in every wind that
blows. Bubonic offers a fair chance of a
cure; pneumonic practically none. And, finally,
death from pneumonic plague is incomparably
more agonising than from bubonic. The sur-
vivors in the afflicted districts of Chowdom,
having no time or inclination to either bury or
burn their dead, have been dumping them on the
ice of the nearest frozen rivers, relying on the
remains being carried down stream when the
melting sun of summer transpires. Those rivers
are melting now. In a short time a considerable
proportion of China’s 400 million inhabitants
will not only be breathing the deadliest variety of
plague, but drinking it and washing in it. By
that time there should be enough of it drifting
round the yellow empire to infect the universe.
In the circumstances, if the Commonwealth
authorities aren’t already busy thinking out some
particularly baffling test-sentence in Norwegian
for use on visiting Mongols, they are unworthy
of their hire.

Our reliable brown brother gave another
illustration of his virtues on the Guthrie during
its last trip down from Singapore and Java. The
ship got into very bad weather, whereupon the
Hindu firemen knocked off work and gave their
whole attention to prayer. In fact, they knocked
off work so thoroughly that the white engineers
had to go below and do the firing. The Burns-
Philp people, who own the Guthrie and run this
line, long ago lost the last remnants of their affec-
tion for the brown brother; but their trouble is
that they are up against the competition of subsi-
dised foreigners who use the cheap, unreliable
person as a habit.
* * * *
A public official in a southern country
town keeps his appendix preserved in a bottle,
and shows the trophy to callers at the office. For
thereby hangs a tale. The owner of this ampu-
tated curiosity went to a
metropolitan doctor, in the
first place, to be relieved of
his complications. Appendi-
citis was diagnosed as the
trouble, and the patient was
operated on, not once, but
thrice. After the third
operation he returned to his
provincial home, feeling just
about the same as before,
plus the effects of three surgi-
gical assaults. Finally, a
local doctor induced the
wounded man to let him
make inquiries into the case
with a knife; and the country
practitioner duly removed
the appendix, which is
now on exhibition. The
moral seems to be that no man should pay for
having had his appendix removed until it is pre-.
sen ted to him by the operator. A dentist shows
the abstracted tooth to his patient as evidence of
good faith, and an appendicitist should be re-
quired to produce the missing link. No appen-
dix, no pay.
MaSterbon, one of the M.L. inland towns
that held its hand up for No-License in 1908,
appears to be sorry now that it spoke. Some
months ago the Cold Tea party’s local organiser
announced that, as the result of inquiries
put to 128 retailers in the town, 82 had
stated that No-License had been good for
business, 16 were indefinite and 14 were
unfavorable; 105 stated that the closing of
the bars had improved the public order and general
moral tone, six were indefinite and one only was
unfavorable. Which seemed clear and altogether
encouraging. On the other hand, the party which
is against Local Prohibition said that business was
going to ruin, and backed the saying with an un-
precedented list of bankruptcies. In the middle
of the argument one Bedford, of Dunedin—the
city of that Nicholls who last year found out more
about Sydney pubs than ever the Oldest Inhabit-
ant knew —arrived on the scene to pat Cold Tea
on the back, and tell it to stick its chest out to
meet the foe at the ballot box in a few months.
Over 1000 people turned up at Bedford’s meeting,
and took it over and cursed what it was expected to
bless. Business men mounted the rostrum and de-
nounced No-License as a failure and a fraud, and,
in place of the anticipated motion that “this
meeting pledges itself to strike out the top line
next poll,” an overwhelming majority declared
itself for the restoration of licenses. The difficulty
is to get the necessary three-fifths majority. For
years Ashburton, another “dry” district, has
returned a bare majority of votes in favor of
restoration, but that is not enough. At the last
poll it was 347 votes short out of a total of nearly
6000 valid votes, and the old Jewish law that
sauce for the goose is Worcester for the gander
hasn’t got many friends in that part of theLoanly
Islands —that is, leaving out the associated forces
of Wowserisin and sly-grog.
A church was built on the quick-lunch
principle at Preston (Vic.) on Saturday, March
25. At C o’clock in the morning 150 voluntary
workers assembled on a vacant allotment. At
night the completed church stood in its place. It
was 65ft. x 30ft., of wood and iron ; and gas and
water were laid on. The particular brand of sal-
vation to be offered in the tabernacle is “ Church
of Christ ” —which is a sort of back-handed sug-
gestion to the other brands that they are affiliated
with the Devil. Still, the other brands gave the
hot gospellers a lift. Included in the crowd were
a dozen Chinese, some of whom did the cabinet-
making.

While the Victorian Employers’ Union,
barracking for the agricultural lmplement-
makers, declares for “free labor,” it takes good
care not to swallow its own medicine. Thus,
rule 1 of the General Carriers and Contractors’
Ass. declares that “ members must give prefer-
ence to association members when requiring
assistance” ; rule 2 says “ members must not em-
ploy ” those who are ** not in the association ” ;
and rule 3 declares that they “ must not tender
for cartage being done by another mfember.” On
tho other hand, rule 5 tells them that they “are
not tied to association rates when competing for
work done by non-association contractors ” —they
may knock the horrid non-unionist out just how
they please. The preference to unionists sought
by the workers is a mild thing compared with that.
The Age was rapped sharply by Justice
Higgins the other day, and told that it was
going the right way to make the industrial
trouble which it professes to be so anxious to
stop. The worry arose over what was printed
as an “ official report,” but was really the em-
ployers’ ex-parte statement of what took place at
the Agricultural Implement Dispute Conference.
“ It is my duty,” said his Honor, “to warn the
public that the account is untrustworthy, and
does not convey any true impressions of what took
place.” Also—“lf newspapers are going to
accept ex-parte reports, the ad vantages of these
conferences will be greatly diminished.” But
while things were certainly bad for the Age,
what about the party to the dispute which put
forward a statement that was untrustworthy and
misleading? The Age was deceived, and so, no
doubt, was the public. Now what was the object
of the deceiver ?
The Cold Country Board of Trade, which
has been afflicted with ideas of late, has invited
Australia to give its opinions on some proposals
that are to be considered at a conference at Berne
this year. One of these is that Leap Year shall
be abolished, and, instead, an odd day that is
somehow not to count at all shall be dropped
somewhere into the middle of every fourth year.
Other arrangements are proposed whereby eacli
quarter will consist of two months of 30 days
each and one of 31 days. Also it is
suggested that Christmas Day and New
Year’s Day should be on the same day
of the week ever and always, and that Easter
should be nailed down and prevented from wan-
dering at lai'ge over the calendar. There are
points about the proposals—like there were in the
already discarded daylight-saving idea. But if
the Board of Trade pushes on with them it
will bump against the ingrained conserva-
tism of churchianity, and that is a large
thing of great dead-weight. And, anyway,
the job, though it is picturesque in spots,
would be merely patching the garmant in a
place that doesn’t shriek nearly as loudly for re-
pair as some other parts of it. A country that
has tables of weights and measures and distances
like Britain’s ought to give its attention to that
monstrosity first.

Seemingly, the natural desire of woman to
get married accounts, for 550 of her having de-
parted from Britain to Salt Lake City within the
past year. Cable headings in Australian news-
papers libel the 550 by stating
that they were “decoyed” to the
land of matrimony, whereas
the propaganda of the Mormon
agents in England appears to be
openly conducted. Possibly they
cherished expectations that it will
prove an “improper gander” (as
the stout lady in “The Arca-
dians” comic opera would say)
when they get to Salt Lake City,
for there is a lingering idea
that the polygamy of Mormonism
used to cut both ways and
provide plurality of husbands as
well as plurality of wives.
Of all professedly Christian
religions Mormonism has the
giddiest reputation, and nobody
embraces it without a thought of
being embraced in return. The
object of the 550 respectable English
village females is clearly defined.
They have gone to get married
individually or collectively. Their
celibate condition forces them to
the faith of Brigham Young,
whilst foolish members of the
British Parliament ask the Home
Secretary whether he intends to
take steps to prevent the exodus.
The same question will be asked
in the Commonwealth Parliament
one day, perhaps, for there are a lot
of Australian girls who would gladly
join a Mormon community in, say,
Fiji. In the meantime the pertinent
suggestion of a tax on bachelors is
not even seriously entertained.

and has visions of political greatness, had, at
last report, raked in nearly £25; but when
Auckland was asked to parade its Maries, there
weren’t half a dozen to jingle their thrippennies
in the hat.
POLAR ETIQUETTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Tuesday.—Tlie cablegrams from Stewart
Island relating to the Scott Expedition have caused a
sensation in London, first owing to the presence of Captain
Amundsen in the Hay of Whales; and secondly, because
Captain Scott has established his second party at Cape
Adare. Sir Ernest Shackleton questions whether Captain
Amundsen is following the dictates of Polar etiquette in
wintering within Captain Scott’s sphere of influence, but
also asks : “ Is Scott following the same dictates in land
ing a party at Cape Adare, which is Mawson’s prospective
base ? ” — Cable.
The moral and the social code
Together make a trying load.
He is a fool who talks with glee
Of “ unconventiouality.”
Mankind will never cease to fret
O’er little points of etiquette.
For some are writ and some are not,
But ev’ryone must know the lot.
Yet what we have will not suffice ;
We need another rule precise :
You must not cut your neighbor’s ice.

* * * *
The barbarous practice of flogging prisoners
for certain offences came in for criticism the ot >w
day by the Judge in a Westraliau case, m wine
a young man was found guilty of an offence on a
child. In passing sentence the Judge remarked
that by law he was compelled to order a flogging
of at least nine strokes of the “ cat.” In this case
it was, he declared, ordering a man to be flogged
for his mental deficiencies. He could have gone
further than that without being wildly inaccurate.
Most of those who are flogged or hanged suffer for
their mental deficiencies, and the retention of the
old form of legal vengeance should revolt a gener-
ation which has given up the old habit of chaining
its mental patients to rings set in the floor, and
driving stakes through the corpses of its suicides.

TOWZER AND WOWSER.
“Here, Towzer! Towzer!”
“Did you call, Miss!”
“ Hamer ” ; With the formation of many
new unions, an old dispute has suddenly grown
big in Australian trade union circles. The dis-
pute is as to whether union organisation shall be
on trade or industrial lines. Many of the old
unions, which are almost exclusively trade unions,
are bitterly opposed to the new unionism, which is
mainly industrial. Yet, apparently, the industrial
union must triumph.
The employees in an
industry in which
engineers, coach-
builders, carpenters,
moulders, enamel-
lers, ironworkers’
assistants and a score
of other callings are
engaged, amongst a
mass of workers who
are nearly but not
quite entitled to
membership in the
unions embracing
these workers, are
frequently entirely
unorganised; and
when they think of
formingaunion, they
naturally consider
that a union of the
wholoindustry would
be best. The exist-
ing societies, how-
ever, usually kick
hard against this. The
Labor Council is the
body to which these
objections mostly come
in Sydney ; and the
Labor Council has so far
side-stepped the diffi-
culty by allowing new
unions if the applicants
are “not eligible for
membership in existing
unions,” but not other-
wise. This rule is, how-
ever, often ignored ; and
the Labor Council has
itself been guilty of some
inconsistency by granting the application of an
industrial body, the Motor and Cycle Workers’
Union, and turning down another, the Harbor
Trust Dredge Employees. The squabble is keen,
and is one of the most distinctive features of
unionism at the present time.

“0.C.C.”: The Rev’. Ash (what’s in a
name?) at Dubbo (N.S.W.) :
lie regretted that an additional hindrance to the work
of the churches had recently arisen in Dubbo. lie re-
ferred to the picture shows on Sunday night. Most of
the subjects, he understood, were secular, and some of
them were farcical —this on Sunday nights. The effect
on i lie young people was unsettling and bad, and it was
making light of one of the commandments—to keep the
day holy.
Picture shows on Sunday nights ! “ Secular sub-
jects,” too. Awful! And “farcical.” Heavens!
“Ye maurina whustle o’ the Sawbath —nor lauch,
forbye !”, Then look at the “hindrance to tilts
work of the churches ! ” This competition is too
cruel ! But what sort of a church is it, or what
kind of a creed can it be, that feels itself going
down before the cheap and cheerful picture show?

sang frequently, and the refrain which they droned
immediately before “ the offertory ” was, “Take
all my guilt away.” I gave threepence.

From J. H. Watson, concerning the old
record-making" wind-jammers :
“ Joynt” (B. 23/3/T1) is not correct with regard to the
Elizabeth Ami Bright, which he i-ays did the voyage from
Liverpool to Melbourne in 51 days. She left the first port
on June 20 and arrived in Hobson’s Bay September 9,
1860—81 days. No veksel lias done it, I think, in under
60, which is credited to the Thermopylae, 1868-9. One
writer says she did it in,56 days, but I cannot trace tlie
exact figures.
“10-2-4” : During the hearing of a Syd-
ney assault case, last week, the plaintiff, a dentist,
stated that defendant had charged him with
being “no gentleman.” Of course there was
trouble. There are thousands of citizens who
will sustain accusations of drunkenness, defraud-
ing the Government, and immoral conduct gener-
ally, with equanimity, not to say pride. But
hint that they are “ not gentlemen,” and they
see bright red and smash things. What is a
gentleman, anyhow? In Quedn Elizabeth’s
day Shane, eldest son of Con Bacagh O’Neill,
holder, of, the earldom of Tyrone, remarked:
“Being a.gentleman, my father never refusid no
child that any woman naymd to be his.” It is a
point of view that has always appealed to me.
Yet, how many little attorneys and so forth in
our midst would be simply maddened at the sug-
gestion that their soundest claim to the proud
style of “gent” lay in the fact that they had
“ never refusid no child that any woman naymd
to be theirs ! ”
From the Oldest Inhabitant ;
The most curious official census ever taken in Austra-
lia was collected in 1807, and was, of course, confined to
the little bit of settlement around Port Jackson. The
object was to ascertain the population, area of land
alienated, area under cultivation, stock, etc. The enu-
merators found that the total area held by settlers was
181,898 acres, of which less than 14,000_acres were in cul-
tivation ; the number of “settlers” — i.e., persons occu-
pying land—was 708. There were 270 wives, who were
victualled by Government, and 201 “ concubines ” fed by
their protectors. There were 35 legitimate children fed
by Government, and 289 illegitimates fed by th' ir parents.
The Government did not feed concubines or illegitimates.
They just) had to take their chance —and anything else
that their protectors could engineer for them from-the
bleeding country.
* * * *
It is a hopeless business trying to reform
the dishonest milkman by fining him apologetically
time after time ; water is so cheap that this most
cowardly of thieves will adulterate again and
again so long as he sees that each successive con-
viction brings him no nearer gaol. Barnett, S.M.,
at the Glebe the other day, passed a fine of £l5 to
a milk-and-waterman who, it was stated, had-been
convicted six times during the last three months.
The magistrate, m fining iiim £l5, seemed to think
he had done something drastic ; so lie allowed the
chronic mixer 21 days in which to produce the
money—possibly from the water-trough. Con-
trast this With the Adelaide way of doing things.
There the adulterator would have been asked fdr
£2O the first time ; after that, £5O aiid £lOO. Or,
if the initial fraud had been particularly watery’,
lie might have'been asked for £5O for the, first
offence, and told to take six months the next
time. And, after that, the papers would have
been splashed with a bold official advt. notifying
that Cowpump was a persistent swindler who had
better be given a wide berth. But as lie adulter-
ates in a different State, where almost anything
will pass if it is done m the sacred name of Com-
merce, he is merely clattering round, trailing his
seven eon victioijs him and looking for that
trifling £l5.

Victoria is to have two public holidays
this year on account of George’s birthday. r i'h<•
Monday following June 3 —if June 3 doesn’t fall
on a Monday—is a statutory holiday. But the
fifth George is graciously pleased to desire that
his arrival into the royal family shall be annually
celebrated on the actual anniversary of the day
when the lucky accident happened to him. June
3 this year falls on a Saturday, so froth Friday
night until Tuesday morning Victoria will be out
of work, so far as a Government proclamation can
make it so.
THE GIRL WITH PURPLE HAIR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Give me a shade that is soft and cool,
And a girl with purple hair,
Whose eyes are kind and who doesn’t mind
How little I chance to wear.
I want her to sit on the silent grass,
With nothing at all to say,
While she puts clear ice in the liquids nice
That she hands to me all the day.
I want her to look like a queen of dreams,
With a neck as cool as snow.
From her carmine lips to her finger tips
As fresh as the winds that blow.
I want her to smile like a goddess fair
When Aurora’s curtains shake,
With a hint of bliss in each lingering kiss
That I’m far too lazy to take.
Her warm, warm heart must be all my own.
And her soul must mate with mine,
While she keeps the flies from my dreaming eyes
And feeds me on frosted wine.
She must stay at the least a yard away,
That I may have perfect rest,
While I seem to mean I would like to lean
My head on her silken breast.
And thus shall I lie on the silent grass,
While the world grows young and fair,
Dreaming of bliss in the unborn kiss
Of a girl with purple hair.
N.S.W.

THE LAST “HONOR.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A big batch of new justices of the peace will shortly be
appointed. The list of nominations, which numbered
probably 2000, has been considered by the Oiiief Secre-
tary, and it is likely that from 900 to 1000 appointments
will be made. — Sydney D. T., 29/3/’ll.
When Henry Splodge was very young
He joined a small debating club ;
They said he had a silver tongue ;
He thought so too—the silly cub 1
On any night he’d stay up late
Debating any blessed thing;
A trusted Minister of State
He yearned to be and serve his king.
And so he talked till all was blue.
The members of his wretched lodge,
Friends, relatives and fm-men, too,
The victims were of Henry Splodge.
liis great ambitious shrank as he
Drew nearer to his middle age,
And wrapped up pounds of soap and tea
To earn his grocer’s weekly wage.
No longer did he look afar ;
This spacious land his daydreams pent;
He saw H. Splodge the M.H.il.
And idol of a continent.
He gave three Heps, a helping hand
By talking at a dreadful rate ;
Then stood himself, got beaten, and
Decided he would try the State.
But down his fine air-castles fell;
He ranged himself with Wade, V.C.;
But Wade went out and Splodge said, “II I■' ’
And slammed and d d his pounds of lea.
His hair was getting grey and thin
When Splodge around his suburb lore,
And tried ratepayers’ votes to win
As municipal councillor.
He did not gain the longed-for right
To wrangle madly with a mayor ;
Thereafter he was full of spite—
A bitter man with falling hair.
But “ While there’s life”—you know the part ;
God bless eternal-springing Hope'.
Boor Henry Splodge again took heart
Whilst wrapping up his endless soap.
He pulled the wures (I understand
That, scattered round, there are such things);
He hurried up and down the land
And pulled the wir> s and jerked the strings.
Then things like semaphores began
To move when trains are nearly due ;
Along the line the signal ran,
But Henry Splodge he simply flew.
At last the honor came— J.P. !. . .
For bits of string Splodge had to grope ;
He wept a little in the tea,
Then calmed his nerves by wrapping soap.

Provident Fund )
This is the “Barrack-street bank.” Nominally it
isn’t a State institution; but in the event of trouble
it is well known that the State would stand behind
it, as it did in the early ’9o’s, when George Dibbs
scrawled his famous “I promise to pay,” and stuck
it on the front door. Its president is the State
Governor, and the trustees are nominated by the
Government. Apparently, by the way, it is a rather
fatal thing for a politician to be appointed a trustee ;
for the Board includes Tommy Jessep, John Kidd,
J. L. Fegan, and O. W. Oakes, all of whom have
lost the number of their Parliamentary mess, leaving
only Arthur Griffith, Cocks and Grahame to repre-
sent sitting members.
There is a State savings bank proper—formerly
worked in conjunction with the post office, and now
extended to include the Agricultural Bank, or Ad-
vances to Settlers Department. It iB a waste, of
course, to have these two concerns instead of one;
but some of the people interested in this Barrack-
street bank have hitherto blocked amalgamation,
mainly on the ground that this iB a Private Enter-
prise concern —which it only is in name—“and be-
cause it owns a reserve fund of nearly £400,000.
A reserve fund of £400,000 is handy, no doubt; but
it would be a great deal better to have as a reserve
fund the whole of the resources of the State —and
that’s what it would amount to if the State took it
over. In the same way there used to be two Savings
Banks in Victoria; but they got over the difficulties
there, and now there is only one—and a very fine
one it is. Yet, while there are two banks in N. S.
Wales, both actually State-guaranteed, far less is
being done to encourage thrift than should be
the case. The old idea was simply to take care of
the people’s savings, lodged in small amounts, and
pay reasonable interest on the total. But the State
can help people to be thrifty by spending as well as
by saving. It recognised that fact when it under-
took the advances to settlers business; and every
State in Australia does it now. But while it is well
to help the man in the country to get his own farm
and homestead, it is equally well to help the man
in the city to get his home. S. Australia has been
doing this for years; and Victoria lately went the
same way. N. "S. Wales is, however, doing nothing
for the city dweller. In S. Australia the city artisan
can get a home of his own by paying £7 12s. Bd.
p.c. p.a. for 20 years. So if the place costs £5OO,
the man pays 15s. per week for 20 years, and the
home is then his, and the landlord is got rid of
for all time. Or by paying larger instalments he can
make the place his own in a shorter period. There
is no reason whatever why the Savings Banks of
N.S.W. shouldn’t bring the same advantages to the
people of Sydney and other established centres.
In 1900 the total amount to the credit of de-
positors with this bank was £4,855,760. The growth
more recently has been as follows: —
1908 £6,686,508
1909 6,847,154
1910 7,263,104
About two-thirds of the funds are in N.S.W. Govern-
ment and municipal securities. A total of £1,049,945
is out on mortgage, as against £1,117,168 at the
end of the previous year. It may be that the bank
finds there is keener competition for mortgages,
and prefers to let somebody else take the risk at
the high levels of value ruling for freeholds. The
balance of the funds, except as to £85,849 invested
in premises, is represented by deposits with various
banks of issue—a very poor use to which to put
over a million of money. The bank itself ought to
be using it, instead of passing on the job of finding
work for it to the private banks.

In addition to the assurance fund shown above, the
co. claims £3240, an amount by which assets were
formerly written up, and there is also £lO,OOO of
shareholders’ capital. The written-up assets consist
of freehold property which the co. bought at a bar-
gain price. The capital carries a 5 p.c. dividend.
Hitherto the business has been entirely Industrial;
but recently Ordinary tables were issued. The co.
undertakes to pay to the policy-holders in this latter
division at least 90 p.c. of the profits. That .means
that the co. proposes to take 10 p.c. No objection
could be laid against that, if the co. guaranteed
the Ordinary policy-holders to do their business at
a certain maximum rate. In that case, the bargain
would be quite fair. If, however, the co. takes 10
p.c. and doesn’t guarantee anything, the arrangement
isn’t as satisfactory as it might be. If a co. under-
takes to do the policy-holders’ business at a certain
rate or less, and pay any excess out of the share-
holders’ funds, there is a pretty fair guarantee
of economical management. But if there is no
guarantee, it may pay those who run the concern
to take big salaries instead of striving to make pro-
fits of which they take only 10 p.c. A guarantee
should, therefore, be introduced.
In setting out after Ordinary business, the, co. pro-
poses, apparently, to tickle the public s palate with
deferred bonuses" —it “appeared to be the most suit-
able form of assurance for Australasia.” It may
appeal to some ; at the same time the system opened
up the way to such abuses that the U.S. have shut
down on it. In a few words, the System means that)
instead of the bonus being added year by year to
the sum insured, the profit or surplus is set aside,
and if a policy-holder dies before his expectation
period has expired, he gets ho profit, whatever; In
other words, the insurer under this piafi backs him-
Sclf to live longer thart the average. It. is d sort of
half-way insurance. On the one hand, it is claim 1
that the best lives ieceive the greatest benefit .
True. On the other hand, those whd die. soonest,
and have thus had the poorest opportunity of making
other provisiori for their dependents, get the leaSt
consideratipii. It is a plan for further fattening the
pig that haS had the best chance of getting fat
Otherwise, and of fattening him at the expense bf
those who have had the poorest chance.
So far the co. does not distinguish in the Revenue
account between Ordinary and Industrial premiums;
nor are the expenses kept separate. It is, therefore,
impossible to say what the expense rates are. It
ought to be compulsory for every office to show
these things separate. In the bulk the expenses (in-
cluding dividend) amounted last year to over 63
p.c. of the total income. Seeing that this includes
some Ordinary business, the expense rate for the
Industrial business must be simply prodigious. As
far as one can make out, of. every Is. paid for insur-
ance by the Industrial policy-holders, about 9d. goes
■ in expenses. It is most unsatisfactory.

In the days of the boom trustee CoS. sprang up ill
Victoria like mushrooms and paper millionaires.
Among them were these three —-the Colonial Per-
manent, the Australasian Natives’, and the Union,
The cost of administering the three was too great
for the good of anybody—-shareholders or clients—
so a unification was agreed upon ; and Alfred Deakin
not only took part in bringing it about, but became
a director after it had been accomplished, PreSuirl-
ably he didn’t tell the shareholders of the three Cds.
on that occasion that, if they handed their affairs over
to one Commonwealth, they would be robbed and
ruined. As far as can be gathered, he didn’t remind
them that they were passing over a signed cheque
for somebody else to fill in. It was a business
matter, and although Alfred D. never was much of
a business man, he knew too much to insult the
shareholders with nonsense of that sort. It is only
when he is talking to electors that he can venture
upon it. Anyway, the unification got to work, and
there hasn’t been any visible robbery, and the gradu-
ally-increasing dividend shows that there hasn’t
been any ruin. It was heavily handicapped, too. It
started off with a much bigger capital than was
absolutely necessary, and a large part of it was in-
vested in mortgages; and the co. which invested in
mortgages in the early ’9o’s usually invested in
trouble. Fifteen years ago (1896) it still had
£32,451 (book value) of these assets. No doubt
that item had to be written down severely out of
profits; and the dividend was consequently very small
for a long while. Gradually, however, the mortgages
were got rid of, and the money put into other
assets, mostly debentures, Government stock, deposits,
and Collins-street property. This shows the move-
ment in the 15 years:—
1896. 1911.
Liabilities .. .. £132 £9,235
Public securities .. 32,667 33,288
Mortgages .. •• 32,451 8,692
Freehold .. .. 27,632
Deposits, cash, etc. .. 10,044 23,806
The liabilities are mostly mortgage on the Collins-
street freehold. The position, it will be seen, has
been much strengthened, the shrinkable mortgages of
the early ’9o’s having been replaced by Collins-
street freehold and cash (fixed deposits). The co.
does not furnish information as to the value of the
estates in hand or the growth of the business. For
the first time in this year it does, however, say that
the commissions taken credit for are “exclusive of
commissions not wholly earned, after providing for
contingencies.” This, no doubt, means that credit is
taken for only a part of the corpus commission—
the lump commission on the capital value of estates
which have come into the co.’s hands for adminis-
tration. This is the proper practice. Moreover,
mention of provision for contingencies seems to
imply that the co. has another reserve fund up its
sleeve. Altogether, the position is probably quite as
good as it looks. The co. has a paid capital of
£73,822, in shares paid to £l, with an ordinary
liability of 10s.. and a further liability of £1 only
in the event of liquidation—the last a very good ad-
ditional safeguard for clients. Business is carried on
in Queensland as well as in Victoria.

Fortune is still on the side of Wright, Heaton and
Co. The chief factor in producing the wonderfully
fine figures is no doubt the great prosperity in the
pastoral industry. Once upon a time the co. was
only a carrier of other people’s goods; nowadays it
is in a large mercantile way too. It brings in
station produce, and it carries out stores, and it
makes a profit on the stores as well as on the cart-
age. It handles produce also —buys in the country
and sells mostly in the city. In Victoria it carries
on much the same business, trading as the McCulloch
Carrying Co.; the latter is a separate concern, but
Wright, Heaton and Co. hold all the shares. For
many years W., H. and Co.’s capital was £70,000,
in 28,000 shares fully paid to 505.; by the re-
cent issue of 4000 bonus shares the capital was raised
to £BO,OOO. The profit put to reserves during the
past few years has been very substantial —more sub-
stantial even than the reserves column in the table
would suggest, because large sums have been trans-
ferred to a contingency reserve and written off. This,
perhaps, shows the position better: —

The reserved, it wiil be noted, are described as
nominal, this qualification being called for because
among the assets there are Various paper items,
chiefly goodwill. Irt the earlier accounts the precise
amount of this “paper” waS not discloSed ; but Since
the stock was listed oil the Exchanges and largely
dealt in, the managemerit haS gofle further lrito de-
tail. Apparently, the poSitidri with regard to this
matter is as follows:—-
Wunderlich's goodwill .. .. •• £38;500

Potai goodwill .. .. £65,702
There will thus have tb. be a further accumulatibri of
profits to the exteiit bi £39,053, before the whole
Capital of £ 169,591 will be represented by assets
that Can be handled by. any sort of measuring or
weighing appliance yet invented.
The present co. was formed to take over the well-
known Wunderlich business, with headquarters in
Sydney. That business was held by a proprietary
co with some pref. capital; and the holders of the
latter were allotted pref. capital also in the new co.
To those first 27,500 pref. shares 7 p.c. cumulative
—-a further 10,000 were subsequently added at a
premium; and during the past year the trustees of
the co.’s Employees’ Provident Fund, having some
money to invest, were allowed to take 2500 other
shares at par—another illustration pf the singularly
fine regard which the Wunderlich brothers have for
the people who work with them. (That is the way
they would put * it —“with them” , rather than for
them.”) There is thus now £40,000 in pref. capital;
ordinary capital, £122,591; total, £162,591. It is
none too much for the business, which has grown so
rapidly that a pretty large debt has been accumu-
lated :
1909 .. .. £20,627
1910 .. .. 42,092
1911 .. .. 66,193
The money is not represented by anything in par-
ticular —there have been increases all round: in book
debts, premises, plant (a very largely increased
item), stock and work in progress. No doubt this
co., like almost every other one engaged in or in con-
nection with building, has lately had an uncommonly
busv time, and the recent rate of profit isn t likely
to be maintained. On the other hand, under normal
conditions the amount represented by “stock in hand
and work in progress,” £76,174, could be reduced;
and then the liabilities would naturally shrink also.
This consideration excuses the rapid growth of lia-
bilities, which would otherwise be rather alarming.
There is mostly a little nigger on toe fence, cap-
able of making a hole in the melon crop ; the thing
always is to size up toe little nigger. (1) There are
the pref. shares. That nigger is entitled to a first
bite —a very solid 7 p.c. cumulative, too. But the
pref. capital isn’t big in proportion to the total
capital. £40,000 at 7 p.c.= £2BOO a year=a shade
under 1% p.c. on the total capital. That is, the
capital has to earn only 1% p.c. to pay the pref.
div.; all over that is available for toe ordinaries.
(2) The goodwill item is large, and it isn’t being
reduced. Certainly the name is good, and the busi-
ness connection is good. But fashions change; and
the time may easily come when the name of Wunder-
lich won’t cut more ice than the name of Smith.
Take up any old directory, and see how many of the
big names in it have been blotted out, and how few
of the big names of to-day are in toe records of yes-
terday. (3) The whole management, practically, is
in the hands of toe three mortal Wunderlich brothers.
When Rooke and Co.’s Melbourne business was ac-
quired, three other directors were appointed; but
they only constitute a local Board. The managing or
permanent directors are entitled, in addition to
salary, to 5 p.c. of toe net profits after toe pref. divs.
have been paid and 6 p.c. has been provided for
ordinaries, with an additional 5 p.c. on toe profit
in excess of £10,090, provided toe ordinary share-
holders get three times as much over and above 6
p.c. The bonuses amount for the past year to some-
thing like £1750. Of course it isn’t paid if it isn’t
earned; on toe other hand, when it is earned and the
directors get it, toe shareholders can’t get it. Still,
as it is a piece of profit-sharing, this paper certainly
isn’t going to say that toe bonus is money out of toe
shareholders’ pockets.

At the end of 1903 the auditors reported: “From the
official documents examined by us, we are of opinion
that the gas plant, meters, buildings, fittings on loan
and rental, the electric light macninery, mains and
services, fittings, etc., are not of the value set fortli
in the balance-sheet.” The co. had, in other words,
been paying away in dividends money which had not
been earned; it had been distributing capital as
well as profits. Two courses were, then open—to
write off the improperly-distributed capital, or to
restore it out of future profits. The latter course
was decided upon. Hence the large writings-off of
the last few years. The profit thus written-off has
been used mainly to pay off debt instead of restoring
the depreciated plant; this, however, is quite regular.
The essential thing was to retain the profit and not
divide it among shareholders. The position has been
improved as follows: —
Dec., 1903. Dec., 1910.
Liabilities .. .. £25,872 £13,991
Liquid assets .. .. 4,391 5,340
Deficit .. £21,481 £8,651
The co. has thus in seven years paid its dividends,
made such repairs and paid for such extensions as
have been effected, and put itself £12,830 to the
good, mainly by the reduction of debentures. These
debentures have been reduced from £25,000 to
£12,500. There is no way of telling whether plant
has yet been written down to its present value.
Even if that has been done there are no reserves;
and in their absence, the div. on the ordinary shares
should certainly not be increased beyond the present
5 p.c. In fact, it is very- doubtful whether, in view
of past lessons, it should have been put up to 5 p.c.
The capital is £47,537, of which £2750 is in 9
p.c. pref. shares.
The Bank of New Zealand is making its
third change of residence in Melbourne. From Queen-
street it moves into its own freehold premises in
Oollins-street, next the Union Bank.
“Khasuble” : The Clergy Provident funds
of the Victorian Anglican, Presbyterian and Metho-
dist churches are in more or less financial difficulty.
Archbishop Clarke has just- been declaring that the
Anglican Provident Fund was short last year. The
Presbyterian Moderator has declared that their fund
could only pay 90 p.c. The Methodist fund has had
losses, apparently through sanguine, pious, but rather
unfortunate tradesmen borrowing money. It is time
to urge that the regular, secular insurance institu-
tions ought to be trusted by the churches. Clergy-
men have a mischievous idea that they are men of
business, able to do better than the actuary. Be-
sides, these eleemosynary funds are bitter for the poor
supernumeraries, with a lot of hungry pietists watch-
ing them, and wanting to know why they don’t die.
I have heard cruel remarks in the Presbyterian As-
sembly, hinting that the clergy’s widows live too
long!” I have heard a treasurer say: “We have been
quite out of our calculations.” If these people paid
into the A.M.P., or something else of the kind, the
poor creatures could live as long as they liked.
