Professor Richards, in the revised edition of his
celebrated edition of “Ore Dressing,” 1909, devotes
14 pages to the Wilfley concentrating table, and,
among other things remarks (p. 1465): “The first
table sold was installed in the Puzzle Mill, at Breck-
enridge, Colorado. Since then over 12,000 have been
sold. This machine has revolutionised ore-dressing,
and as such is a phenomenal success. The original
form, and forerunner of all modern shaking tables
with longitudinal riffles., has been greatly improv-
ed.” And further on (p. 1475) remarks: “One feature
of the Wilfley table to which especial attention should
be called is its ability to, separate sands from slimes
while making the ordinary concentration. This sep-
aration is more complete and thorough than can be
made by any classifier, and should be taken advant-
age of in designing mills.” In the extensive addi-
tions just made to the Golden Horseshoe Mine (W.A.)
a large number of Wilfley Concentrators have been
installed. The Austral Otis Engineering Company,
Limited, South Melbourne, have the sole manufac-
turing rights.* [1

Broken Hill Junction might have been on
a profit-making basis to-day if the hadn t had a
bee in its bonnet when it discussed J^te
first report on the property. Now lt ai f® e ™ s „ t to ®
to do anything except amalgamate, and that course
is undoubtedly the best for the shareholders As it
stands the old mine isn’t much use to its own share-
holders or anyone else. The Junction North doesn
seem anxious to acquire the right to the encum-
brance, but the directors are willing to take it into
the Junction North fold if the Junction people climb
down from their gingerbread pedestal. It is beyond
the power of any manager to get 2000 tons of pay-
able ore per week out of the mine under existing
conditions, and even if that were possible, the present
mill could not treat it at a profit. It will take
almost as much money as Hebbard suggested m his
first report ( £30,000) to put the mine in working
order, and the directors haven’t a ghost of a chance
of scraping that amount together. Amalgamation is,
therefore, the only refuge. If the Junction North
will hand over 20,000 shares and pay off a portion of
the Junction co.’s liabilities, the shareholders on both
sides should be satisfied.
B.H. Block 10 has got its lode at the
1715 ft. level —the deepest ore yet proved on the
Broken Hill line. But just at the spot where the
crosscut struck it, the sight wasn’t a very satisfy-
ing one for the management. The opening up of
the old levels in the Kelly section —abandoned for
years on account of the underground movements —
promises better results, and this work is being pushed
ahead. The mill is badly in want of some better ore
than it has been getting for months past, and the
requisite “sweetening” is looked for from Kelly’s.
Some first-class stuff was left at the 400 ft. and 500 ft.
levels of the Kelly section when the workings were
abandoned several years ago, and a fair amount of it
should be recoverable now. Kelly’s shaft is being
repaired to allow of work being carried on through
it.
Block 10 has been carrying on exploratory work
at the old Rising Sun, on the White Leads flat, south
of Broken Hill, and has got down to the 600 ft. level.
Ore of good quality, but in very limited quantity,
was found there, and what could be scraped together
was sent to the mill. Diamond-drilling has not
shown the outlook to be encouraging. The Rising
Sun is only a little distance further along than the
South Extended, and the fact that nothing but small,
irregular bodies of ore, with patches of good sul-
phides, has been met with in the Sun, should cause
buyers of South Extended shares at 4s. 9d. to do
some hard thinking.
, * * * *
Bourke’s Hill Tin Sluicing Co. was formed
in Melbourne early in 1906 to work some ground
near Emmaville (N.S.W.). The co. got on the right
side of things in 1909, and started to pay divs. in
strict moderation, and by August of last year £5625
had been paid on a capital of £16,875. In the
past six months the co. has been a teetotaler; the
profit only ran to £348, and £263 of that was
written off for depreciation. The ground was too
hard, and the tin was too scarce, and the water-
supply failed—-there are plenty of excuses. The co.
is now picking up odd scraps of ground in the
struggle to give its shareholders a bit better run for
their money.
* * * *
The strong Melbourne crowd which has
stuck tight to Y Waterholes (N.S.W.) has been
cheered with a first div. of a shilling. The co. put
Moline in charge, and gave him good financial back-
ing, and he beat down obstacle after obstacle. Then,
with a straight run, profit has come fast, and five
weeks’ work showed £3400 to the good.
* * * *
Grafton'copper mine, Cangai (N.S.W.),
is putting a tram line three miles into the bush;
and it is a pretty expensive job. Last year the co.
paid £7749 for firewood used in the treatment of
9231 tons of ore, and the line ought to drag the bill
down by over one-third. Ore reserves are not very
large, probably not more than three years’ supply, if
the furnaces are run to their full capacity. But the
ore is of high grade, the recent average being 11
p.c. as trucked into the hopper. For a run of four
weeks 637 tons ore and 330 tons slag produced 179
tons matte, containing 63 tons copper. Some of the
stopes show a width of 4ft. of black ore and. yellow
sulphides. During 1910 the co. paid £5OOO in divi-
dends, from a production valued at £38,627, which
is a very moderate result considering that the ore is
high grade—from 8 to 10 p.c. —and easy to^treat.
The Sydney syndicate which was formed
to test blocks north of the prospectors’ claim at
Meglo (N.S.W.), has done a lot of costeening along
the supposed lode outcrop, and, failing the discovery
of any appreciable ore-values, has decided not to
exercise its option.
* * * *
Budgery (N.S.W.) is putting up a game
struggle with the copper market. A recent con-
signment of 301 tons, averaging over 6 p.c., re-
turned only £344. Thus, under existing conditions,
the co. doesn’t get much more than £1 per ton
for ore averaging 6 p.c., which is barely enough to
pay for mining. It doesn’t receive more than one-
third of the gross metal value. The copper mine
that has to send its ore any distance to a custom
smelter has a hard row unless the stuff is very
rich. Shipments from Budgery have been now sus-
pended, and prospecting and development work is
to be continued from the 218 ft. level down to 400 ft.
vertical. The main stopes show ore worth about 6
p.c., with promising improvements in one or two
places.
* * * *

Great " Fitzroy (Q.), leaves in search of health;
and J. W. Moule, late metallurgist at Chillagoe,
and for a long time on the staff of the Sulphide
Corporation at Cockle Creek, takes his place.
. Mt. Morgan shells out its usual quarterly
£50,000; but it sets one thinking to find that the

£7O 000 to £BO,OOO per month from 25,000 to

the co. should waste its substance. There are so
many blanks in mining that, when the rare rich mine
ought to pay well, it oughtn’t to be content to only
pay fairly well. The rulers of Mt. Morgan have got
into that frame of mind. “We are doing well
enough,” they say. The whole business wants shak-

When State Premier Denham got to Char-
ters Towers the other day, the dulness made him
sad. Whereupon his thoughts naturally flew to
butter and spuds, and, without being asked, he pro-
mised to have the district overhauled, with a view
to agriculture. Local bodies are asked to co-operate.
Denham points to Gympie and Ballarat, which
turned to butter when their gold-mining sickened.
* * * *
St. Mungo, Cloncurry, has only 10,000
shares, which helps to explain three sixpenny divi-
dends in quick succession; for sixpence all round
only means £250. Shipments of 26 p.c. copper
continue to be made, and it is reckoned that the
quantity at the mine and on its way to the pot
should return 3s. per share ( £1500). The mine is
very patchy, and the rich veins often only a few
inches thick; so a big capitalisation for the property
would be absurd. It is a gamble over which one
might easily get his fingers burned.
* * * *
Officially stated that the last consignment
of blister copper from Mount Elliott, Cloncurry,
was laid down in Townsville at £26 per ton. Just
as well, however, to remember that the value ex-
tracted from 16,585 tons in four months was
£124,844, which means an average of £7 10s. 6d.
per ton of ore; and when that average drops, up will
rush the cost per ton of metal.
* * * *
Golden Horn, a Melbourne-owned mine at
Mount Leyshon—reckons that its costs will be nearer
ldwt. than the 2dwt. mentioned last week. If that
figure can be realised, the co. will be doing good
work. For the first fortnight in March the co.
treated 1280 short tons for a recovery of a shade
under 7s. per ton, and 1150 tons in the second fort-
night for 7s. 7d. per ton; and it estimated a profit
of about £4OO for the month —say, 3s. 3d. per short
ton. If the profit works out according to the esti-
mate, the other estimate that ldwt. will cover costs
is pretty near the mark.
* * * •
Times mining correspondent Gerard Wil-
liams knocks the stuffing out of the yarn that Bull-
finch hadn’t any gold to speak of until 40ft. had been
sunk below the surface, and that the barren lodes
in the surrounding delusions are “caps of rich de-
posits that exist below.” Williams says that the
cap of the main ore body was discovered immediately
under the loam, and the ore gave pannings which
looked like averaging lOdwt. to 20dwt. When the
samples were assayed, however, they were found to
be worth as many ounces. Describing a second visit
to the Proprietary mine, Williams says that the foot-
wall of the lode and the main slide must intersect
at a short distance below the 100 ft. level, and this
point may prove to be the downward limit of the
much-advertised rich ore. While even a doubt as to
the permanency and continuity of the bonanza exists,
Williams considers that there should be no delay in
sinking existing shafts, and thus demonstrating the
nature of the unoxidised ore in depth.
It now transpires that the circular issued
to Bullfinch shareholders in London on February 18,
estimated that current ore treatment would give
£lO,OOO to £12,000 net revenue, and not profit, as
alleged at this end. Other things mentioned are that
the treatment of ore on the spot is not to be started
until the character and extent of the sulphides is
determined, and that, as immediate and prospective
financial requirements have been fully provided for,
present profits will be available for dividends.
The Times correspondent’s view of the
Chaffinch: “So far the many costeens on the lease
have given no sign of anything even remotely ap-
proaching to payable gold. Nor, indeed, did I see
anything which had been exposed that appeared even
likely to carry gold. ... I have examined speci-
mens which came from the trench, and the gold is
very similar to the gold in similar hornblende
schists in the North-East Coolgardie goldfield. _ If
such a pocket of rich ore should occur in the midst
of rocks which are very free from hornblende (and
gold), it is a very strange coincidence. If another
such pocket be found in the same rock I shall begin
to believe that the age of miracles is not dead.”
Roe, P.M., not only refused to send Green-
way, Dyason and Bonwick on for trial over the
Chaffinch business, but scarified some of the parties
who were trying to gaol them. “It was not his duty
to criticise the action of certain people in this
gamble,” nevertheless it was “a blot on the mining
history of the State.” Certainly . Dyason and Bon-
wick were bears. That is to they sold in the
expectation of a fall. They believed that the thing
was a swindle, and that it would soon be exposed,
and they backed their belief. There is no offence in
that. The offence is to circulate false reports to
procure a fall. And as there was no evidence that
they did anything of the kind, the Court acquitted
them. They should never have been charged.
Greenfinch (W.A.) seems to have got hold
of something attractive at Boodallin. Costeening
has exposed 18ft. of 3oz. ore, and a good judge in-
forms The Bulletin that the property is the best
surface show he has seen in the West for a long
time. Unfortunately, these good surface things
mostly die young, but maybe the Greenfinch has
fluked one that won’t. #
Kalgoorlie is whooping loudly over the
Golden Horseshoe development at 2800 ft. The lode
over which all the fuss is being made has now been
stabbed four times within a length of a little over
600 ft. First of all it was cut by three bores, drilled,
fan-shaped, from the Boulder main shaft in the
direction of the Horseshoe and Ivanhoe boundaries.
The Horseshoe Co. then put in a fourth hole further
south from the Boulder Co.’s Edwards shaft, and at
this point the ore body is some 20 odd feet inside
Horseshoe ground, and worth 13dwt. for a width of
27ft.
Cumberland (W.A.) is officially stated to
have a few months’ payable ore still in sight, after
which the blinds will probably be pulled down and
the shutters put up. Several new shows are being
sampled, one of which the Board thinks will “turn
out well.” The co. has £7OOO in hand, so if
the worst does happen in the case of the old pro-
perty, the Board need not go short of a feed for
some time.

Ingliston Consols (W.A.) has just strength-
ened the faith of those who believe in the perman-.
ency of Meekatharra by the discovery of first-rate

* * * •
Cassilis (Vic.) has swapped horses again.
The world wonders who gave the kick. If divi-
dends came as fast as managers have been changed
of late, shareholders wouldn’t pull their mouths
down so. Now they merely sigh, and murmur,
“Another man—more plant!” Drummond’s suc-
cessor is A. S. Lilleburne, who has been trying to
infuse vitality into the shrivelled South German
carcase at Maldon. There he couldn’t get a new
plant. At Cassilis the Board is tuned up to such
demands. His lead-off is for a tube-mill, as he
wants fine grinding to get at the slimes. Like every
other man who has happened at Cassilis, he pins
his faith to the slimes, and, like all before him,
he thinks he can raise a halo by cheapening costs.
All the same, it is cheerful to hear him telling
sluggish shareholders that, as there isn’t 12 months’
ore proved, development is the word. This has been the
fault all through. Shareholders wanted divs., when
there was only a narrow reef, and short lenses of
pay ore to treat; while managers have had a tough
problem to keep off calls. Now, with power avail-
able, stone is short in supply, and the grade not so
promising as of old. What the Board has to do is
to reverse old way3—to prove stone, and then take
time to settle up the matter of treatment. Lille-
burne isn’t a novice in metallurgical dodges, and he
did good Westralian work; and as he has had a
poor claim to handle at Maldon, and therefore prob-
ably learned hard economy, he may do the needed
trick at Cassilis.
* * * #
“Al” Dickenson, who hit a nice win back-
ing gee-gees, is wondering if he will do ditto with
his Al at Gaffney’s. Only a perky and persevering
man could have taken up that mountain derelict
after it had been turned down by fatman Miller.
“Dicky,” however, got the Al South slung in as a
make-weight. Preliminary work disclosed a floor
of quartz running in the dyke. Then came a set-
back in shares, on a hint from the men of the moun-
tains that the floor wasn’t anything to talk about.
But it was better than nothing, and “Dicky” has
been wearing his hat at a jauntier angle than ever.
A bulk crushing is to tell the full tale. Will the
sequel be the one so often related in the Woods
Point district —that gold makes in angles and
patches, and that, as the dykes get hard at depth,
the quartz veins become as scarce as winners on a
Cup day?
* * * *
Peter Mcßride is being pelted over the
ukase ordering the Bright dredges to resoil all land
with a surface covering of 2ft. of alluvium. Peter
in this matter is as high and as huffy as a girl in
harem skirts. Everyone except the Sludge Board
knows the impossibility of putting back dredged
land in the same condition as before buckets cut
into it. What is so foolish is that, if miners want
Bright ground, they can huddle and heap it into
mounds worse than Tewksbury’s machines could do
it, and Peter can’t gainsay them, nor can the Sludge
Board whimper. Peter won’t go to Bright to decide
for himself, and Tewksbury won’t go to Peter, so the
snarling is likely to last.
* * * *
Lord Nelson. St. Arnaud, is on the divi-
dend list after a five years’ wait. Stone from which
profit comes is from the once-despised Lord Nelson
North lease, into which the lode dipped, to the con-
fusion of all old-timers. Lord Nelson could have got
the ground for a few pence, but turned up a nose of
disdain. Then the Baillieus walked in, and about
made their own terms. Lord Nelson’s adversity came
with a fault at about 1400 ft., which brought the
average down to about sMwt. • As the gold is low-
grade, and .there was a slab of debt to meet over the
battery, it seemed to be a toss-up if the claim would
ever build up its £254,000 divvy- record any higher.
Though the two shoots of stone have functioned at
about the present depth, there has not come an ap-
preciable widening of the lode to the old aggregate
dimensions; but, to compensate, the shoot of gold is
longer. This is one good point 6f the present posi-
tion. Another is an improvement in the grade at
the bottom level; and a third is the better recovery
obtained. On the other hand, the eo. has now
80,000 shares to spread profits over, and costs won’t
decrease as depth is attained. Finally, shoots of gold
at below 2000 ft. are not often dependable. Still,
with the list of Victorian dividend mines shrinking
so much, the best is hoped for.
* * * *
Scamander Copper (Tas.) is collecting
pennies, to find if there is ore in a long-shepherded
lease near St. Helens. A J. Bull crowd had the first
try, but let go a tired hold after doing a bit of
prospecting tnat would have shamed a lazy grave-
digger. Next, the ’Cootas took a hand, but copper-
mining spells cash, and, as Tassy is hard up, Tom
Husband’s Melbourne crowd, who were sick of
Balfour, married St. Helens to the Scamander.
Work done by previous owners has proved carbon-
ates, a little glance, and more chalcopyrite; and
coin is now wanted to sink below the tunnel, to
prove what lives at depth. This is all-right mining.
If Husband keeps his team in hand, he should in-
sist on going down a fair depth, for you can’t trust
the East Coast’s surface or shallow depths.
* * * *
Mt. Lyell tops up its half-year with 661
tons blister, containing. 653 tons copper, 43,4750 z.
silver and 870 oz. gold. The mine has already sent
away 3932 tons to the refining works, and it looks
as if the half-year to March 31 would close with a
normal production of slightly over 4000 tons of
blister, which, by the way, is about 98 p.c. pure
copper. The big co. has been handling ore of slightly
improved grade recently; and on its big tonnage a
rise or fall of 14 p.c. means in the half-year a dif-
ference of 400 or 500 tons of copper, worth over
£20,000. The average extraction of silver and gold
varies within narrow limits, and seldom exceeds 7s.
per ton of orei Last month’s gold and silver were,
approximately, worth £BOOO, while the copper at
£5O per ton gave £32,650. The mixture of crude
ore smelted at i Mt. Lyell isn’t half as valuable as
that treated at Morgan; but costs have been got
down very fine. With drooping metal markets,
profits are likely to show a slight reduction for the
past term. #
The Tasmanian Government has made a
liberal advance to the Anchor Co. Favored in this
way, it has completed its aerial tram from The Blue
Tier deposits to the mill, a distance of 1% miles.
The Anchor has frequently quoted costs below 3s.
per ton; but the stuff is low-grade, and the co. is
big, and very large quantities of dirt are necessary
to give shareholders the chance of a meal. Hence
the tram line.
* * * *
Taranaki Petroleum Go. (M.L.) has been
boring deeper in its No. 3 well, and at 3680 ft.
has had a great “blow out of gas, oil, water and
silt. It was the biggest explosion m date, and
saturated buildings and fixings so that work was
stopped. When the flow was got under control, 70
barrels of oil were saved in three days. No. 2 bore has
been giving 10 barrels a day for 15 months past. In
contrast with these successes, another oil co. in the
district is in the hands of the bailiff.
* * * *
Waiki (M.L.), whose shares are now
pretty steady at about £3 55., has struck another
new make of quartz in a prospecting drive north of
the Martha; but the first 13ft. passed through gave
no values. It is thought to be a branch of the
Martha, though it has not the same dip.
People who are in the habit , of looking
only at assay values of ore, without considering what
it may cost to get the metals out, ought to consider
the Hercules (Tas.). From that mine 3306 tons of
40 p.c. zinc ore were lately shipped to foreign
markets, the actual metal contents being; Gold,
5450 z.: silver, 32,5100 z.; zinc, 1472 tons. There
was thus £30,000 worth of zinc without the precious
metals; yet the Hercules didn’t get one-tenth of this
amount. In fact, it only got £2563. A ton of
Hercules crude zinc ore contains twice as much zinc
as the Broken Hill tailings, and the cost of mining
and transport to Williamsford is only 12s. Bd. per
ton; but it is the vast tonnage that allows Broken
Hill zinc cos. to get fat while those of Tasmania
starve.

Every engineer in charge of factories and mills
should give close attention to the power wasted in
transmission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller
Bearings save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent,
of oil, so one of the first duties is naturally for
engineers to direct their special attention to these
bearings and to car boxes for trucks. Hyatts are
in continuous use in Cement Works, Ore Dressing
Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bushings of
flexible rollers may be applied to loose pulleys or
truck wheels, etc. Should engineers require further
details, they can apply to the Australian Agents,
W. & J. Lempriere, Melbourne; or E. W. Hurst-
liouse and Co., of Wellington, the agents in New
Zealand.*

“Gip” on peripatetic explosives: —
On this part of Monaro (N.S.W.) we don’t sav
politely, like “Yarrum,” that a horse or beast died
of “internal combustion.” We just say it busted
itself. Like this: —
“Two er Jim Mannix’s bullicks in the crick.”
“U-r-r-r-h?”
Long pause.
“Bogged ?”
“No-00. Busted theirselves.”
The art of busting oneself has always had a vulgar
fascination for me. An old Monaro resident was
telling me the other day of a terrible busting year.
(This, too, by the way, has been rather a remark-
able year for lucerne and busting.) During that
memorable year beasts were busting everywhere;
and the narrator assured me that one evenin’, after
puttin’ the cows up, he just chucked ’em a harmful
o’ dry lucern from the barn — dry lucerri, mind —
and come into supper, an went straight up agen, an’
two of ’em was busted. He said if he could ha’
seen ’em in time he could ha’ saved ’em, because
all as yeh had to do was stick a pocket knife in
their side and get away quick before you was
blown. “Yarrum’s” par. makes me blush for my
coarseness. As he is so inexperienced he may not
mind the suggestion of an equally inexperienced, but
deeply-interested person. The game of “knives,”
practised assiduously, should prove useful. Then,
when next a poor horse comes to expire like a dog
on his lawn —Who left the gate open? Forgive me.
I enquire from habit —a penknife, deftly thrown
from the balcony, might save the life of the suffer-
ing animal. And “Yarrum” would have ample
time to retire behind the French lights before,
boomerang-fashion, his knife returned to him.
RUBBING IT IN.
THE MAN WITH THE B WAG: "Say. Mister, would
you mind playin’ ‘ Home, Sweet Home,’ for
a cove?”
“Jimmie Pannikin”: I have changed my
mind about ignoring “A. William-Cann” ; he s too
interesting, too full of color and conceit, of fact
and fancy and front, of a shallow, superficial salt-
bush-and-sand knowledge, to be regarded as a negli-
gible quantity. His utter disregard of truth so
impinges nis personality upon one’s consciousness,
that it’s easier and better to accept him as a neces-
sary Outback Evil. Neither “Camp Fire’s” scheme
for baffling bugs nor “Gil Gunnia’s” drastic me-
thod of Applying potassium of cyanide would cause
the slightest perturbation of “A. W.-C.’s” equanim-
ity, nor disturb his colossal disregard of the truth.
Note the following items: (1) I have never diva-
gated with “A. W.-O.” Ido not know him. I have
never seen him. “Turramurra Tommy” wrote me
some weeks ago. This is an extract from his letter:
“We had great excitement here lately. A long, at-
tenuated, ungainly, awkward, suburban swagman was
caught robbing the boss’s best bulb beds. He is
evidently one of the street flower-sellers, whose won-
derful wares make patches of glorious color in Mar-
tin-place. The boss,and I booted him off the prem-
ises with great vigor and accuracy. When we
lifted him over the high hedge, he blubbered like' a
baby, and said we would be sorry for our roughness—
that he was not a pilferer of flowers, that he was a
passionate lover of Nature, that he had merely been
getting a few rare specimens to send up to his pal
‘Pannikin’ at the time an inmate of The Walker
Hospital!” Evidently.the fellow is another of the
band who make their alleged acquaintance with me
an excuse for any interference with property or
propriety. Mind, I don’t say the hoisted bloque was
"A. W.-C.” I only note that they both hang on to
“Pannikin,” instead "of going on their own.

“Hecagus” : Agree with “Stan” (B. 16/2/11)
re longevity and egg-laying powers of tne green-
back turtle. Caught one once making an incu-
bator on Bamborough Island (Northumberland
Group). Asa curio-wolf who was with our party
wanted the shell, we dragged the armor-plated lady
aboard the cutter, and took her to camp six miles
away. After the operation was over, the body, minus
the shell, was thrown off the jetty. Fourteen days
later, whilst searching Bamborough Island for a
missing man, we came across the self-same greenie
laying away for all she was worth. On seeing us,
she made a hurried wobble towards the sea, and ex-
pired. It was too much for her.

“Pat O’Maori” : I was delighted with the
gentle tale of “Yalgun” (B. 2/3/’ll) about the 9ft.
carpet snake caught,
in the act of swal-
lowing 6in. of himself
with an eagle-hawk
hanging on. The story
has set me thinking,
and the thoughts I
have had tease me “as
doth eternity.” How
much of himself could
a snake swallow if he
tried ? Fom all ac-
counts, a carpet snake
can swallow things
• much bigger than him-
self ; and, therefore,
there seems no good
reason why he should
not swallow the whole
of himself, except the
part that does the
swallow. If this rea-
soning is sound, will
some Aboliar—“ Yal-
gun” for preference—
tell us what shape a
carpet snake would be
when he has swallowed
the whole of himself
except the part that
can’t be swallowed be-
cause it is too busy ?
I am really anxious to
know about this, be-
cause it seems to have
an intimate connection
with J. Ward’s Maori-
land borrowing policy
and his proposal to borrow enough money to pay
off all the debt in 80 years. If I knew exactly how
much of himself a snake could eat and what shape
the rest of him would be by the time the process
was finished, I should be able to calculate how much
of a loan that loan could pay off, and what form the
Maoriland budget would take after it had done the
trick.
“Pat O’Maori”: In this part of Sydney
Suburbia they drive pigs by night. If you have a
good nose you can hear them half an hour before
they arrive ; after that, if your hearing is acute,
you can smell their voices through the gloom. They
form a noisy procession. At every step each indi-
vidual pig enters his emphatic protest against the
fate which keeps him so long out of bed, and every
now and again one or another will indignantly de-
clare that he won’t go a step further. A cart fol-
lows the swine battalion, and when a grunter de-
clines to continue the march there is a wild uproar,
in which every dog in the street joins. New arri-
vals in the district fancy on these occasions that the
whole population is being murdered by wild Indians
escaped from the picture shows, but in reality it is
only a protesting porker being lifted into the cart.
Local tradition asserts that a petition was once
lodged with the authorities against the slaughter of
slumber involved in these pig parades, but the ap-
peal nearly led to a civil war. A monster. manifesto
was issued by the suburban gardeners declaring that,
if the fertilisation caused by the wreath of swine
were taken away, the economic loss to their ro?e
bushes would destroy half the charm of the locality.
After that the protesting party collapsed ; and it is
now a favorite topic of dispute whether the pigs or
the pianos of Jacaranda Avenue are the more bracing
to the nerves.
“lvobbi”: That nervous swaggie (B.
2/3/’ll) seems to have no faith in vinegar or
Condy’s as a snakebite cure. He ought to get
ready to peg out; for Australian snakes kill more
people by fright than by bite. Ligature-tying is
the most important thing, though death has its own
way at times through the placing of the lig. Where
there are two bones, as on the lower arm or lower
leg. The lig. won’t stop the circulation there.
Get it on the upper arm or leg. The late Dr.
Krefft, the best snake authority we have had, held
that, in the absence of an antidote, it helps, after
lacerating the wound, to bathe it with water or
wine. As to cutting the wound, stick a pin through
the skin where the punctures are; then twist a
string around underneath, thus lifting the skin.
After that, slash across with a sharp knife. If at
your wit’s ends, apply salt. It will hurt; but it is
better to dance than to die.

the Cow, Corn and Creek Country, about the Mighty
and Magnificent Manning. It is a very hard thing to
.find one Australian book in any of the school-libraries
so far inspected. Surely it is quite time that Aus-
lian teachers in Australian schools should teach Aus-
tralian children something of Australian literature
In one big school, where the library contains over
200 books (all un-Australian),'l said: “Give the
names of any Australian writers whom you have
ever heard of,” The first three names mentioned
were Rudvard Kipling, Mrs. Henry Woofi, and Marie
Corelli. When I recovered from my severe fainting
fit, one girl, anxious to placate “Pannikin,” said: “I
know two, sir—Sir Walter Scott and Charles Gar-
vice.” I re-fainted. In the March issue of The
Public Instruction Gazette (called “The Pig” for
short), a senior-inspector remarks 'truly: “Only a
few. teachers remember that there is. .Australian
poetry worthy of attention.” The formation of a
strong, solid society for the compulsory emigration
of all ashamed, apologetic and apathetic• Australians
is. hereby recommended. . . . The .Cow Country is
easy on bird-life, but cruelly hard on child-life; the
sheep-country is the reverse. In all mv divagations
(f hope this word won’t annoy “ANifC’N) I have
never seen so many Willie Wagtails- 'find butcher-,
bird,? ,as there, are along the roads of; the Taree dis-

cheeky Willie Wagtails alongside the road, during a
10-mile drive. . .., It often rains between the
showers: up here. ...
“Cellu Loid”: When Chief Protector
Howard starts out to back the Bananaland or any other
binghi against his Pacific Island brudder, he is. as
the Ruhiana (Solomons) man would sav, talking
“Colo lomosu. Colo lomosu” really means water
that is ever running, rippling and prattling along;
and the Solomons apply the term to continued,
thoughtless talk. I have seen all these black brothers,
and the South Sea Islander—in staving power,
fighting, bushcraft, house-building, canoe-making and
all branches of primitive life—can lick the binghi
blind. In inventing words it is the same. Why, in
one dialect of the South Seas you will find aimost
as many invented or coined words as there are words
in the whole vocabulary of the binghi. When the
Shortland Islander first received the dog to his
bosom he at once named it ow-ow. Then the pig
came along to share the family spud, and was
called ungh ungh. Trousers they call poko tee (poko,
clothes; and tee, a man. The Ruhiana native’s
name for hospital is veato moho (veato, house;
moho, sick). Medicine they call colo moho, i.e.,
sick-water. Matches they name nika pindala (nika,
fire; pindala, one who strikes). Once had a crew
of Island “boys,” who sighted a vessel and informed
me of the fact. I didn’t bother to go out to see the
boat, but asked the boy if it was a steamer. “No.”
Schooner? “No.” Square rig? “No.” Then he
informed me it was a put put. The first few oil-
engine auxiliary vessels taken to the South Seas
were fitted with old-tvpe Hercules engines, and you
would hear the put put, put put for miles on a still
night. This class of vessel is still “put put,” and
will always remain so to the Islander. Taken all
round, the South Sea Islander could sell the binghi
a fish-ball stuffed with sand and make him believe
it was fresh-caught rock cod.
* * * *
“Kobbi”: The question is. Can a man
be fined for “language” if he uses it in his sleep?
At Cairns (Q.), recently, evidence was given by the
constable that, being telephoned for, he presented
himself outside the residence of the language, and re-
mained outside for lOmin., during which time the
zigzag expressions continued to waft themselves about
the neighborhood. Whereupon he entered and ar-
rested the language reservoir, and had to wake it
up to do so. The accused pleaded: “Muster been
talking in my sleep.” Case dismissed.

“The Nipper” : Bill—you know Bill —
lately had a nerve-destroying encounter with a scrub
turkey, which he tree’d on the Dawson. Bill told
me about it this way: “I was out with the ole gun,
when I spots the bird a-sittin’ on a limb about Sft.
off the groun’. I sneaks on to ’im, an’ lets fly.
talcin’ no charnst, mine yer, bein’ meat-’ungry. I
blew feathers off ’im, but ’e never shifted a hinch.
Then I gives ’im the magazine, but got nothin’ but
feathers. Bein’ natorally a bad-tempered cuss, I
fires my belt full at ’im. Gorblime, there e’ was still
a-sittin’ on the limb, with not a blarsted feather on
’im.”
* * * *
“J. W. Flanagan,” concerning an Austra-
lian abroad: —
Many of the Western States of America are paying
marked attention to the Australian gum-tree, par-
Southern California, where it is being cul-
tivated to great advantage. Besides being planted
for shade in the streets and parks, great tracts of
land are being devoted to its cultivation for com-
mercial purposes. The tree grows surprisingly fast,
and is usually very straight and tall. As firewood its
corpse brings the highest price in the market —I
have known it to be as much as 16 dollars a cord
in Los Angeles. The timber is also extensively used
in furniture-making, and some of the most stylish
chairs I ever saw were made out of it. The wood
polishes beautifully, and it has a lustre and grain
which even mahogany does not surpass. Strange to
say the manufacture of eucalyptus oil has not yet
been gone into any great extent; a druggist told
me that most of the oil used in America is Austra-
lian, but imported via Germany.

“Yalgun”: “Marloo” (B. 23/2/T1) resur-
rects an old myth when he speaks of pigii
cutting their throats when swimming. I
have repeatedly seen swine taking aquatic
exercises. Knew a “cocky” at Bexhill, Rich-
mond River (N.S.W.), who had to fence
along the river side of his pig run to prevent
gninters swimming across and helping themselves
to young corn on the opposite bank. At a spot
where the punt crosses the Orara River, passengers
often * saw numbers of pigs swimming among the
river weeds, and regaling themselves on the same.
Never knew one pig to have his throat cut, in either
case, except by the local slaughtermen.
RESTLESS NIGHTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The first faint fowl sighs sadly, meek and mild,
By distant arc-lights plaintively beguiled
To thoughts of dawn. The feline on the tiles
Steals to the trysting, full of feline smiles,
And bursts into wild laughter, shortly, when
I’m just about to fall asleep again.
The clock strikes two. In language strong and terse
I put my bitter thoughts into a curse
Upon these restless nights, when I must sprawl
Wide-eyed, and count the clock-ticks as they call
Their cackling promise of a crimson dawn,
When I shall rise, at last, and mow the lawn.
A far, sad cow lifts up her voice and cries,
And, full of helpless rage, I curse her eyes ;
I sit up in the dark and firmly damn
All cows that cry by night or day, and slam
My pillow hard upon its rocky bed,
Whence long that gentle angel, Sleep, hath fled.
Oh, blow the moments, flopping clumsily
Into oblivion, leaving sleepless me
To hear John Dunn clump slowly down his beat;
To hear the late sport’s heavy, tangled feet
Go by ; to lie and wait until the morn
Brings to my door an Argus, newly born!
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
THE MAJOR PORTION.
[Foe The Bulletin.]
A prominent permanent Rechabite informed the
cold-water conference in Melbourne that there were
22,000 youths in that city alone, whose haunts were
the street corners, the dancing hells and the bars.
“In consideration of this shocking juvenile deprav-
ity,” said the permanent Rechabite, “all Rechabites
should bestir themselves.” It was cordially (tem-
perance cordials) agreed that those 22,000 youths
must be dragged into the Rechabite tents. If that
were done, the major portion of political power
would soon rest with the temperance party.
Young men who'on the city street
Your daring deeds recite,
Or dance on trim, ungodly feet
At vulgar, hops by night,
Take heed, be hasty to repent,
And swarm in Brother Manger’s tent.
Forsake the pubs’ pernicious beer,
And cut the dancing out;
Drink water in the tents, and hear
The whales of temp’rance spout.
Their talk is neither smart nor new,
But, oh, it is so good for you !
Devote yourselves to water-pots,
Get only water-tight.
Boss Rechabites will teach you what’s
To set the country right.
It can be righted in an hour
By utilising water-power.
When all our youths are Rechabites
No Parliament will know
The politician who invites
Fell whisky’s vicious glow.
Praise God, the power to rule all men
Will be the Mauger portion then !
victoria.

THE DOGS OF LAW.
“Geofred” : Various Aboliars have at dif-
ferent times claimed superior sagacity for various
breeds of dog; but I believe politicians can be as
sagacious as poodles. I have three recent instances
in mind. First case: Jack West, the present Labor
M.H.R. for East Sydney, had been for a couple of
elections making the pace willing for G. 11. Reid,
with the result that the same G. Reid didn’t wait
for the 1910 elections, but got in out of the wet by
accepting the High Commissionership in London.
In 1910 West won the East Sydney seat easily.
Second: Kidston’s position as junior member for
Rockhampton began to play shaky, so Wullie gave
himself a billet on the Prickly Pear Board. Now
the Kidder’s seat has been won for Labor Third:
Newton Moore, who was the member for Bunburv
(and incidentally Premier), resigned to become
Agent-General for W.A. And Labor has likewise
won the Bunburv seat. I don’t like dogs getting
credit for all the sagacity.
“Wang” ; Does any Abo. know a higher price
paid for a dingo corpse than £44? I knew one that
brought that much. He was a huge white beast,
in himself a curiosity, as I understand that albino
dingoes are rare, and he frequented the districts
between the Barcoo and Thomson Rivers, near the
junction of the two. His weakness was a sheep
diet, and four mutton-proprietors offered a reward
of £lO a-piece for a permanent cure of the habit.
Subsequently the price was raised to £ll each, and
‘ Old Snowy,” as he was called, was knocked down
at that.
* * * *
“H.J.D.”: The curious thing about the
gidyea, gidya or gidgee, is that, though the leaves
exude *a scent like an old corpse, more particularly
before the coming of rain, the wood, when polished,
has an extremely agreeable smell, very like raspberry
jam. In fact, it is known in Westralia under that
name. , On account of the high polish it will take,
the wood should have a high commercial value in the
future for veneers. By the way, talking of native
timbers, can anybody tell me whether the emu apple
tree can be transplanted ? It makes a fine shade;
but, from all I can learn, it throws a- “seven” if
you try to shift it from its original surroundings.
“Augustus Blowfly” tells the story of how
wily Konstantin avoids bad debts :
“You talka da strong man!” cried Konstantin—-
“Jacka d’ Johnst,’ Ilackenschmitta—bah! I tell you
stronger man than heem live all day in da room be-
hind da ovst. Mad Raff we call heem —ver’
strong, ver’ dangerous. Once Spiro throwa da oyst’
at heem in fun. Mad Raff he throwa da till at Spiro,
breaks da front teeth, spoila da cooking for weeks.
Another time Mad Raff take-a da oyst’ knife to fat
poleece, and ver’ near m Yes’day drunk sailor
order da fry: we bringa da fry an’ drunk sailor say
bad fry, no pay, fighta da crowd. So Spira an’
George take chairs to drunk sailor, catcha da punch-
on-da-nose, fall over, break seex plate— ver’ bad bus’
ness. Then I take-a da oyst’ knife to drunk sailor,
drunk sailor wipe me over face with cray’—spoil
Cray’, break seex more plates —teerible bad bus’ness.
All sit on floor, call-a Mad Raff ; he no come. Drunk
sailor say: ‘By cripe, / fetch heem!’ rush to liddle
door, Mad Raff come out, fall on top sailor, stun
him. When Mad Raff see he stunned, he catcha da
scruff and kicka-da-behind up, down shop two, t’ree,
seex time; then heave-a to pave’ and run like mad
to pleece station. Very strong, Mad Raff—ver’
dangerous, yiss! T’ank you—one-an-seex.”
“Yarrum”: At Araluen (N.S.W.) a neigh-
bor of mine, a retired bushranger, took to rolling on
his lawn of mornings. At first I thought that re-
morse for the chances of robbery he had missed
had eaten into his brain; so I seriously thought
of laying an information and having him removed;
but he took me into his confidence one night over a
game of billiards. He explained that he rolled
daily for the same reason that a horse does—that it
is Nature’s way of removing inward obstructions.
All his people, it seemed, were subject to and died
of appendicitis; and he was in “mortal dread of it.”
The lawn was a sloping affair, and the rolling
’ranger used frequently to bump the bottom fence
hard. That is probably why he died, before I left,
of cancer in the stomach. Still he escaped the
appendicitis.
“Maple” : Saw a queer sight the other
day. Avery dead, full-grown, whip-tail ’roo in the
fork of a tall apple tree, with two black eagles in-
vestigating its contents. Can any Aboliar tell me
if whip-tails fly, or if eagles indulge in weight-lifting
contests? It seemed to me like a Hell-sent allegory
of the colored agony dissecting Australia, who had
been killed while waiting for her kid soldiers to
grow.
* * * *
“Wonga”: Concerning the water-hyacinth,
which is, beyond doubt, spreading rapidly on the
northern coastal rivers of N. S. Wales, “Itan” (B.
16/3/’ll) suggests a sub-department to deal with
that curse, and likewise prickly-
pear, blackberry, etc. What’s
the use? There is plenty of
power now to deal with these
things, but the same old trouble
crops up every time. When an
attempt is made to have any
particular curse poleaxed, the
“backbone of the country,” es-
pecially the squatting joints of
it, endows the curse with all
sorts of virtues. That is, of
course, the moment the land-
owners think they are to part
with some lucre to eradicate it.
Pretty well every noxious weed
in the country thus finds a bar-
racker—prickly-pear, star thistle,
Patterson’s Curse, melon vine,
sweet briar and the rest. They
all turn up as “capital fodder
for stock in drought time” and
so on. The truth is that stock
will then try and eat any old
thing, however noxious. They
have been known to wrestle with
a copy of an Anti-Sosh news-
paper. Consequently, there is
probably no country in the world
with so much good soil covered
with so much bad rubbish as
Australia, and it’s getting worse.
The toughest feature is the way
the different weeds are getting
from one district into another.
Travelling stock is mostly to
blame for this. Cookies will take
a mob of sheep into a good pad-
dock for a night or two for the
sake of a few shillings, and get
it filled up with some new weed.
Patterson’s Curse is a case in
point. Starting on the Upper
Murray, it is working steadily
down past Wagga. Last summer
writer noticed a neat, purple
patch in a clean holding miles
ahead of any other sign of the
pest. It told its own story:
travelling sheep camped for a
night. It was, when seen, in
full bloom; and next year it
will be over twice as much coun-
try. “Gwy’s” idea of making oc-
cupiers destroy these noxious
weeds is nothing new. That
power lies with the Shire Coun-
cils now; but when it is pro-
posed to so deal with the pest,
friends spring up for it whole-
sale. Later on it is agreed that
the “cost of eradication would
be too heavy a burden on pro-
perty-owners,” and the thing is
let slide. That’s the short and
true history of most curses in
most districts in the Ma State,
anyhow.
“T. J.M.” asks :
How’s this for toughness? Shot a black snake
with a Winchester rifle, less than 10yds. from me.
It dislocated his locomotive gear, and I finished him
per stick. Got the surprise of my life, on turning
him over, to find the bullet had not gone through
him. His elastic belly had stopped it.
The Bulletin reckons that the tale matches the
belly.
* * * *
“W.C.M.”,: I spent one Sunday last
spring watching a co-operative building society.
The shareholders were 12 black magpies, who had
taken up selections in various trees near the Mur-
rumbidgee. They set to work to build one nest at
a time, bringing mud and other materials, while a
pair sat on a close handy tree and seemed to boss
operations. Perhaps it was a wedding gift from the
local choral society, or they were the winners in the
Magpie Star-Bowkett. Anyway, by sundown the
pair had shifted in and the missus started straight
away in her own particular line of business. A few
days later half a dozen nests were built, and I sup-
pose they were all done the same way. Can other
Abo. writers give similar instances? I’m prepared
to swear to this before three Jay Pees —including
“Jimmie Pannikin.”
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany. '
Demos: No; there is no plural voting;

think they will do most good—for the electorate
where they have an office, or for the other one where
they have a home. .. . J. 5.8.: It is a painfully bald

THE ST. SINGER.
A wealthy bachelor was seated before a fire
In an hotel sitting room,
When the sound of a woman singing
Wafted out thro’ the still night gloom;
Marble Halls was the song she sung
In the face of the drifting sleet,
And the bachelor listened with a saddened heart
To the poor singer in the street.
He opened the window of his cosy room,
And gazed down at the singer Below,
Whom sweetly rendered her pathetic song
And fell prostrate upon the snow.
He hurried down the stairs of the grand hotel
Where the lights did glitter and glare,
And made his way to the homeless girl
Shut out in the freezing air;
Fascinated by the outcast’s charms
He rescued her from her piteous life,
Procured her comforts in the grand hotel
And made his lawful Wife.

“Mimosa”: If “Bush Botanist’s” (B.
9/3/Tl) knowledge of plant life is not more acute
than his palate, he cannot pose as an Abo. authority.
He wrongly describes the common “bosch” or “bush
tea” plant of S’Africa as a species of Leptospernum.
The common bush tea referred to by him is sold
throughout S’Africa by storekeepers at a couple of
pence per lb., and is gathered by natives and
“poor whites” from three various species of Cassia.
Cassia leaves are the good 3 out of which senna tea is
made. If “Bush Botanist” had stated that he had
tasted tea made from “Gwari” leaves, which is an aro-
matic beverage, he would have been nearer the mark.
The “Gwari,” which is a large tree, is, not, how
ever, a species of Leptospernum.

Trade Mark Case.
A WARNING.
It having come under our notice that various
substitutes are sometimes sold and passed off
as 0.T,, notice is hereby given that this practice
is a breach of the Trade Marks Act, and con-
sequently illegal, as O.T. is a Registered Trade
Name, and applies only to the beverage made by
this Company.
After this date all persons who so continue to
pass off other drinks as O.T. will render them-
selves liable to proceedings under the “Common-
wealth Trades Marks Act.”
O.T. LIMITED,
F. M alvin Quinn,
March 1, 1911. Acting Managing Directo*
PERSONAL
ITEMS
Mrs. Piercy, the wife of a South Coast
(N.S.W.) doctor, has successfully promoted the
first Woman’s Rifle Club in Australia. The mem-
bers were presented to his Ex. Chelmsford when
he recently motored through that corner of the
State.
The Longmans-Green London publish-
ing house has a book in hand, “British Dominions:
Their Present Commercial and Industrial Condi-
tion.” Professor Ashley, of Birmingham Uni-
versity is the editor, and George Reid, as the
principal Australian contributor, will no doubt ex-
plain what a blessing the tiger of Sosh has been
to the Commonwealth.
GEORGE WHELCH, CHAMPION
SCULLER (M.L.).
Thomas Ballantyne, an old identity of
Maoriland, who died in that much-mortgaged
country recently, was the man who gave Richard
John Seddon his first job in the Islands. Ballan-
tyne emigrated from Londonderry to Melbourne
in 1840, and thence passed to the M.L. West
Coast gold rushes, where he became a contractor.
He was constructing what was known as the Big
Dam at Kumara when young Seddon “tapped
him for a billet,” and was started with a shovel ;
and so well did he perform with the instrument
that the boss was never quite reconciled to the
change “Dick” made in professions. To a
statement one time made in his hearing that
“Seddon was the greatest statesman M.L. ever
had,” Ballantyne replied : “ Maybe ; but he had
the makings of a great pick-and-shovel man.”
When Cardinal Moran first
arrived in Australia he was a
“ bigoted ” teetotaler, and merci-
less to anyone under his
authority who indulged to excess.
In the battling veteran’s early
days in N. S. Wales, while
travelling by coach in the Back-
blocks, he found himself seated
between the driver and a jovial
priest, who, while the coach was
descending a steep hill, was
asked by the Jehu to put his foot
on the brake. This his Reverence
did, but the wry face he made in
doing it was noticed by his
ascetic superior. “ What is the
matter, Father MacKinahan ? ”
coolly inquired the Archbishop.
“Oh, your Grace, Pm greatly
troubled wid rheumatism, and
me fut’s very sore this
mornin’.” “Father MacKina-
han,” suggested the Archbishop
icily, “ perhaps if you took a little
less whisky at dinner you might
have a good deal less gout.”
“Ah !” expostulated the priest,
“ it’s rheumatic gout I’ve got—if
your Grace had to get up on a
cold midnight to ride 20 miles
to a sick-call and swim creeks,
perhaps you might be glad to take
a little something to warm your
heart.” “Father,” continued" Dr.
Moran, in a conclusive but
more kindly tone, “let us
hope there will be no gout in
Heaven.” ‘‘Perhaps there won’t, your Grace,”
returned Father Mac; “ but, anyhow, I hope
there’ll be a lot more sympathy for it.” In cleri-
cal circles it is understood that there is much
more sympathy even on earth for “ rheumatic ”
gout than when the Cardinal first came, and the
bhoys have hope that, when his Eminence reaches
his 90th birthday, he will greet them at Manly
with a two-fingered blessing and a three-fingered
nip.
Editor H. E. Boote, of Brisbane Worker,
has quitted that office to join the staff of Sydney
Worker. As a writer about Arcadias, Boote has
very few rivals; but, as he once mournfully re-
marked, wehave to come down from the Delectable
Mountains to scramble for our dinner.
Oom Bill Lyne, back from America, looks
more than ever like uncle Paul Kruger. Which
was the “slimmer” of the pair History only can
decide. Bill has had a hearty welcome back, by
reason of his reputation as one matchlessly loyal
to his personal friends. Such loyalty was preached
as a creed in Oom Bill’s beloved roost, the Sydney
Warrigal Club. “It’s easy to stick to your
friends when they’re right,” old Jack Robertson
used to say, “but give me the man who’ll stick
to me when I’m wrong.”
“Locust” : Travelled from Cairns a year
ago in the wrecked Yongala. We had a cyclone
about the place where the vessel is supposed to
have been wrecked, and a better-behaved ship it
would be impossible to have under you. Captain
“Bill ” Knight was a fine character. He would
tell you his adventures, which were voluminous,
from his start before the mast, and down to and
including two very serious operations in Mel-
bourne. He escaped the perils of the operating
tables only to go down with his ship. There was
a cat on board named Tom Yongala. The animal
was reported to have an uncanny sense of bad
weather, and would drop off at intervals and stay
ashore for a trip or so. Generally when Tom was
absent the ship was in for a rough time. One
can imagine Tom standing on the wharf, a lonely,
desolate tabby waiting for the ship that never
returned. I wonder if any of the Adelaide S,S.
Co.’s wharf people can tell us what happened to
Tom Yongala !
Entry in Time’s duty book last week :
“To report for duty with Maoriland agency in
Eternity, George Hart.” Fifty-seven years ago,
as a youth, George Hart was apprenticed to learn
the compositor’s trade in the office of the old
Canterbury (M.L.) Standard. At the end of his
time (1859) he crossed to Melbourne and “snatched
stamps ”on Bell’s Life. Like so many comps.,
he early set his mind on the literary side of the
newspaper business, and promptly snapped an
opportunity offered to join the Ovens and
Murray Advertiser as a reporter. From there
he returned to M.L., and spent the eight following
years alternately as compositor and reporter. In
1870 he got a berth on the Christchurch Press as
a reporter, and worked to the position of chief of
staff, which he held when his last assignment was
booked. An enraged and important politician
once exploded to the writer, after having been
scalded by the Press, thus: “S’help me, if it
wasn’t for my regard for old George Hart, I’d
well the rotten rag ! ” It was
a high compliment in the circumstances.
The Independent Cable service told the
other day that John N. Longden, Australian min-
ing engineer, had died in misery, possibly of sheer
starvation, in New York. To those who knew
the honest and generous Longden in other days,
this news came as a stab, for he was a man among
men, and loved on every mining field which ever
felt his steps. He left Ballarat more than 30
years ago while in the prime of life, and must
have been 65 years old when he passed out. The
present writer, who had known Longden in the
Far North, last met him in London. That was
before the days of cyanide, and he was then en-
deavoring to perfect a process based on the idea
of passing finely-powdered refractory ore through
heated mercury. This scheme, which was re-
ported on by the pallid and ghost-like Prof.
Crookes, of radiometer fame, came to nothing ;
but Longden, who in the course of his career had
been associated with the invention of many in-
genious and useful mining appliances, worked on,
and ultimately found his way to America, from
which country his Australian friends had heard
nothing of him for many years.
THE COUPON CRAZE.
ST. PETER: “ What’s this you’ve brought?”
APPLICANT: “Fifty thousand coupons for a seat
in Paradise.”
The late Speaker “ Joey ” Bell’s toughest
c , , • IAAO i , •• .
fight was in 1902, when he was up against David
Tone Dillon, barrister, and then editor of the
Dalby (Q.) Herald. Dillon arrived in Dalby in
1900, with the suppressed intention of capturing
the seat for Labor, and during a year or two
flayed Bell for neglecting his constituency. A
vitriolic newspaper war ensued, Bell having con-
trol of the columns of the Record, a rival local
journal. The climax came at the elections in
1902, when Bell found himself opposed by Dillon,
In those days there was plural voting, and the
franchise had not been extended to women. The
counting of votes occupied several days, during
which Bell and Dillon were alternately on top,
and Bell finally led by a dozen votes. That
shaking-up undoubtedly made Bell a better man
and member. Dillon didn’t try again ; his wife’s
ill-health drove him to the dry air of inland, and
nowadays he edits the Inverell (N.S.W.) Argus.

sft. sin. high, and his expanded chest measures
44in. It looks like a record.
There is a little Melbourne schoolgirl to whom
the sea has been tragically cruel. Her father was
Gaptain English, who, some seven or eight years
ago, left Lakes Entrance in the little steamer
Glenelg for Melbourne, and was never afterwards
heard of. Though the sea had taken heavy toll
of her, Mrs. English preferred to follow it for a
living, instead of running a business that was
offered her ; so she got a place as chief stewardess
in the Yongala. Now there is only the little
schoolgirl left.
Artist George Lambert has two much-
discussed pictures in this season’s show of the
Modern Society of Portrait Painters, London. A
study, “La Danseuse,” is either loved or
loathed—it seems that you can’t sit on the fence
and view it. The other is “A Dancer Entertains
Jane and Some Portrait Painters”—a large
group subject, said to be reminiscent of the late
Fantin-Latour.
The grapes are very sour for poor, dis-
appointed, disgruntled old “Billy” Trenwith,
the ex-Senator who once stood for Australian
Nationalism, but is now sent to State-Fright in
his native Tasmania. “Do you not think, Mr.
Trenwith, that the best available intellect is in
the Commonwealth Parliament, and not in the
State Parliaments?” queried an elector at his
Delorame meeting. "No!” thundered Tren-
with, with concentrated bitterness. “ A German
sausage could get into Parliament these days.”
Poor, extinct Trenwith—of less account nowa-
days, it seems, than even a German sausage.
“Condot ” writes with boiling blood :
The time has arrived for doing something with Douglas
Brooke Wheelton Sladen. For our enemy hath written a
hook—another of many bad ones. It is called “ Oriental
Cairo,” and the best thing to be said of it is that the
illustrations are good. The letterpress is worse than
bad. The Poet of the Virtues of Condensed Milk for
Babies prints, as the application of an Egyptian seeking
employment, a letter setting forth that his “ means are
circumcised by his large family, consisting of live female
women and three masculine, the last of which are still
taking milk from mother’s chest, and are damna-
ble noiseful through pulmonary catastrophe in
their interior abdomen.” This is vouched for as genuine.
It may be—for it first appeared in a book called “ Baboo
English as ’tis Writ,” by Arnold Wright, published, in
1891. “ D.8.W.5.” should be disowned by Australians.
We are suffering from his patronage. In London, he poses
as an authority on Australian literature. There never was
in Australia a writer with duller literary sympathies;
and there never was a writer who wrote worse verse.
Yet D. B. W. Sladen passes for a representative Aus-
tralian poet, and looms large in his own anthologies.
What should be done with him I don’t know ; but surely
George Reid could be asked to tell the people of England
that D.B.W.S. is not an Australian at all, and that he is
not our apostle. Sladen’s claim to Australian citizen-
ship is built on the sad circumstance that he lived in
Melbourne for five years.
COLONEL J. STANLEY (VIC.).
The old folks at the Benevolent Asylum at
North Melbourne have been shifted to the new
home at Cheltenham, a thinly-populated suburb.
Some of the old ’uns strenuously objected to
the remove. Peter Newton, the one-time well-
known pugilist, got back some of his fighting spirit
and said he’d be counted out before he’d go. He
threatened to leave and take out a pension. This
dismayed the authorities for a while, as Peter is
responsible for a lot of the public interest in the
institution. But in the end the soft persuasions
of lady members of the committee and the allure-
ment of a six-mile ride in a swell motor-car on a
sunny day were too much for Peter ; and he went,
looking extremely respectable as he lolled back on
the cushions.
THE PERI AT THE GATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I beseech travellers, when they go to Rome, to deny
themselves the vulgar indulgence of a vain curiosity, and
to refrain, no matter how solicited, from going to see
the Pope. Let them remember that they are Christians.
—A Wowser in Sydney Watchman.
I hate to think about the Romish Pope.
Poor, poor old, lonely, friendless man !
Unless he chucks his job, he may not hope
To shift the agonising ban
Which keeps him rigidly excluded from
The gracious realms of Southern Wowserdom.
It matters not how hard the Outcast pravs,
Nor how he struggles to explain ;
His frantic pleas, the points he seeks to raise
Are each and all of them in vain :
Snowball, though pained, vouchsafes him no reply,
Dill Macky turns on him the Frigid Eye.
Ere Preacher Bloggs, of Deadbeat, starts for “ Home,”
He gravely vows he will not call
On Pius Decimus. Arrived in Rome,
He cuts Pope Pius dead ! And all
Because, besides instructing folks to dodge
Old Nick, Bloggs bosses Deadbeat’s Orange Lodge.
Year after barren year the Pope must go
Toward’s Life’s inevitable goal,
Nor hear the Righteous testify ; nor know
The mental feast and flow of soul
And joy and general uplift which come
From-howling hymns to a harmonium.
I hate to think about the Romish Pope.
Poor, poor, old, lonely, friendless man !
Unless he chucks his job, he may not hope
To shift the agonising ban
Which keeps him rigidly excluded from
The gracious realms of Southern Wowserdom.
n. s.w.
J.B.D.

A RHEUMATIC
CRIPPLE.
Tells How He Banished Suffer-
ing; and Pain*
Could just drag; along;—Cured by
Dr. Williams' Pink Pills,
«,
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are recommended to
rheumatic sufferers with confidence because they
have cured hundreds of cases of rheumatism.
That is the greatest test any medicine can have—•
the ability to cure. Mr. 0. Shanahan, bootmaker,
of 67 Buckingham Street, North Richmond,
Melbourne, was so badly attacked by rheumatism
that often he could hardly drag one foot after the
other, and the weight of the bedclothes was some-
times agonising to his aching muscles and joints.
Mr. Shanahan’s story, given in his own words, will
be of interest to anyone suffering with rheumatism.
“I could not make it out at first,” he said,
“but I noticed severe muscular pains coming into
my legs from the feet to the knees. I got worse
attacks as time went on. The attacks might go
away for a week or so, or appear for a fow days ;
but once I was laid up for five weeks in bed most
of the time, and unable to go out, and then I was
attacked in arms and shoulders as well. I was
always freer from the pain in winter, but I dreaded
the summer, for I always suffered then. The per-
spiration would run off me with the pain. I felt
every change in the weather. There was no siern
externally, but the muscles of the legs would feel
as if they were being all drawn up and twisted.
I was always having the parts rubbed with lini-
ments ; but I could not find any relief. I could
not bear any heavy bedclothes, the parts were so
sore and tender, and the warmth always made me
feel worse. I would lie awake afraid to move. I
was afraid for anyone to come near me. When
the attacks were on me I dreaded the least touch.
My legs were so stiffened that walking was most
painful. I would just drag my feet after me, and
I dare not put them on any cold surface. I might
feel quite well one moment, and perhaps the
slightest change of weather would bring on an
attack. I would have to get up and walk ahout if
I had been resting awhile, and again if I had been
walking home very tired I would have to go and
lie down. lam glad to say that a course of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills took the complaint right
away. lam only too glad to give this testimonial
to help any other sufferer. This remedy cured
me, and I have not had any sign of the rheuma-
tism since. My limbs are as supple as could be
wished.”
Remember, only the genuine Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills cure. Don’t be gulled into taking
something said to be just as good. Of all dealers
at 2s. 9d. per box, 6 boxes 15s. 3d., or from the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia, Ltd.,
Sydney.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Joke Cook, heckled by a Hobart audience,
said that there was no danger of trusts in Aus-
tralia assuming the power and proportions of the
American article, because the railways in Australia
were State-owned. This declaration, coming as it
did from the gentleman whom the simple folk of
Hobart had been taught to regard as the accepted
high priest of Anti-Sosh, fairly convulsed the
meeting, and it rocked on its seat with glee.
THE NO-NO FEDERALIST AGAIN.
Mr. Fisher and Mr. Hughes continually do cry that the
Federal Parliament has absolutely no intention of using
all the powers they want given it. That is well enough,
as far as Messrs. Hughes and Fisher are concerned. No
doubt they are honorable men, and would never use a
power which was not, in their opinion, national. Hut
they can only pledge the present Parliament.— Daily
Telegraph.
CAPTAIN LABOR: “So I’m not fit to be trusted
with, this ship, eh?”
EX-CAPTAIN FAT: “ Oh, nun-no, sir! Wot I

Tne man on the land got another illustra-
tion in Tasmania the other day of how his friend
the steamship owner loves him, when the co. that
drags the Huon apple crop to Hobart for tran-
shipment to England raised freightage by |d. a
case. The local Mercury hastened to inform
fruitgrowers that the increase had been rendered
necessary by the rise in wharfside workers’
wages, and was only what the producers deserved
for haying voted for the Labor party. The Labor
people' then poked inquiring noses into things,
a nd found that, while the wharf-lumpers, for
shifting 30,000 cases, received £10 more in wages,
the eo. collected £62 10s. from the fruitgrowers in
higher freightage. As the unfortunate fruit-
grower must ship his fruit at once or lose his
market, he is practically helpless in the grasp of
a monopoly. But he is wild, just the same—
almost wild enough to vote “ Yes” on the ques-
tion of giving the Commonwealth power to argue
with trade monopolies.
Nothing doubtful in ‘ ‘ Montserrat ” Lime
.Tuice. It s good and pure. The genuine juice of
cultivated lime fruit, it is an exquisite beverage
for all the year round. A healthv drink for
children.*
The cable briefly notifies that an associa-
tion has been formed in England for the suppres-
sion of debasing and demoralising fiction, and
branches are to spread to the colonies. So the
great “Liberal” dailies are up against trouble
again.
The last straw ! Peter Bowling has now
joined Bruce Smith and Wade in advocating the
“No-No” side in the Referenda. Joint meetings
to be addressed by Peter Bowling and Wade
would “ take well and give pleasure to all.”
Suggested title : “How we leg-ironed Australia.”
Apropos the Americo-Canadian reciprocity
treaty and its probable results, it is well to recall
the sonorous and comprehensive Yankee defini-
tion : “The United States, bounded on the north
by the Aurora Borealis, on the south by the Pro-
cession of the Equinoxes, on the east by the
Rising Sun, and on the west by the Day of judg-
ment.” How stay the march of a nation which
makes phrases like that !
“Aeneas B.”: The “Great Liberal Party”
is recommended to a study of Disraeli’s writings
for a picture of what will happen to the Common-
wealth if Labor’s Referenda proposals are
carried : “Amid the roar of the wind, the bosom
of the earth opened, swift columns of sand sprang
up to the lurid sky and hurried towards their
victim. With the clang of universal chaos, im-
penetrable darkness descended on the desert.”
“ P.X.”: “ You can’t fix prices by law ” is
the latest popular Tory cry in Melbourne, the idea
being to suggest that, armed with power to handle
monopolies, the Labor party proposes to fix prices
wholesale. Alf Deakin talked so much on this
point and was so dead-sure about it that, when I
went to the post office next day to get half a
dozen tuppenny stamps, I put down lid.
“What’s this?” said the attendant. I ex-
plained that I wasn’t going to pay any tuppence
for tuppenny stamps. “ Well, then,” said the
ignorant person in the cage, “ you can’t have ’em.”
“Can’t! I shouted. “Can’t! why can’t I?”
“ Simply,” answered the fool, “because the Law
says you’ve got to pay tuppence for the sort of
stamps you want.” Absurd," isn’t it ?
The No-noes never give one “ reason ” for
voting their way without knocking out another
“ reason.” Mrs. Bogue Luffman, one of their
organisers, issued a catechism for women electors
the other day, and it contained this:—
Q.: Would not the Federal Government give more
power to the workers ?
A. : No ; it would only transfer power from the State
to the Commonwealth.
It is quite true ; but, in that case, what becomes
of the shuddersome yarns about the dreadful
things that are going to happen if the answer is
Yes-yes? For it is admitted that the very same
people who will have the power to do the dreadful
things in the future have had the power to do
them all the time.
Sampson, M.H.R., of Victoria, has dis-
covered another horror which may result from a
“ Yes ” vote on April 26 :
Legislation in regard to forwarding produce from one
State to another was enacted by the State Parliaments.
If the matter came under the jurisdiction of the Federal
Parliament they would probably be in a worse condition,
as, if areas were quarantined, they would be prevented
from operating in any part of Australia. If laws similar
to the Federal quarantine laws existed in regard to
diseased potatoes, districts like Port Fairy and Koroit
would be cut off from trading, not only with the other
States, but also with Victoria. This was what might be
expected if they handed over to the Federal Parliament
those wide and sweeping powers sought under the refer-
endum.
In short, the State at present prevents the N. S.
Wales consumption of diseased potatoes from (say)
Victoria ; but the same diseased Victorian pota-
toes may be supplied to Victorians. And Samp-
son is horrified at the thought that perhaps the
Commonwealth will put a stop to the supply of
diseased potatoes to anyone. It is anew battle-
cry, and the enlightened Sampson can have it all
to himself : “ Vote for State Frights and Rotten
Spuds!”
Ararat (Vic.) is just getting a close view
of the playful little ways of Monopoly. It was
gas in the Ararat case, and the local council
thought to get out of the toils by starting an
electric-light business of its own. “Of course,
you’re perfectly entitled to do it,” said
the co.—“ we haven’t got a monopoly.”
And forthwith, by way of encouraging
the other to come on, it dropped the
price per 1000 ft. from 12s. 6d. to 10s.,
with a special rate of Bs. for Government
institutions. The council came on,
whereupon the co. cut down its price to
the Government by a further Is. 4d.,
and, it is said, completed a five years’
contract with the State. This last was
a staggering clout to the council, for it is
claimed that the success of the municipal
scheme rests on the extent of Govern-
ment support, Government institutions
being unusually plentiful at Ararat. So,
for the present, the scheme is hung up ;
and when it is nicely dead and buried,
the chances are that up will go the
price once more, and the people will thus
be made to pay, as usual, for the knife
which was used to cut the throat of their
own municipal scheme. Meantime the
councl has made a report on the subject. It
naturally inquires w hat the Weary Govern-
ment thinks of itself for helping to flatten
out municipal enterprise, and preventing
the public getting out of the hands of
the dividend-grabber; and it remarks that pro-
bably the end would have been different if Swin-
burne hadn’t been connected with the gas concern.
“Timothy Tufthunter”: “Old Soldier”
B. 9/3/’ll) mentions a very good idea adopted in
the British navy, i.e., granting “hard-lying
money.” Don’t you think the Wimmen’s Leagues
and the capitalist press should be rewarded, too?
What does Fisher say to £10,000,000 ? I know it
isn’t enough, but it would be a small recognition
of great efforts.
Freetrade Hodge, tothenumberofs6, struck
work last May in Norfolk (England). His wages
were 13s. a week, and his hours 6 a.m. to 6 p. m.,
less meal time, six days a week. Hodge demanded
145., or 2s. 4d. a. day, and a Saturday half-
holiday. These impudent demands were resisted
by the employers, and after a seven-months’ fight
half the men went back on the old terms ; the
other half were refused work on any terms.
The 2id.-an-hour vacancies were rushed, and
triumphant Fat watched the vanquished revolu-
tionaries take another reef in the belt and join
the hopeless, endless army of the unemployed.
Australia the saddest country ? Well, perhaps it
is—if you look at it from the point of view of the
man who wants labor at 2|d. per hour.
WOMAN’S RIGHTS.
HIM: "Now, I suppose you think every woman
ought to have a vote.”
HER: “ Well, yes; hut I'd he quite satisfied with
a voter.”
Attorney-General Hughes’s Yes-Yes meet-
ing in Hobart was the biggest that ever assembled
under one roof in Tasmania to hear a political
address. Some 1600 people squeezed into the
building. Until recently there was no hall in the
village with a holding capacity of much over
1000; but the uprising of the Empire, His
Majesty’s and King’s Hall has upset the mono-
poly so long enjoyed by the ancient Royal. With
all these new theatres, Hobart has more sleeping
accommodation than ever.
Bruce Smith, Wade (V.C.), Wood and co.
are continually screaming about the yoke sought
to be imposed upon the people of the Ma State
by some persons unknown in Melbourne —an echo
of the cry of the “Prudent” and other anti -
Federalists when first the Constitution was
framed. “Beware of Victorian Gold,” said
Reid’s famous placard. Now Deakin, of all men,
is trying the same dodge on Melbourne :
Those who suppose that all our affairs can be governed
by 126 men at Canberra are committing themselves to a
line of constitutional concentration which must break
down.
This is Deakin’s way of saying about N.S.W.
what Wade and Co. are saying about Victoria.
In brief, Deakin in Victoria is shouting, “Vote
‘Yes’ at this referendum, and N. S. Wales will
rule and rob you from Canberra,” while Wade and
Co. are yelling in N.S.W., “ Beware of giving the
Victorian despots power to rule you from Mel-
bourne.” It is the old, disgraceful provincial cry
raised again in the interests of State Frights and
Houses of Fat Landlords; and itstamps the “No ”
side as the anti-Federal, anti-Australian side in
this campaign.

An example of how the “ Liberal ” party
barracks for free speech, whilst the Labor party
basely suppresses it, is given by the various ex-
periences of Howe, M.H.R., and Cocks, M.L.A.
Howe interjected once or twice when Bruce
Smith was a little more absurd than usual, and
was promptly fired out. Cocks, whilst in Bris-
bane, went to Senator McGregor’s meeting, and,
when he interjected, was invited up to the plat-
form to say his worst for 15 minutes ; and a bored
but patient audience allowed him a few extra
minutes in which to conclude his remarks. The
result of the two incidents was much the same.
When Howe left Berlud Smith’s meeting most of
the crowd went with him and gave cheers for
Australia; and McGregor’s meeting also cheered
enthusiastically for the same Best Place.
The Sydney Clerks’ Union, after wrestling
for three years with Wade and other adverse cir-
cumstances, is now within sight of its thousandth
member. Once a Clerks’ Union was considered
impossible ; to-day it threatens to be the most
influential union in the Commonwealth. The
N.S.W. body has already provided five Labor
members of Parliament, the president of the
Labor Council, a vice-president of the P.L.L.,
and a score of other persons of more or less
prominence in the movement.
HIS TROUBLES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
South Africa gave the Union Parliament full power
because for her no other form of government was possi-
ble. There lie ahead of South Africa great national ques-
tions which nothing but full power could tackle—
questions which do not trouble Australia.—Sydney D.T.
No, worry ain’t yet turned me grey ;
I has me three big meals a day
(Includin’ of me beer-feed too),
An’ I ain’t shook on nothing new.
Wot say ? I orter learn to shoot ?
Now, come it easy there, ole coot!
I can’t be bothered with no gun ; .
Just pass the little nipper one —
I want to slumber in the sun,
My troubles 1
Wot’s that ? They says there’s war ahead ?
I wish to Heaven they'd drop down dead
Instead of spoilin’ of me nap ;
Ho, yes I know ! the Chow an’ Jap,
An’ all this foolish, blanky scare—
Oh, let ’em rip ! ’Tain’t my affair.
My troubles!
Wot, immigration ? Give it best!
Now, bloke, do lemme ’ave a rest!
They goes to Canada in shoals?
Well, let ’em ! Let ’em climb the Poles
Or swim about the blanky sea,
So long as they don’t trouble Me l
I got me bus’ness to be done,
I got to slumber in the sun ;
Don’t want no millions—l’m the ONE !
My troubles !

Here is a ballad of Deakin A.
(Thistles and bluff and a pair of Noes),
They re making 1 beautiful soup from bay,
And Alfred’s heard of it, I suppose.
Alf was all right till he fused with Cook
(Thistles and bluff and a pain of Noes),
But later the pair were brought to book,
And ever since that he’s had the Joes.
He looks on things with a State Frights eye
(Thistles and bluff and a pair of Noes) ;
When you’re not in, you are out, that’s why—
And out of office a cold wind blows.
The latest gag is the cheque that’s blank
(Thistles and bluff and a pair of Noes),
The people will loot the people’s bank.
And Deakin rush off with Deakin’s clothes.
Alf says he’ll fill in the right amount
(Thistles and bluff and a pair of Noes)
And carefully nurse our bank account—
Renegade Alf is a beggar to pose.
The ass is fond of the feed so green
(Thistles and bluff and a pair of Noes),
But lately asses have scarcer been.
Which makes hard hoeing in Liberal rows.
All in a stew are the State Frights group
(Thistles and bluff and a pair of Noes),
Doing their best to make thistle soup
And drug with drivel their scornful foes.
