QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney. April 3, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
Chief Justice Griffith received a presentation
portrait of himself from the solicitors of Sydney,
the other day. The four senior pursuers of Costs—
Air. Makinson, Mr. C. A. Laurence, Dr. G. J.
Sly and Mr. Robert Smith—acted as a committee
on behalf of subscribers. Percy Spence was the
artist. At the handing-over a legal atmosphere
prevailed. Mr. Makinson, the very oldest human
law-tome, began his speech : “ By leave of vour
Honor, Chief Justice of the High Court.” Mr,
Alfred Nathan, who gave this presentation por-
trait its first push, modestly hovered in the back-
ground with no limelinht accessories.
INo explosives were present at last week’s rally
of the National Council of Women in the Royal
Society’s House. The rejuvenation of Mrs. Sara
Nolan, who bears the brand of recent European
travel in improved chest measurement, was a
pleasing feature of this mild attempt to drive back
seas of moving-picture shows with a moral-
instruction broom. The weapon was handled in
the proverbial Partington fashion by Mrs.
Ebenezer Vickery, who hurled the full force of
her purse and person at the alleged abandoned
conduct of certain cheap offshoots of the picture-
panorama trade. Miss Rose Scott, whose taste in
bonnets generally causes an eclipse in adjacent
millinery, shook a white tulle pom-pom (a frivolous
thing at best), and refused to believe that weary
workers parted with spare coins because of an in-
ordinate craving to be morally instructed by a
cinematograph.
Miss Meager, of the Y.W.C.A. dove-cot, has
not sufficiently recovered from the imported
English point of view to do more than drape a
threadbare theme —the “thriftless” toiler —with
pleasant platitudes. She is mildly obsessed with the
idea that the poorer parts of Sydney contribute
too much cash to moving-picture money-boxes.
A venerable British superstition requires Hodge
to beware of extravagance, lest out of his princely
income of Bs. a week he should not be able to put
by anything for “the rainy day.” He is there-
fore required to fall down and worship this greedy
god with an old stocking, or a tin pannikin dis-
guised as a savings-bank. Hodge, if he be a
decent, well-brought-up person, knows that he
encumbers the earth for three purposes—to till it,
to pull a forelock to the resident gentry, and to
save enough coppers to burs' his rheumatics with-
out the help of the parish “union.” Miss
Meager’s contribution to the National Council
attack on picture shows missed the Australian
bus by about 14,000 miles.
Between the Hunch of the Australian torpedo-
»oat destroyer Warrego, at Cockatoo Island and
t ie militant attitude of the Peace Society, there
is no prospect of friendly relations. Mrs. Nolan,
who recently scampered through European liquor
acts on behalf of the W.C.T.U., deplored the
‘glamor” of the “military fever” at the last
National Council of discursive women. Mrs.
Nolan is such a soft-voiced, mild-mannered little
woman that even a Licensed Victualler would for-
give her ferocious antipathy to “’arf-an-’arf ” if
ue met her suddenly at a Sunday-school picnic.
The Peace Society does not suffer from scarlet
fever. It lives in a miasma of polite platitudes.
The W.C.T.U. is prepared to offer ginger-pop
even to the hordes of Asia. The “ glory ”of war
is a defunct superstition. But the national neces-
sity of protecting even Peace Societies remains
alive.
The Queen Victoria Club is not showing any
signs of collapse in its tea-and-china department.
By arrangement with other members," Mrs. R.
Mackinnon is lending the club rooms fora welcome
home party to Miss Helen P. Phillips. When'the
intrusion of Ai stralian women into Greek odes
was first countenanced by the Sydney Women’s
College, Miss Helen Phillips was principal of the
dusty-faced terrace house tnat preceded the pre-
sent brick barracks in University grounds.
Sue eventually gave up the serious pursuit, of
logarithms, to teach the elusive Ceylon native how
to say his prayers. 11l health laid her missionary
efforts low. Old girls of St. Catherine’s School,
Waverley—Helen Phillips’s first charge—will
mingle reminiscences and rainbow cake to express
their entire approval of the learned lady’s return
to Sydney.
Strained relations are said to exist between
Mrs. Bogue Luffinann’s club and Mr. W. M.
Hughes, the Federal Attorney-General. The
latter says, “Yes-Yes. Vote for Australia on
Referenda day.” The Queen Victoria Club says,
“No-No.” The well-known comic song, “Waltz
Me Round Again, Willie,” is not a favorite at
the moment in the club’s drawing-room.
Indignant Woman has set her teeth and told
the truth. The censu card demanded her age at
the point of a £5O fine. Unappropriated blessings
in N.S.W. retract any better feelings they ever
had for Commonwealth Statistics Knibbs. Jiu-
jitsu has lately been recommended by British
Australasian to Australian girls ; they are cordi-
ally invited, when in London fog circles, to visit
Mrs. Edith Garrud’s jiu-jitsu school. They will.
Next time census collectors try to worm their

Amaryllis, who is cruelly accused of having
toyed with 40 summers, fled from her boarding-
house on Sunday morning. She left a note pinned
to her powder-puff, to say she had not committed
suicide in consequence of the census, but intended
to while away 24 hours as a long-distance railway
traveller. Hurried word now comes that Ama-
ryllis was asked by a guard, in the presence of
four male adults and two female foes, to tell her
exact age. She disappeared. Her anxious friends
will be grateful if the railway authorities would
carefully inspect any window curtains or uphol-
stered seats. They fear that Amaryllis has con-
cealed her age for ever in a soap dish or pillow
case. Railway fettlers are advised to keep a
sharp look-out for the mangled remains of 40
years. No flowers.
Somebody has said: “ There are only two
stories ip the world. One concerns two women
and one man ; the other is about one woman and
two men.” Frances Hodgson Burnett has chosen
the latter for her new play, “The Dawn of a To-
morrow.” She divides the “local color” with
rather a heavy hand into London East and
London West. At the one matinee trying-on
show of this well-meaning stranger. Miss Irby
Marshall, as Glad, was torn to shreds by a part
that nothing but the stage-craft of a Duse or a
Bernhardt could lift from the level of a Religious
Tract Society’s wet-Sunday literature.
Though “ language ” is frequent in Apple Blos-
som Court slum area, “The Dawn of a To-
morrow ” is paved with good intentions. They
are treated in the American one-star part manner,
and lead to a Better World. “East Lynne’s”
Little Willie, going up to Heaven with
colored lights and a wet handkerchief,
only works half-time compared with the corres-
ponding person in the “Dawn,” whose damp
cloud and harp atmosphere is punctured by a
wandering baronet with a revolver in one pocket
and a ham sandwich and large collection of sove-
reigns in the other.
Frances Hodgson Burnett takes a really admir-
able young woman in “Rag and Bone” circles
for her heroine in this play, which has four dull
acts and three good situations. She is described
as “plain,” which is a help to any young person
who hopes to have a shred of reputation left.
“ Glad,” having talked for three successive acts,
with brief adjournments for refreshments, and a
two-minutes interlude in which she strokes the
hero’s back hair, admits (in the fourth act) that
she is “done.” The audience at the Royal on
Friday afternoon believed her.
Miss Mabel Trevor has the emotions of Marga-
ret Anglin, though she lacks the avoirdupois.
A beneficent Providence designed her tall,
slender figure to fit the present fashion in
eelskin skirts. Miss Trevor, as Margaret Rolfe
in “The Woman in the Case,” first glided into
the affections of the Criterion gallery on Saturday
night in a sinuous gown of gleaming ivory satin.
The “ Criterion ” captured a Duchess (Adeline
of Bedford) for its first-night performance of
“The Woman in the Case.” Governor-General
and Lady Dudley were responsible for this flight
into the higher walks of the Dukeries.
“My first remarkon the stage—in a one-line
part—was ‘I begyour pardon,”’ says Miss Trevor
over the tea cups. “Seems only yesterday.
When did I first get the usual feminine hunger
for footlights ? Strange to say, I never thought
about the stage or wanted to go on, like most
girls. Then quite suddenly I felt tired of
doing nothing, and walked in to Mr. George
Alexander’s office. ‘ Why do you want to go on
the stage?’ was his first question. ‘Oh, I’m
just tired of doing nothing. I don’t want to act,
and I’ve never played in amateur theatricals in
my life.’ ‘ That’s a blessing, anyhow,’ said he.
‘ You’ll be tired of it in a week.’ He let me walk
on in a comedy called ‘ The Wilderness.’” Miss
Trevor unloaded her one line to her manager’s
satisfaction. He took her on tour.
Miss Trevor belongs to the feminine type that
uses up every fraction of its vitality at high
pressure. She seems to be bright enough to throw
out sparks. Her magnetic current is in good
working order. Yet a mild fit of “ the blues”—a
highly popular phase of girl-imagination—drove
her to the footlights. “ It’s more than a week,”
says Miss Trevor in cheerful retrospect; “but
I’m not tired of the stage yet.”
“On the eve of his departure for Europe ” is
worrying the personal column of every Common-
wealth chronicle at the present moment. This is
an integral part of the ascent of Australian woman
into diamond tiaras, and the descent of man into
the “extras” of London Court dressmakers.
Knight Albert and Dame Gould are said to be
contemplating an invasion of England. The great
June drapery display is given as the first cause.
The Jim Allisons—she was a Miss Burdekin—
have taken the Philip Mortons’ house in Roslyn-
avenue. The Morton family leaves for England
after the races.
Mrs. Dalton, of Wheatleigh, North Sydney-
one of the well-known Orange family—will leave
Sydney by the St. Albans next week. Her ob-
jective is the land of the early-rising Jap. A
niece, Miss Nugent, will be one of the Dalton
cherry-blossom party.
The Sydney winter season is likely to have
Cattle-man Kidman’s daughters amongst those
present at its most alluring festivities. One Miss
Kidman will be eventually annexed by Adelaide.
Mr. Reid, of the Malcolm Reid merchant family,
is to be the bridegroom.
The Owen-Cox dinner last week was up to its
usual popular standard. The much-travelled Mr.
Cox departs for Europe in a few days. He will
take with him an extensive knowledge of the
world’s largest cities, and his only chick, Miss
Doris Cox.
The morning post brings a determined rumor
with an orange-blossom complexion. It asserts
that pretty Maie (Peach) Sydney, who wore her
own fair hair, sandal shoes and floral gown effects
in “ The Arcadians,” has married Captain Rey-
nolds. The bridegroom’s regiment is stationed
somewhere in India’s bomb resorts, and plays polo
in the best society. The lady’s sweet little pipe
will be an acquisition to Anglo-Indian private
theatricals.
Mr. and Mrs. Dalglish Bellasis arrived on the
Orvieto. The lady was a Miss de Lauret of
Goulburn. A sister is Sydney’s good-looking
Mrs. Ted Simpson, now in England. The
Bellasis family has been identified with Catholicism
in England since the days when the priest’s sleep-
ing apartment was, as a measure of ordinary pre-
caution, beneath a ringed paving-stone in the
family coal-hole. Some years ago the London
Oratory was in the same unfinished and un-
sightly condition that St. Mary’s, Sydney,
is in to-day. The Bellasis family came for-
ward with a donation which enabled the big
church to be completed out of hand. Tim pre-
sent visitors will stay at Pomeroy, the station be-
longing to Mr. Alec Dalglish, Mrs. Bellasis’s
eldest son.
Mrs. Willie Manning, a sister of Dr. Jack
Osborne, went to Melbourne for the Osborne-
Atherton wedding, which will cover Toorak with
a haze of bugle trimmings and diamond pendants
on April 5. Mrs. Peck, another Osborne sister —
formerly from the home nest, Hopewood, Bowral,
and latterly from the Straits Settlement—is going
to take the Toorak wedding party as an incident
in the Sydney Easter ball-and-bridge festivities.
A voice from Glen Innes sends the following
tale of two women and one policeman : When
Miss Brace, the English agricultural arrival,
bought a farm in that district, no one in the local
chaff business took her seriously till she showed a
knowledge of horns and hoofs that ran away from
adverse criticism. With her friend. Miss Alcock,
she recently tried to assuage official thirst for
information re shire rates by describing their com-
bined occupation as “Farmer.” Albert Edward,
the house cat, who was using the official visit as
an excuse for washing his whiskers in the milk-
jug, remained calm during a discussion in which
Shire Rates insisted on “female ” being prefixed
to “farmer.” Later on, the owner of Yarraford
farm met the rate-paper face to face. It
accused her of the mysterious occupation “ dom
dut.” Miss Brace searched an encyclopaedia, a
fowlhouse, a Latin dictionary and a cow-bail,
for the key to this closed door. Before smiting
the shire clerk with her agricultural certificate,
the accused demanded an explanation. She got
it. “ Ladies always appear in shire rate-papers
as domestic duties—otherwise ‘dom dut.’” The
shire council’s best bulldog will attend to any
future attempt to introduce “ female farmer ” into
a highly-respectable tax-paper.
Silkworm culture has been nursed into good
results by a little band of women in Brisbane. In
spite of solid helpings of that upstanding joint
known as “cold shoulder,” they went on showing
so many mulberry-leaf reasons for their de-
termination to bring up large families of
high-class ■ worms, that the Government
is now said to extend a friendly finger
to home-grown silk. An expert was brought
from Italy to teach the industry, and
a local worm factory is now gathering itself
together. Sydney, somewhere in the latter end
of this month, is going to wriggle with a branch
of the Brisbane silkeries. The Corticelli people
in Canada are chiefly responsible for this boom in
the silk-worm family. A friend, who never tells a
cold-blooded, unnecessary lie, says that the
Canadian manufacturers have written out to con-
gratulate the Queensland silk-worms on having
found the climate that will make them beat all
other egg-laying competitions.
“ All Nations Fair ” in the Town Hall will be
vigorously extracting the nimble shilling from
pocketless woman when this reaches you. A tidal
wave of sympathy has recently hurled itself against
the old iniquity of housing nurses-in-training in
herring-tins, or noisy wooden match-boxes. The
passing of Mrs. Gamp was good for the patient,
but bad for the nurse. Sairey would have re-
sented, with the business end of her umbrella,
conditions under which the hospital nurse of 20
years ago did her highly-trained work. Gamp
slept on duty in the patient’s best arm-chair. She
took his pillows and drank his medical comforts.
Her immediate successor could sleep neither on
nor off duty. She was badly fed and atrociously
housed. Every big Australian hospital has made
an upward effort in this matter. “All Nations
Fair” ought to put Prince Alfred Hospital in a
good financial way to continue the upward move-
ment and house its nursing staff in comfort.
Architect Hardy Wilson, who brought from
England the George Lambert picture show, is a
keen admirer of things and persons artistic. His
present shrine is a flat near the tram-scratched
heights of Darlipghurst. The Hardy-Wilson
drawing-room is a survival of the fittest in fur-
niture. It comes between the New Art decora-
tive scheme, which runs riot in hand-beaten
brasswork and spindle-legged sofas, and the
severely solid comfort of the Tudor period. It is
—in a quiet fashion—a minor museum of art trea-
sures. Antiquities in fans, old pieces of rare
china, quaint draperies and good pictures make this
room an unsafe place for any collector of curios.
It will drive to despair every visitor whose private
meditations are surrounded in the home circle by
plush chairs, white antimacassars and woolwork
scenery.
Horatius is reported to have kept the bridge ip
the Lays of Ancient Rome. Vaucluse aldermen
—with the notable exceptions of Mayor Johnson
and Alderman Read—wished to keep the old
wooden shanty known as the Camera Obscura.
They refused to lend it to the military authori-
ties, who wanted it for “wireless” purposes.
Public opinion has jumped on the objection,
and Mayor Johnson—descendant of Johnson,
sole survivor of the Dunbar wreck—is reported,
in consequence, to wear a smile of patriotic
architecture. The Obscura is toiling up the hill
to the military reserve. It will get into training,
as a “wireless” station, for Easter-camp use,
before the Yaucluse Council has time to shriek
for help.
It has been mentioned “by the way ” that Mr.
Lauchlan Mackinnon (of Melb. Argus) is a
brother of the Duchess of Somerset. News of this
kind ought not to escape into English papers with
a merely middle-class preface. “ By the way ” is
good enough to introduce a census card to a rabid
fox-terrier. It is not meet to link it up with the
Somerset strawberry patch. As a matter of cold
fact, the Duchess was a Mackinnon ; but her
father’s name was Charles, whereas Lauchlan’s
male parent owned the initials A.K. The London
Evening Standard can't make up its linotype as
to whether Lauchlan or Francis Mackinnon is en-
titled to hail himself chieftain of the clan Mac-
kinnon. All these speculations are apropos the
fact that the Lauchlan Mackinnons are on the
motor for the small, bare Isle of Skye, which the
family left 60 years ago for Australia and afflu-
ence.
Mr. E. W. MaCkay, of Lismore, who generally
brings something with a bush pedigree to this
city, is staying at the Royal Hotel. He is not
attended by an aboriginal corroboree, as in the
past. A museum of Australian birds, stuffed, is
his chief excuse for carrying excess luggage on a
hard-worked permanent way. This fine collec-
tion of feathers and dead songs was gathered
together by a A. P. Goodwin, F.R.G.S., of
Byron Bay. It includes, amongst those
reluctantly present, the shy, snow-white
crane from the Brunswick River. This
beautiful creature eluded the bullet of fate for
six months, and finally fell before the unerring
aim of the late Mr. Jack Manchee. Black velvet
feathers, shot with iridescent green, belong to
the bush-whacking rifle bird ; the gorgeous red
and emerald dress-coat of the king parrot; dragoon
birds from northern scrub country ; the funeral-
reform cockatoo, who is out of mourning in his
scarlet tail; lyrebird and bronze cuckoo, blue jay
and whistling eagle, friarbird and brush turkey—
all are having a dull time under circumstanc< s
and glass eases over which they have no control.
A friend in London writes that Felix Ogilvie.
who so despicably duped the unfortunate Ella
Caspers into a bigamous marriage, turns out to
have been born in Germany of Scotch and Irish
parents. Journeying to England, he enlisted in
the Royal Artillery and transferred to the Black
Watch. From that regiment he deserted
and joined a Scottish regiment, but was
sent back to the Black Watch as a
deserter. In order to get clear of the
ranks, however, he misconducted himself
in such a way as to be dismissed with ignominy.
There is no doubt that Ogilvie, or Bradley, was
an accomplished adventurer; he was a man of
education, with a dress and demeanor that gave
him the appearance of a person of refinement and
culture. He spoke German, English, French,
Spanish and Italian fluently, and Justice Dar-
ling, in passing sentence of 10 years’ imprison-
ment, characterised the prisoner’s statement
as a manifesto of which he had not read the
like except in the romances of Dumas. Miss
Caspers, who is suffering intensely, has the
sympathy of all the Australian “colony” in
London. She has decided to remain in the big
city and continue her studies as far as practicable
To this end she will give a recital during May,
when the strong support of the “colony” is
assured.
Yours, with a broad nib,
VANDORIAN.

A GENUINE HAIRGROWER.
We have received the following recipe from a
correspondent who has found it to be of great
value. Our correspondent says that this mixture
is a hair tonic which, if rubbed briskly into the
scalp night and morning with the finger tips, will
not fail to promote the growth of hair, to remedy
baldness, to restore grey hair to its natural color,
and to destroy dandruff.
Take £ dram of Menthol Crystals, and put them
into a bottle containing 3oz. of Bay Rum, and see
that they are entirely dissolved, then add loz. of
Lavonade Comppsee, and if desired one teaspoon-
ful of To-Kalon perfume. Shake well and let it
stand for half an hour when it will be ready for use.
Other readers will doubtless be glad to try this
remedy which our correspondent has sent. We
understand that the ingredients may be obtained
from any chemist.
CAUTION.—As this is doubtless a remarkable
hair grower , it should be applied only where hair is
desired*

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 3, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Lady Honor Ward, the gentle-looking eldest
daughter of Governor Dudley, who some time ago
departed Fog ward under the wing of a governess,
made an impressive debut in London, according
to all accounts. Grandmamma Georgina chaper-
oned the maid, and all the brilliant younger
generation of Wards (uncles and aunts) backed
her up. After a perusal of the list of guests, I
am sure that dukes must have been at least three
a penny—which is cheaper than bananas nowa-
days. Lady Wolverhampton gave the dinner and
ball, and the bud motored up from Pembroke
Lodge for the event with the DowageV Countess.
She wore white muslin—unadorned ; and report
says she looked like a copy of her mother.
Lady Morvyth, the G.-G.’s next daughter, who is
just now galloping round the country and putting
her horse at the Ercildoune fences, is more like
Dowager Georgina, and promises to be a brilliant
beauty some day. She is “Dick” in everyday
life, and is a thorough little sportswoman. She
rides anything alarmed guardians will let her
approach, and never goes through a gate. Over
it—with the dogs at her horse’s heels—is her
method. She used to ride demurely enough along
St. Kilda-road and the Avenue in the mornings ;
but when safely away from horrified governesses
and alarmed grooms she goes across country. Her
riding rig in town included riding boots that were
the envy of every Melbourne horsewoman. They
were of soft tan, and as high as fishermen’s
waders. A brief skirt, well-fitting brown tunic
coat, and a brown felt hat planted on the back of
her curly, brown hair completed her turn-out.
Governor-elect Fuller is a cousin of Better-not-
Wallington, who knew the shoals and deeps of
local society, and through them
as a good pilot takes a liner into a dangerous port.
What an excellent recommendation it is for the
post! If the Wallington wisdom is a family
trait, the new Governor will have a smooth time
officially, and socially he will be snugly wrapped
in a swandown robe of tact.
The Victorian Artists’ Society assembled on
Wednesday, the 29th, to welcome Longstaff on
his return to Australia. When the painter
reached these shores, he bolted straight off to the
Goulburn Valley to see his mother and father,
who live in a delightful, rambling, old-fashioned
house in Shepparton. Consequently the artists
had to lie in wait for him. When he had been
caught, Rita Hope, with gold beads in her hair,
was the star of the musical programme that was
played and sung to him. The crowd of welcotners
could not all, however, squeeze into the large
music room. They filled the upper rooms and
the picturesque corridors, and littered even the
branching stairway ; and some of them danced to
the music of a commandeered piano.
All the well-known artists were on hand, in-
cluding Withers, Mather, Ford Patterson, and
the travelled Ambrose Patterson, who has been
picking up sea glimpses round Beaumaris since
his return. Blarnire Young towered above the
happy crowd. Cousin Will Longstaff and his
wife smiled a welcome to all; and Mrs. Muntz-
Adams, who also claims relationship, beamed
at the shoal of guests. Artist Mather made the
usual speech at the- lion, who, like most lions,
looked uncomfortable. He murmured several
good anecdotes ; but the decorations mostly ab-
sorbed them and left the eager listeners hungry.
Longstaff is a fine storyteller in a studio or over
a dinner table ; but he seemed shy and nervous on
t,he platform. His voice rang out enthusiastically,
however, when he spoke about the possibilities of
Australia for artists. “Color,” “line,” “light
and shade” —he catalogued them and ejaculated,
“ Extraordinary ! ”
Mrs. Muntz Adams, the clever portrait painter
who made quite a masterpiece of her portrait of
Duncan Gillies, is back in Melbourne. She has
been travelling for some time with her sister,
Miss Edith Muntz, a University girl who lost her
health through over-study. The artist is opening
her studio again after Easter.
Secretary Victor Hood, with his fishing-rod
over his shoulder, has come back from his Maori-
land holiday a much-refreshed man. He caught
enough brown and rainbow trout to stock a fish-
shop. Unfortunately, it isn’t reckoned quite the
thing in vice-regal circles to run a fish-stall; and
if a gentleman, help arrived at some function with
scales on his evening clothes, something would be
certain to break.
Two garden parties are happening at the Mal-
vern Government House this week. The second
is apparently sacred to Art. Longstaff will be
present, and lots of people in the Art world have
accepted invitations to wander about the beauti-
ful terraces and talk to one another.
The Free Kindergartens are growing and multi-
plying. Two years ago there were only three in
Melbourne ; now there are ten, with three nur-
series on the kindergarten principle. A de-
monstration of the work was given in the
Town Hall on Wednesday, and Alfred Deakin
made a soft, purring speech about it. Mrs.
Deakin, who works hard extending the
scheme, sat on the platform and listened
approvingly. With her were Mrs. Supervisor
Champlin, Doctor Edith Barrett, and Mrs.
Davev. Education Tate and Treasurer Watt,
were present, and Gaud Mayor Davey set the
work-ball rolling.
After the business proceedings, Miss Semmens,
of the Burnley Kindergarten, marshalled 80 of
her babies for show. The little ones invaded the
platform, and went through a “morning” pro-
gramme, which was instructive to the admiring
grown-ups, and just a play to the small demon-
strators. Samples of the youngsters’ work were
hung round the walls—things of the kind that
tend to make fond parents rave.
The story of “Goodrest ’’ has arrived at a chap-
ter which, it is hoped, will be long and peaceful.
Goodrest is the tall, stuccoed mansion in Toorak-
road standing next to the stately domicile of the
Fred Paynes, and fronted by a festive garden
with a fountain in the middle of it. Old Mr. W.
P. Buckhurst —who wasn't chemist Buckhurst, of
the Mutual Store, but his uncle—left the fine resi-
dential property (as the auctioneers would call it)
to his second wife for the term of her natural
life. The young widow was to have the
ownership of it; but after her death it
was to go to the Melbourne Hospital,
and be used as a home for convalescent patients
or a rest-cure establishment for tired nurses, or
what not. The widow’s hold of the property was
expected to be long, seeing that she was 40 or 50
years younger than her late husband. But one
never knows one’s luck. The lady married again,
and a few months later she followed her first hus-
band to the bourn that Hamlet speajts of, where
travellers stop for good. Then the Melbourne
Hospital committee found itself possessed of a
white-elephant legacy. It couldn’t devote
Goodrest to the purpose willed by the testator, nor
could it convert the place into cash—certainly
not without the permission of the Buckhurst
trustees. So it took the case to court in a friendly
way, and an accommodating Judge decided that
the Hospital could sell the house and grounds if it
agreed to share the proceeds with old Mr. Buck-
hurst’s heirs and assigns. The other day the
Argus announced the sale to a “private pur-
chaser.” It seems that the new lady of the house
will be Mrs. Charles Atkins, who knows every
nook and cranny of it. Charles Atkins married a
daughter of W. P. Buckhurst; and just 25 years
ago he took her away from the old home to which
he escorts her back this week. Twenty of those
vanished years don’t count as far as the happy
matron’s appearance is concerned ; all the same,
a numerous family has been growing up, and
everything in the garden’s lovely.
The Army and Bar, who were dying to bowl
and bat at one another on their old-time school
pitch, were defeated with great slaughter and
heavy damages by the weather. Three times was
the date of battle fixed, and as many times post-
poned. The teams rushed the matter, however,
on a recent Saturday on the ground of the Church
of England Grammar School, and flocks of ad-
miring womenfolk fluttered out to pat their kid-
gloved applause and drink tea. Donald Mac-
kinnon flung himself about like a grey-haired boy;
Guy Madden and lawyer Billy Williams were
conspicuous bowlers; while the Army was best
represented by Major-General Hoad and the tall
and stately Colonel Stanley.
Of the approaching sassiety weddings, the
Atherton-Osborne ceremonial will have the
prettiest bunch of bridesmaids. Sisters Molly
and Muriel Atherton are acknowledged beauties.
Gwladys Chirnside, just back from a Maoriland
romp, is one of the smartest of Melbourne’s
woolly-wealthy girls. Gladys (without the
“ w”) Box has her full share of good looks ; and
Nana O’Hara is a show-girl when it comes to pro-
gramme selling at amatuah charitable shows.
Medico Stawell is the proud father of a brand-
new infant. The baby is lucky in possessing a
pair of knowledgable parents. Stawell is a
noted specialist in children’s illnesses ; and Mrs.
Stawell, previous to her marriage, held a responsi-
ble nursing post. Stawell was supposed to be a
confirmed and unconvertible bachelor. He and
three other medical men made solemn vows of celi-
bacy. Davenport was one ; but he fell many years
ago. The name of the second I disremember.
Stawell, the third, took a wife after many moons.
The fourth remains still staunch to bachelordom.
He is handsome, white-haired Hamilton Russell,
art lover and haunter of musical high-planes.
Manderson, of Melbourne Age, and Jack Mac-
donald, of Melbourne Argus, were sent off by the
Melbourne Press Club lately. They are among
those whose eyes shall see the King in his beauty
at the Coronation. Jack Davidson sat in the
chair and flattered them; and Leipsig, of the
Opera House, toyed with cards, etc. “Mandy”
and “Mac” expressed much emotion, and threat-
ened to come back if funds ran out.
Mrs. Fred “Dewar” Thiel gave a jolly solo-
whist party at the Grand, last week, to farewell
her sister-in-law, Mrs. Percy Thiel, who is off on
a long world-wander. The whole of the big draw-
ing-room was commandeered for the occasion, and
the guests played in intense silence. At afternoon
tea-time, the two Thiel husbands came along with
20 or so other men. It was quite a turning of the
card tables. Usually, it is the women who hurry
in just as the kettle is boiling. On the arrival of
the males, talk and farewell speeches stopped the
play. Mrs. Percy Thiel wore some wonderful blue-
feathered poultry in her black hat, and had a few
discreet touches of Coronation-blue in her black
dress. The hostess gowned herself quietly in
mouse color, and wore flame-color wings in her
mouse-colored hat.
Clever Rita Hope gave a well-attended piano
recital in the Athenaeum last week. Marshall
Hall, in an inconspicuous seat behind the ranks of
Fashion, applauded loudly and often. Her biggest
item was Beethoven’s Sonata in D minor. Alter
a rest, Liszt, Schumann, Chopin, Leschetizky
and Greig were collected in an ambitious pro-
gramme. Rita has such talent, and is blessed with
a magnificent physique and the strong Carreno
type of arm. Tralford Foster, tenor, contributed
a whole music-roll of songs. An air from Rossini’s
“II Barbiere ” proved not so charming as the usual
tenor talk of loves, doves, trees, stars and deserts.
Miss Baskerville, with some assistance from
Mr. J. Brennan, gave acircleof Austral Salonites
a delightful hour or so on Monday afternoon. The
time was spent in a reading on the art of sculpture.
Miss Baskerville supplied the remarks, and Mr.
Brennan tired them off for her at the audience,
which had some of the clever woman’s best work
to look at whilst listening. Her “Wings of
Motherhood ” was thought much of by the Felton
Bequest people; but, unfortunately, it is in
plaster, and the Gallery must have either marble
or bronze. Miss Baskerville has visions of repro-
ducing the work in marble, and then she’ll wait
eagerly to see what happens.
Miss Grey and Miss Steele, a devoted pair of
Melbourne girl-chums who lived, breathed and
dressed in duet, have been separated at last by
the marriage of the Steele partner to Mr. Edouard
Jacot. The bride, who is only 18, writes a Swiss
address on the corner of her visiting card. The
couple are now honeymooning at a city tavern,
but will journey later on to the Italian lakes.
Alf Tracey, the good-looking naval officah, is
off to Fogland with his bride. He annexed the
second of the Robertson girls, who carries two
handsome figures—one in her clothes and the
other in her bank-book.
The Tracey bridegroom’s memory must have
been stirred by the recent loss of the yacht Mavis
and her crew of four, in one of our sudden gales.
About 16 years ago, just such a gale happened
during a Mornington-St. Kilda yacht race, with
the result that 23 boats were driven ashore.
Yachtsman Jack Scott was captaining an open
24-footer. Tracey was one of the crew; the
others were Charlie “ Allan’s ” Tait and young
Frazer. They struggled home at last, with nothing
but a jib, the rest of the sails having been torn to
ribbons. For his seamanlike work on that
occasion Tracey was voted a “brick ” of the most
buoyant sort. Todd, the unlucky man who had
to run down the pier to catch the lost yacht as it
sailed away from St. Kilda, had been chief clerk
in Allan’s for many years, and the tragedy made
Charlie Tait reminiscent. The battle between the
Mavis and the storm must have been much the
same ; only, in the case of long ago, the boat beat
the storm.
Mrs. Harcourt Beattie has been indulging in a
new and bothersome form of “ itis,” which kept
her in bed with one leg in plaster of Paris for a
fortnight. Then she was well enough to be motored
out to the Ballenger home by the daughters of the
house. She will return to the city hotel in time
to meet her actor-husband.
The R. S. Smythes entertained Winifred
Gullett-Etherington (daughter of Henry Gullett,
of Sydney Daily Telegraph proprietary), on her
way through to Colombo with her tea-planter
bridegroom. Amongst the guests was South
Australian barrister Paris Nesbitt, who is, in his
large way, the handsomest man in Australia.
The late James Smith’s house at Hawthorn is
to be opened as a home for inebriates. James
Smith was an ardent believer in spooks; these
later inhabitants must have believed in ardent
spirits.
Manager E. J. Tait had not long got back to
Her Majesty’s after his attack of appendicitis,
when his wife went down with severe nerve-
strain. Dr. O’Hara now has her in a hospital,
and he and Dr. Mackeddie have made her practi-
cally a prisoner. It is hard luck for E.J., com-
ing on top of his own long bout of sickness.
In the most elevated frocking circles, the much-
discussed garment is the Pasha skirt—not the
Harem. Pasha is the more acceptable term;
Harem is too aggressively Oriental. The pathetic
outcry of the infrequent exiled Turk in Australia
against the local versions of his pirated pants
is warranted. The shape varies in the most
bewildering way, according to the whim of
the wearer or her dressmaker. The label
“ Esquimaux” would fit some of them better than
“Harem.” With others, “Dutch” would fill
the bill; a back view of the bags, when worn
with the straight jacket, calls up memories of the
“ Hook of Holland ” men.
When half a dozen women who have flown (and
there are at least that number here now) chance to
meet in a teashop or elsewhere, it is well nigh im-
possible to get the talk back to earth. But even in
the air most of the talk is of dress. The skirt is
the chief difficulty. One adventurer in the blue
wore the narrowest tube obtainable, and clamped
it down with a close-fitting sweater. But even
that wasn’t a success ; the air-currents made sport
of it. Miss Dutrieu, a Parisian, and one of
the most daring of women fliers, has, according to
the illustrations, a weird dress composed by her-
self. The nether garments are very loose bags
until they reach well below the knee; then they
clamp in tightly like harems. Her helmet is all-
in-one with the tunic ; and that gets over another
difficulty of the female fliers.
Louise L. L. Smith is about again after her
accident. She nearly lost the sight of her very
blue eyes by the explosion of a gas stove. As it
was, she lost the skin of her face, her eyebrows
and eyelashes and much front hair. Dr. O’Hara
shut her up in his hospital for a time, and made a
complete cure. The patient has come out, as from
a beauty cure, with a new skin, sweet and fair as
a baby’s, and with the eyebrows just pencilling
back into life.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Darby-Crozier wedding ceremony was effective but
quiet. None but relatives were asked. The bride and
her attendants wore short skirts. The Crozier girls are
keen horsewomen, and are to be found in the saddling
paddock on most race days. Moreover, they have high
hopes of the mare that races in their name. Bridegroom
Darby, when not racing, mends our adhi ng teeth.
Mr. Owen Smyth is back from the place we still call
“ the East.” He left his daughter behind, but, in ex-
change, acquired some new views of the yellow man.
His regard for the average Jap is now low. The returned
traveller splits his time between quail-shooting and pre-
siding over the make and destiny of public buildings.
Our young men are doing well. Elton Mayo (brother
of one of our women doctors) has secured a good appoint-
ment in Queensland, after a meteoric University course
here ; while Ronald Angus and M. G. Murray are carrying
off athletic honors for their rival ’Varsities in England.
All three are Adelaide “old blues,” hailing from St.
Peter’s College.
From an Adelaide daily : “ Lady wishes housekeeping,
one gentleman; clean, careful, methodical. Moral.
.” Apparently the paragon has not
been rushed, for the advt. has appeared more than once.
The servant problem is no less acute, and distracted
housewives are seeking to fill the blanks by “lady”
nurses, etc. One superior being demanded £2 a week
and many privileges, which apparently out-numbered her
qualifications. Anyhow, it was refused, and she has
shaken the dust (and there’s plenty) of Adelaide from
her feet.
Government House is understaffed no longer. Mr.
Row, R.N., arrived the other day to fill the niche vacated
by Mr. Baillie Hamilton last year. All last summer
Captain Fletcher was A.D.C. and private sec. in one.
The Church is in a ferment again. Bishop Thomas hag
imported Bishop Wilson from Melanesia (you remem-
ber, he was an old Cambridge cricket “ blue,” and got
wrecked in the China), and intends installing him as
archdeacon or something. Howls of indignation are
issuing from many local divines. Which suggests that
even clerics possess ambitions—and tempers.
On Thursday the “ Jimmy” Gosses departed for W.A.,
which is to be their home in future. “ Jimmy ” Gosse
was well known as an oarsman and footballer till a year
or so ago, when he appropriated a Barr Smith bride and
settled down to more domestic things. The same boat
bore off the Peter Woods, who are to swell the Coronation
crowd, and “Gus” Clarke, who owns some acres near
Angaston.
If Mayor Cohen’s banquet on Saturday night was any
index to his popularity, his energetic worship must have
a heap of friends. It was a gorgeous show, and the nice
things said were even nicer than usual. He carries with
him Adelaide’s good wishes and a commission to make
preliminary arrangements for an Edward memorial.
During their stay in the South-East their Exes, are to
be part of the while guests of the Riddochs. Their place
is one of the show stations of the district, and has an en-
viable reputation for hospitality and picturesquenesg.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
A practical discussion of the wrestlingr. boxing
question took place at Melbourne Athenaeum Hall
the other night, when the students of the Vic. Health
and Strength College gave their annual show. A power-
ful young boxer (list. 71b.) and a ’rastler weighing lOst.
or thereabouts had a willing go. They were up to fair
average amateur standard, and their condition was good.
The boxer was never in it. He led o£E with a swipe which
the lightweight ducked, at the same moment catching the
gloves professor by the ankle and downing him heavily.
Then he twisted the ankle with a cross-lock, and the boxer
signalled for mercy ; so he was to all intents and pur-
poses knocked out before he had hit his opponent In
the second exhibition round he biffed the ’rastler once on
the “dial” ere he was down, and then he had to
cry “Enough!” to save his arm from dislocation.
Next round came rougher on the ’rastler, who took
several heavy punches before securing a strangle-hold.
And in the fourth and last round the same order of things
was repeated, except that an arm-hold gave the boxer his
quietus. He had full license to punch his adversary
whilst they were both down ; but he couldn’t save him-
self. This good sporting go was suggested by Clarence
Weber’s recent offer to take on a heavyweight boxer
under the mixed conditions. Still, as a test of fight-as-
fight-can between rival specialists, it was of no value to
the ordinary citizen, who wants to know how to get the
best of an unexpected rough-and-tumble. The boxer was
handicapped with gloves, and the 'rastler, on the other
hand, was forbidden to punch. Now, if a bare-knuckled
Johnson were to meet Weber on “all in” terms, the
demonstration might have sorrowful results for the
’rastler.
* * * *
Ernie Fullalove has accepted Clarence Weber’s
challenge to any heavyweight boxer—Jack Johnson for
choice —who will allow him to utilise the methods of the
wrestler against the boxer’s arts, best man to win. Fulla-
love is an amateur, and not a daddy at that; but he
reckons he can rock the wrestler to sleep while the latter
is pawing for a grip. #
Mr. Lang, pugilist, now on his way home from
furrin parts, seems to have lost that white-flower brand
of modesty which sat so gracefully on what Milton would
have called his “ horrid front ” ere he went away. Mr.
Hang’s career abroad was anything but wildly successful.
He seems, according to cabled accounts, to have lost his
head ; and now, also according to the cabled accounts, to
have likewise lost his mouth, so that the aperture is career-
ing round, talking without any restraint. The gentleman
who lost twice on a foul is anxious to take on Champion
Johnson, above all men. How he can figure out that he
is entitled to the honor of being walloped by Johnson is
a conundrum that only Mr. Lang can answer. At pre-
sent, he has his own chance and Mr. Buckley’s.
* * * *
Those Yankee boxers who recently raided the
Commonwealth would appear to have demoralised the
local pugs. Hitherto the Australian bruiser fought with
his mauleys ; now he is adopting the Yankee method of
fighting extensively with his mouth. Hughie Meeghan
recently opened his head in Melbourne, and gave some
startling explanations as to why Arthur Douglas and he
did not meet; and in due course, as in honor bound,
Mr. Douglas’s manager replied. And the sum total of it
all is that somebody, one or the other of them, is lying.
The public doesn’t care a thrum why they didn’t meet.
It doesn’t want to hear their talk. All it wants is to see
’em fight; and if they won’t do that, it wants them to
shut up. * * *
Distance changes the outlook in bruising as in
landscape. The morning after Johnson broke Burns up,
Burns admitted that he had received some punishment
and was a good deal knocked about. A week later he
couldn’t remember Johnson hitting him, and was ready
and willing to eat him before breakfast, so to speak, the
day after Langford had won from Lang lie re-
marked: “Lang is a type of fighter I never met
before. He has a good punch with either hand,
and many of his blows I felt a bit. Less than
a week later the outlook had changed, and the
contusions had gone. Then Langford spoke again : I
would have won in another two rounds. He could not
have stood up against me very long. That terrible punch
of his was a snowflake. It wouldn t hurt a babv. I
wasn’t knocked down ; I just tripped, and that s all. I
don’t like to express m,v opinions; but I have, them
about a man who hits another when he is down. It is
all good proof that immediately after a fight a bruiser a
mouth should be sewn up and remain sewn for four
months. „ *
* * *
James Griffin, one-time amateur champion of
Maoriland, who came to Australia and promised great
tiiirm'S in the professional ring, has struck a financial
snagT A long and wearying illness has sapped his strength
and depleted his purse, and a benefit will be tendered
him at the Gaiety Athletic Hall, Castlereagh-street,
Svdnev, on the night of Easter Monday. The object is to
raise funds to see the plucky fighter through a serious
operation, and send him home to Maoriland to get better.
The idea deserves support.
* * *
Jack Johnson is in the jug. He received a
week or something for furious driving, and then ap-
Dealed. In the Sl ates an appeal is a loaded thing that is
liable to burst at any moment, and if it fails it always
bursts, sometimes very badly. Johnson s appeal burst,
and the Judge who blew it out sacked another week or
10 days on to the sentence. Johnson is doing it now. He
found some solace the second day from the allegation
that the John Hop who had twice run him in had been
caught letting “ speeders ” go on receipt of palm oint-
ment What Johnson was probably kicking himself over
was that he didn’t try some of that ointment. He broke
up the gaol people by petitioning to have the policeman
placed in the same celL # #
The people who organised the Parramatta Hun-
dred were shocked, and inexpressibly so, at a demand
made bv some of the scullers for appearance-money, and,
rumor hath it that the men who asked were the least
important of the bunch. But that may be the promoters
wav of getting back on the presumptuous caitiffs. Pro-
moting sculling is a game that is calculated to sour the
most amiable nature. Scullers are, as a rule, not cursed
with over much modesty. The gate is not safeguarded
and the proportion of deadheads beats even that which
afflicts Sydney Cricket Ground. Moreover, the steamer
owners who made much f t coin by conveying people
deadheads —to see the show, would not contribute a
bean : and neither would the Tramways. So the people
who promoted the show have notified that they consider
they have done their whack, and if there are to be any
more Parramatta Hundreds, some other mugs can pro-
mote and finance them. It’s a sad world !
The scullers of the Ma State are wondering if
Sydney Kemp, who is to row Harry Pearce for the cham-
pionship of Australia and a swag of £lOO, is a dark horse.
He gave no indication of it in the Parramatta Hundred ,
but then, it is said, he was not in condition, lhe fact
that his father, ex-champion Peter Kemp, is enthusiastic
about his chances impresses a lot of people, who say that
Peter has no sons where sculling is concerned. Anyhow,
as far as the man in the street is concerned, Kemp is an
absolutely untried young man, except for a f ew ?ooa
handicap races, and is getting the chance of hn ■
But, though untried, he has nice possibilities about him.
The struggle takes place on the 22nd—a Saturday.

The Athletic Championships at Christchurch
(M. 8.) were a great success, and the record book was
badly damaged. The local record book, that is, of
course. Four new' entries had to he made. McKaj,
Kerr, Keddell, Hill and Opie divided the 12 champion-
ships, and Wellington retained the championship shield
with 46 points.
Gerald Keddell afterwards fled to Invercargill
and swept the board at the Southland championships meet-
ing The 100yds., 220yds., 120yds., and 440yds. hurdles
and hop, step and jump trophies all went into his kit ,
and after that Southland took a hitch at its kilts, re-
moved its “ bonnet ” and acclaimed Keddell as All-
Round Champion of Southland.” Which, unless he takes
flight, means that he will be drowned in usquebaugh.

There was revelry in Auckland (M.L.), the
other night, and when'Auckland revels it can hold its
own with the next one, whoever he may be. The
occasion of the excitement was the defeat of the Canadian,
“ jimmy ” Fitzgerald, by L. J. Ornstein, a local flier,
over 10 miles, for a wager of £5O. What Auckland re-
joined over mostly was the magnitude of the defeat,
Ornstein winning by nearly three laps.
* * * *
A record performance has just been put up at
Glen ward (N.S.W.), where a hand of tough-muscled and
stout-hearted men carried an injured companion 20 miles
to Rawdon Vale. The road was over mountainous
country, and the patient was carried in a stretcher.
Seems to be a vacancy for a V.C. or a thumping big purse
of sovereigns round Glenward way.
* * * *
The small crowd of Australian and over-seas
professional runners at present sheltering in Victoria are
beginning to shake it up. Arthur B. Postle is alleged to
have waltzed over 50yds. in 5 l-ssee., equalling his own
world record ; and jack Donaldson claims to have run
the 100yds. in 9 2-ssec. This beat the great Duffy record
by l-sth of a second; but, alas for human greatness,
when the track was measured it was discovered to be
12 inches short of the 100yds.; and the record blew up.
These times have a wild aspect about them, but it is pos-
sible, of course, that Easterude at Stawell may see some
world records really broken.
. * * *
On a small ground in demure Windsor, the
Victorian croquet championships were decided lately.
The singles championship was annexed by J. Tuckettj
the doubles by N. H. Richards and Mrs. Klingender, and
the ladies’ singles by Miss Howatt. This writer doubts
whether he fully appreciates the niceties of the relaxa-
tion or religion, or whatever it is ; but the enthusiasts
became wildly excited over the conclusion of the ladies’
singles bout! An enthusiastic damsel with a gaudily-
decorated mallet explained that if Miss Howatt’s oppon-
ent-having pegged out the green, potted the yellow,
put blue through the rover, outed red and got white-
had sent black to the peg, 4ft. away, she must have won ;
whereas she didn’t, while Miss Hovvatt made a marvel-
lously long shot with black, got red, made the peg and
won. It was a great feat, no doubt ; anyway the writer
cordially agreed with the enthusiastic damsel.
* * * *
The football rumpus in Melbourne has produced
a new word—woofer. A “ woofer” is apparently a person
who barks but doesn’t bite—who looks round and sees a
mess, but doesn’t do a thing to put it straight. It was
first applied to ex-Senator Best, MJ3.R., and seems to fit.
This week is really the beginning of the Vic.
’oofball year, inasmuch as the newly-elected League and
Ass. delegates hold their first meeting. A few' of the old
representatives have retired, and a few have been fired
out by their clubs ; but mostly the same crowd rules as
before. The ’Roy club, which has been no great shakes
for some years, put “ reformers ” into all the club offices,
whereupon 14 of last season’s players passed in their uni-
forms. The new sec. and committee are skirmishing
round after promising youngsters, and also announce,
significantly and lamentably, that they “are in tieat.y
with a dozen or so top-notchers from other clubs.” Mean-
while the Ass. has fired out “for all time” the North
Melbourne delegate who made himself conspicuous In the
reform movement, and is busily engaged in ignoring the
challenge of president Prendergast, M.L.A., to do some-
thing to that club for having, as openly admitted, paid
its plavers £5BO last season. This being a violent frac-
ture of one of the Ass. rules, and North having gained
the premiership thereby, an Ass. with an ounce of self-
respect w'ould jump on the club with enthusiasm. But
there is nary a jump. Wherefore the public jeers. Before
next issue the two sets of new delegates are to foregather
to consider a scheme whereby one body will control the
game < '• business or profession ; so, if there is an explo-
sion or an earthquake or a loud call for disinfectants, the
public will know what it means.
The Northern (N.S.W.) Rugby Union, which
controls the sport in and around Newcastle, is tottering
badly. Last year it began with a credit balance of £62,
and though the year yielded £6Ol, the books closed with
a balance in hand of 9s. 9d. Amateur Rugby is in a bad
way everywhere. #
Longreach (Q.) Jockey Club held a meeting the
other night, and was discussing starting machines, when
a 3ft. snake crawled into the room. The members bolted.
No better start was ever witnessed. #
James Moorliouse, secretary of Adelaide Tatter-
sail’s Club, is retiring after 21 years’ service, during which
the club has grown from an infant to a strong body on
both the social and the sporting sides. Jack Goffage,
long secretary to the Licensed Victuallers’ Race Club at
Broken Hill, is to replace him next month.
;g rnce _the speediest miniature pony Sydney
racing has seen for quite a long time—reached the top
rung last week by winning the Ascot (Sydney) 14-hands
handicap under the mountain of 13st. 41b., conceding
from 581 b. to 811 b. to nine opponents, and speeding over
the half-mile in 51sec. Jockey Walsh (who had a joy ride
on the champion) carried over 801 b. dead weight, which
casts an extra halo around the champion piece of horse-
flesh What weight the brute will have forced upon him
henceforth is an awful nightmare to the prad’s owner.
Since the Maoriland betting law began to biff
the bookmakers and parasites, the totalisator has had its
value fully tested. Though there has been larger invest-
ments in many centres, Napier Park takes the sandwich
thus far. At the recent two-days’ meeting £21,561 was
hauled into the machine—a mighty increase over the
£ll 433 wagered in the machine last year. The increase
of £10,128 suggests that either the bookmakers have wal-
lowed in a joyous time, or else Maoriland is going to the
dogs mighty fast. *
The Sydney cricket season is hurrying down to
its close, and Saturday in Sydney saw the opening of the
10th round of the First Grade competition. It was a dull
afternoon, with rain in the air ; but the followers of the
flannelled fools came out in force and made a great noise.
Not that there was much to make a noise about, for no
one whoops over bowling. Public interest is always
centred on the batsman, and only one of him succeeded
in reaching three figures. This hero was P.
Newton, of Petersham, and he carried his bludgeon for
111 the total score being 254, made for the loss of eight
wickets. That was the .top score of the day. That 111 of
Newton’s was more than a three-figure score. It was
the stemming of a rot and the hauling of a rout
out of the fire. Newton’s slogging exhibition
was excellent, and the young man gave no chances
while bitting one sixer and 12 for four. Public
interest is keenest in the struggle between Redfern and
Gordon, and 10,000 looked on. Gordon went to the sticks
and smacked up 237 before the last man fell. Then Red-
fern got busv, and by the time the stumps were drawn
the board showed 28 for one wicket. Waverley made
204 and Burwood had made 46 without losing any timber
at sunset. Paddington accumulated 255, and University
responded with 16 for three wickets-which is a mighty
noor thing in the way of responses. Sydney would
appear to be in for a doing, making only 156 ; to which
Middle Harbor let fly 107 for the loss of only five wickets.
The best of the howling was Emery s six for, % '? T *•”
fern against Gordon ; R. B. Mirmetts six for 82 foi Uo -
versity against Paddington ; and three for 18 by Randall
of Middle Harbor against Sydney.

“Plum’’Warner & Co.’s loud yells about the
unfairness of Australian crowds found their way to South
Africa, with the result that the Springboks, according to
Captain Schwarz, “were thoroughly convinced that when
we P landed at Adelaide we would be greeted with glass
bottles.” (So they were—full ones !) *We also heard that
the cricket barrackers of Australia were nothing less than
accomplished larrikins. But we found bv experience
that the Australian cricket crowd is the most sportsman-
like in the world.” Now, how is it that these English
teams cannot be as gentlemanly and create as good an
impression among our people as these savage South
Africans? Is the Hub of the Universe a place of bad
manners, or what is the matter?
Mrs. Masseran, who trains Society’s body the
way Fashion savs it should go, will be at the Sydney
Metropole from April 10 to the 22nd. Never before has
the woman of generous proportions had to subdue ner
figure so severely; consequently, never before has
Society had more need of the famous corsetiere.
Hockey, which has thrived for several seasons
past in N.S.W., threatens to boom this winter and take
rank as a first-class sport. And certainly it is an exhila-
rating game, with all that spice of danger and risk of
smashed bones and sudden death that gives tone to
sport. The numbers of players increased enormously last
season, and this winter threatens to find several new
clubs in the fieid.
* * * *
A vague sort of cable from Florida (U.S.A.)
announces that Louis Disbrow won the 300 miles motor-
car race on Saturday last. It also stated that the time
(3h. 53min. 33see.)-was a world’s record. If it was done
on the road it Is easily so, for hitherto no .one has
averaged 78 miles an hour for 300 miles. But the chances
are that it was put up on the hard sand at the Ormond-
Daytona Beach, that splendid speedway where so many
records have been made; and, if that is so, it is not a
world’s record, as C. M. Smith, in November, 1909, drove
over 300 miles on the Brooklands track (Eng.) in 3h.
30min. 17 l-ssec., which is roughly 2bmin. better than
Disbrow’s time.
* * * *
An interesting feature of the Olympic Games
at Stockholm (Sweden) this year will be the 200 miles
cycle race around Lake Malar on July 6. It is to be a
combined individual and team race, and carries sundry
gold and bronze medals and diplomas. The teams
will be national, and may comprise as many as
nine riders, three of whom will be reserves.
Six men will start, and the first four will, count.
The event is to be entirely unpaeed, and is creating con-
siderable interest among the riders of the nations.
More’s the pitj T , Australia will not be represented,
although, on the times, it is less than doubtful whether
the European men are as good on the road as ours. In
Europe they always profess a profound incredulity re-
garding the times put up in the Dunlop Warrnambool-
Melbourne ride.
A speed-judging test took place last Saturday
under the wing of the Vic. Auto. Club, which did the
judging. Thirty auto, men were set a secret course,
which was disclosed to the contestants in sections only.
Along this they had to go at a set pace which varied from
16 to 23 miles an hour. The pace set was handed
to the contestants at each starting place. Some
of the judging was remarkable, the winner, J. H.
Grice, being only three seconds out in every mile. Fully
half the competitors got very close indeed ;| in the
majority of cases the speed was over-estimated.
, * * *
The collapsible bicycle is unknown in Australia,
but in France it has a great vogife, and alLthe French
cyclist regiments are equipped with folders. The
English military people stick to the ordinary Ngid-
standard type of bike for their troops, on the ground that
the process of folding and unfolding takes up too much
time, and that if the knack is acquired there is little or
no difficulty in marching with a bike slung over the
shoulder. # „
When N.S.W. cyclist A. C. Weston recently
bolted and won the 10-Mile Championship it was regarded
as a lucky fluke. But Weston evidently has some of the
real stingo about him, as last week he beat most of the
cracks in the struggle for the Half-Mile State Champion-
ship. Weston is one of the best of the new crop of
cyclists, and the old hands will find out all about it
shortly. , * *
The fearsome crop of aviation accidents last
year mostly occurred when the fliers were about to
alight. But evidently it was just a phase which is being
rapidly left behind, as of late accidents have been very
rare. A reckless Frenchman recently tried how
many descents he could make in a day, flying back-
wards and forwards over an aerodrome. He succeeded in
scoring 62 in a journey of 70 miles. In almost every case
the engine was stopped and had to be started again
before the next ascent was made. The test is interest-
ing and instructive. Evidently much more control over
the machine has been acquired.
The just-concluded N.S.W. swimming season
was a splendid one, and saw more swimmers in the water
than any other in the history of the State. It may not
have been so brilliant as other seasons—in fact, it wasn t.
But that doesn’t matter. Where before one could count
the swimmers on the fingers of the hand, this season the)
ran to scores ; and the most remarkable development of
all is the extent to which the exercise has caught on
among girls and women. Every afternoon sees hundreds
of women and young girls scattered round the baths of
the city. Mere infants can be seen striking out bo ' dl V 1,1
the shallow parts of the baths, with mothers and elder
sisters teaching them how, or holding them up by the
chin. And that sort of thing is infinitely better for the
community than a whole galaxy of new records.

Prime Minister Andrew Fisher and almost-
blind Gregor McGregor are now added to the list of
people who have motored from Melbourne to Adelaide.
Said Gregor McGregor after the trip was over : Twee
we lost our way near the Coorong. Once at night Mr.
Fisher and the chauffeur took one of the lamps and fol-
lowed the tracks back to a station to ask for instructions.
They walked altogether 15 miles. I remained in the car
and had a sleep. The Prime Minister got back at 3 a.m.
and was up again at five, boiling the billy We had two
rolls of matting and a shovel, or we would not have nego-
tiated the Coorong sandhills ; and once we got stuck in
mud.”

THROW AWAY YOUR PIPE
and
SMOKE MY CIGARS
dn/y\ t
I'VE knocked around a bit over Australasia in .
my time—been in the back parts of pretty
well every State and New Zealand.
I’ve mixed with shearers and musterers and
rouseabouts at different sheds; been at cattle
camps in Queensland; up among the snow
country of Monaro- in the jarrah forests of
Westralia; in mining camps ail over the Com-
monwealth; and everywhere I ve seen the boys
smoking their pipes—and seldom a sign of a
Cigar.
Then, I’ve pulled out my case and handed
some DIADEMAS around. And after all this,
people say to me —“Oh, Australians won’t smoke
Cigars!’’
ĥ
I do just get riled when I
hear this. I tell you that
nearly every pipe-smoker,
once he gets the taste of my
DIADEMAS Cigars, swears
by them. They knock pipe-
smoking hollow!
There’s nothing but To-
bacco in them—and the best
Cigar Tobacco that is grown
—the wrapper, finest Java
leaf; and the inside filling,
all clean, straight,long Brazil.
They draw easily and burn
freely, for they are hand-
rolled by as good Cigar-
makers as there are any-
where in the world.
The boys out-back I’ve
given them to, as I said, just
swear by my Cigars, Men
in the Cigar trade here, too
—men with whom Cigar-
smoking is a life habit—come
to me and say—“ Look here,
those Cigars of yours are
better than any we can get
in the city. Let us have a
box as a favor."
Now, I want you to try
my Cigars —at my ex-
pense.
I want to convert you from
the pipe to Cigars—to my
Cigars. All I want isfor you
to buy my Cigars as against
your pipe and favorite to-
bacco —and I know you'll be
a regular customer of mine.

IN
BANNANA LAND
Vowles, the chosen Denhamite candidate
for the late J. T. Bell’s Dalby seat, is a local
alderman and Gent One. tie opposed Bell for
Dalby once before; but that time he ran as a
Philpite against the Kidstonite, and his present
selection as a Kidstonite shows how completely
the Kidstonites have turned their jackets.
March 16 was the jubilee of the birth of,
the Eight Hours movement in Brisbane. The
meeting of 50 years ago took place at the North
Australian Hotel, a well-known pub of those
days; and that grand old democrat, Charles
Lilley, afterwards Chief Justice, proposed the
principal motion. It was not till nearly six years
later, however, that the first Eight Hour demon-
stration took place.
Felton station, on the Darling Downs,
which was Jimmy Tyson’s favorite property, was
sold, the' other day, by J. M. Greenaway; who
had bought it from the Tyson trustees. It ha,s an
area of 34,200 acres. The new owners are said to
have a notion of selling the stock on it—there are
31,000 sheep, 600 cattle and 200 horses—and cut-
ting it up for closer settlement. Greenmount,
Pittsworth and Clifton—three towns thereabouts
—should benefit greatly if this is done.
A decayed industry got a start again the
other day in Bananaland. Two men, after drink-
ing heavily, started for Mount Cuthbert to look for
work ; but on their way the thought of the graft
ahead of them drove them._to desperation; so
they stole some horses and ammunition and took
to “the road.” They stuck up an Afghan
hawker and tied up a Chinaman ; also they held
up the mail change, and finally a pub, where they
got cheap drinks. The police from all the points
of the compass were closing in, and the thought
of glory and stripes spurred the force on. But it
all had an inglorious end. Two men were found
sleeping off a drunk under a shady tree; and the
police woke them up (with difficulty), and accused
them of being the desperadoes they were after.
It was a railway accident that got late
Railway Commissioner Thallon his billet. He
came out originally in 1882 as traffic manager of
the Southern and Western lines. After looking
round, he recommended changes in the method of
working the lines; but Herbert, who was they
Commissioner, wouldn’t agree. Thallon said if
the changes were not made, there would beji
smash ; so he resigned, and got a billet in N. S.
Wales. In little more than a year the predicted
smash happened—at Bowen Hills. Thallon was
asked to give expert evidence at the inquiry
which followed. He did, and predicted another
smash, to take place between Brisbane and Oxley.
It duly arrived before the end of the year (1884)
at Darra, and was worse than the one at Boweji
Hills. Then Thallop was promptly invited to
take his old billet again. _ ;
The bush parson has to pay his footing in
all sorts of’coin. Central* Bananaland has one
who is notoriously good with his fists ; and some-
times he has to cash-up in that coin. Lately he
was asked to go out a bit further. Oirfe evening
he turned up at a homestead with his dusty outfit,
looking as little like a city parson as anybody
could. The boss wasn’t in a good temper. “ Who
are you?” he asked grumpily. The parson
told him. “I don’t believe you ; but if you are
not a liar the best way to prove it is to put on the
gloves—there’s a couple of pair somewhere.” The
parson said something—apparently that he didn’t
want to start that way—and then something else
was said, and before the little crowd loafing
around knew what was happening 'the two were
“mixing it’’ with bare knuckles. TTbe parson
wasn’t out to do damage, till the boss' gpt one in
on the nose. Then he took things seriously, and
the boss was down and out inside a minute.
When he recovered, he got to his feet. '“ You’re
not a liar, Mr. —he said. “ You’re the best
parson that’s been this way!” Then they
shook hands. “ See to the parson’s horses.” the
boss called out to a joey, and they went inside.
“ Boro-Belle” on a Northern disease :
The sugar cocky worships cane with an even worse
enthusiasm than the butter cocky worships Cow. It is a
disease. There is hardly one Bananaland cane-grower
Who produces a spud or even a pumpkin. Cane is over
every foot of ground, right up to the walls of the living
house. (n the Mackay district they have the fever in a
very acute form, fdr there they grow cane on the local
racecourse and in the cemetery. Produce that might
easily be grown locally as a side-line.is regularly imported.
Two well-equipped butter factories were erected in the
district. Both are closed, owing to cocky being too busy
watching the cane grow to find time to milk a cow.
Timber abounds in the district (one man built a pub
-entirely of local cedar), yet the cookies send away for all
their building material. At a co-operative mill, when
the cocky shareholders wanted to know why a bridge had
not been tihilt, the chairman stated that they could not
buy timber in Mackay “ for love or money.” He tearfully
added that, as they required 20,000 ft. of timber, they had
decided to obtain the logs in the district and cut them
down with a saw-bench of their own, and not a solitary
cocky was moved bv this statement to throw his hat in
the air and shout “ Hooray for local industry.” T can see
the time coming when the inhabitants will develop cane
whiskers.
“British Ensign ” : Every little while you
can read things like this in the Northern papers :
Mr. Justice Shand returns from Brisbane by the
Kumano Maru on Monday next.
Mr. Justice So-and-so or some other Government
official don’t travel by Jap boat because there is
no other; often one of the magnificent coastal
boats leaves the same day. Seeing that the
Government subsidises a line of vessels which
must carry white crews, it is About up to it
to intimate that, when its servants travel at
State expense, they must travel by the vessels
which it supports, and not by the opposition.
When the Judge sentences a man for (say)
assault, I presume he does it in the interest of
law and order, and I don’t suppose he would
think of leaving the court and paying somebody
a sovereign of public money to go and assault
some other party. Yet that wouldn’t beany
more ridiculous than this practice of paying State
money to support certain vessels which run under
certain conditions, and then paying other State
money to support opposition vessels which run
under exactly contrary conditions.
“ Myrtle ” : First it was the coach that
was biffed out by the motor-car; now the creak-
ing wool waggon has been challenged by the
motor-van. Longreaeh is trying a vehicle capable
of carrying 10 tons, with broad tyres, and a 12 to
15 mile speed. An extra man for night shift is to
be put on, and there will be a continuous smell of
petrol from start to finish —night and day.
Bluffer Badger says his Brisbane Tramway
Co. has hung up the question of needed exten-
sions until after April 26. “ Are you afraid of
the trams being nationalised?” he was asked.
“No,” the bluffer wasn’t. “But, if the vote is
‘ Yes,’ the control of the tramways will not be in
this State, but in the hands of -a .Board in Mel-
bourne.” At present, the control is in the hands
of a Board in London; but let tßat pass. Ine
bluffer no doubt means by silly gulf
that wages may be regulated by the Com-
monwealth. The regulation of wages wili
in that case be in the hands of a High Comt,
whose Chief, so far from being ‘ a Board in Mel-
bourne,” is ex-Premier and ex-Cmef Justice of
Queensland. Big Yankee cos. no doubt use their
power and wealth to squeeze the people into
voting the way they want them to go in politics ,
but Badger will probably find that when he tries
to introduce his Yankee notion to Australia the
public here will kick good and hard as a prelim-
inary protest.

THE CRIMINAL (disgustedly, after taking slock of the only literature the law allows): "'Ere,

Brisbane University authorities make it
their proudest boast that both sexes are admitted
to that institution ‘‘on an equal footing.” Yet
one does not notice the name of any female pro-
fessors anong the teaching appointees; and it
Bella Bluestocking, M.A., should by chance get
a billet at any time on the Uni. staff, she will
probably be paid about two-thirds of the salary at
a male colleague. In the Queensland Education
Department the salaries of male and female
teachers who are doing equal work differ even
more widely than that.
The Bananaland Lands people have been
told one particular yarn so often that they go to.
S leep now whenever they hear the start of it. it
is to the effect that, if the interviewer can get on
to a really decent bit of the earth, a few hundred
others in Canada, England or other foreign parts
will come like the hammers of Sheol to the State
as soon as he says the word. At one time, when
Bananaland was greener, this alleged sampler of
State lands was given a picked block as a bait.
But as the expected rush never eventuated, the
alleged advance agent is now given the warm hand
and the cold eye.
“Kobbi” : Bananaland’s Home Secretary
has got some old ideas—new to these parts—to
put into the local prisons. “Increased comfort
in the cells is the keynote; 60 of them in Bris-
bane are to have “ chair, locker and a few extras.
These are for the comfort of the obedient ana
repentant villain inside the gaol walls. Reading
matter will also be supplied, and increased gratui-
ties for those who do well in the workshops, ihe
Home Secretary should, however, keep going.
The other day I travelled to Rockhampton from
Mount Morgan in a crowded carriage. Among
the congregation was a John Hop and a hand-
cuffed prisoner. There’s room for improvement
in that! , TT .
Thanks to the Waterside Workers Union
and the Seamen’s Union, some of the sugar mills
of Mackay will be a shade “ whiter” this year.
Hitherto, out of the 10 sugar factories in the dis-
trict, four have been in the habit of employing
discolored aliens along with the whites. But that
mongrel mixture won’t last much longer, as the
two unions which have so much to do with hand-
ling the nearly-finished article in transit to Sydney
for refinin'g, have notified that the sugar will not
be handled this year if any colored aliens are em-
ployed in the mills. Four months’ notice has
been given, so there should be ample time, before
the crushing commences at the end of June, to
hunt up extra white workers.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane. April 1, 15)11.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Government House is taking it easy just now to
gather strength for the hard graft to come. Next
week his Ex. will go north, and for 40 days and
nights will voyage through the milk and honey of
the Backblocks. Meantime the Vision will act
as deputy-Ex. On Saturday last the lady made
one of the most attractive flowers at a horticul-
tural show at Toowong, when she ran the eye of
approval over ferns and blossoms, and the inevit-
able d’oyley and scone, and announced to the
multitude that the exhibition was open. She will
probably accompany the Chieftain to Goondi-
windi on Wednesday, and assist in opening the
Show there.
Satin and orange blossoms are at a premium
locally, and you can scarcely move without being
hit by a flying old shoe of luck. Next \veek two
of our most eligible medicos go to Sydney to
marry and bring back wives. What is wrong
with us, indeed ! Dr. Jack Thomson will pro-
mise the usual happiness to Miss Elsie Forsyth ;
while Dr. Herbert Stewart will assure Miss Ruby
Taylor, of Bexley, of his intention to love, etc.
On Wednesday last Miss Doris Davidson, a sister
of Dr. Guilford Davidson, of Dr. C. Davidson
(Rockhampton), and of Mrs. (Dr.) Wilton Love
was given by the medical family into the safe
keeping of a gentleman who wastes a lot of the
alphabet through being called Colquhoun.
And new engagements are being announced at
breakneck speed. Victor, of the Coochin bells,
has received a favorable answer from Miss Kite
Darvall ;andMr.F. Watts and Miss Zoe Gallagher
will obey the will of Cupid next month. Ihe old
station homestead of Jondaryan will hear wedding
bells on the 19th, when the youngest Williams
daughter will become Mrs. William Kent. A
heap of good wishes and more substantial realities
are being addressed to Jondaryan. A well set-up,
athletic girl, with the true sportsman s instinct,
is Miss Gwen, and her friends wish her a rose-
lined future.
Queensland will be well represented at the
head-oiling of George V. The latest departures
for Fogdom are the Mileses. C.S. is the manager
of the Government Savings Bank, and better
known to our “buds” as the father of Lieutenant
Nigel Miles, R.N. Mrs. Bocock, for so many
years on the staff of the COURIER, also eoes per
Cm rah. She tea’d her friends on Wednesday,
and between their attentions to the commissariat
they wished her the usual things.
For three davs before any trace had been found
of the missing Yongala, the vestibules of the news-
paper offices and the shipping corner at the
G P.O. were crowded by anxious women and
children, for the crew was largely recruited from
Brisbane. I leave you to imagine the grief
of widows and orphans when the first tell-tale
chaffbags were washed ashore. On Thursday, a
message reached the city from an irresponsible girl
in the North to the effect that the passengers ana
crew were safe ! The cruel mistake spread like a
bush fire, and increased sorrow followed the denial.
Persons who telegraph such statements withou
verification should be something very bad.
\mong the Yongala’s passengers was Mrs. A.
Murray, the wife of a well-known Cairns solicitor,
with her four babies, returning after six months
with her parents in N.S.W. The Rooneys, of
Townsville, were also on board. They had twice
before been wrecked. The first time I m wrecked
is the last time I go to sea, whether I m drowned
° r Passed through Brisbane last week, Mrs. Frod-
sham, wife of North Queensland, and
their children, after a summer spent in the Blue
Mountains. The lady was not a little anxious
lest her roof tree at Bishop’s Lodge should have
taken to aeroplaning. Luckily she was in -Eng-
land during the cyclone several years mo, .when
the Bishop, after the disturbance, found himselt
sole lord of a stack of timber and galvanised iron.
Mr. and Mrs. George Woolnough had a
“golden wedding” on March 27. The.mis er
half of the old couple was editor of the Brisbane
Telegraph for a quarter of a century, vg
Y ours, affectionately,

AN EMPEROR S APPRECIATION.
—- - M*
The Emperor of Austria has conferred a unique
distinction, as also has the Vienna Chamber of
Science conferred its highest award, upon Dr.
Scheuble, of Vienna, for his discovery of
“Samol,” a treatment for Rheumatism, Grout,
Lumbago, Sciatica, and kindred ailments. So
effective is this treatment that pain is relieved
within a few seconds of its application, and the
poisonous causes of these ailments rapidly
neutralised and eliminated.
As soon as the necessary arrangements can be
made, “Samol” will be obtainable from all chem-
