MEDLOW BATH, Blue Mountains, W.S.W
MAJESTIC HOTEL.
COMPLETE arrangements have been made by the new proprietor (Mr. W. H. Burgess), late of the Grand
Hotel, Melbourne, for the comfort of visitors during the forthcoming season.
Medlow Bath is an ideal resort. The crisp mountain air is a delight, and the mountains, in their
Spring garb, appeal to lovers of the picturesque. Arrangements can be made at the Hotel for motors,
carriages and riding tours.
Motor parties can be arranged any time to start from the City Office-or your own residence-direct
to the Majestic Hotel.
To pedestrians Medlow offers ever-varying attractions. The views in the mountains are superb, and
hours can be spent in walks through the Hotel grounds.
Hot-air pipes are laid in many rooms. Daily Motor trips are made from the Hotel to Jenolan Oaves.
Further particulars can be obtained from the Hotel, or from the Sydney Offices, 42 Oastlereagh Street
W. H. BURGESS, Proprietor.

The Inky Way.
“A.W.A.”: “The Snake’s” graphic narration (B.
29/12/’10) of the eager struggle on the part of
pressmen to report the debates of the Commons in
the latter part of the eighteenth century, and the
subsequent conflict between Press and Parliament, is
highly interesting, but mostly wrong. The Commons
themselves were not averse" to the publication of
debates, and probably had as warm a desire to see
•their “tripe” in print as present-day politicians. It
was George 111., a consistent and strenuous opponent
of any extension of the popular rights, who used his
influence (pretty considerable in those days) to sup-
press the reporting. In 1771, George wrote to Lord
North: “It is highly necessary that this strange and
lawless method of publishing debates in the papers
should be put a stop to. But is not the House of
Lords the best Court to bring such miscreants before;
as it can fine as well as imprison, and has broad
shoulders to support the odium of so salutary a
measure?” George had a reason for trying to keep
Parliamentary doings dark. One Burke, at that
time in business as a member of Parliament, had
a habit of noticing things and of saying things
which, if published, would not have tended to
boost up what remnants of belief in the wisdom
of King George might have been cherished in the
popular mind. Among a host of other things
Burke was known to have hinted that George en-
couraged a high degree of mediocrity as a qualifi-
cation for public office.
* * «■ *
“B. de D.”: While the controversy between
“Hassan” and his opponents drags along, I draw
a scarlet herring across the track. It is time a
word was said on behalf of the casual three-half-
pence-a-line men. The rankest junior is in a better
position than the liner. Conceding that l%d. a
line is adequate (though I don’t see why this should
not be the minimum, with higher pay for superior
stuff), surely the liner should get something for at-
tending meetings which turn out to be not worth re-
porting. I mean, of course, when he has a commission.
At present the casual may find himself put down for
an engagement. He attends, but if no copy results,
no pay results. Or, again, he may write his re-
port, and it may be set up; and then a rush of ads.
squeezes it out "and he gets nothing. By attending
the meeting he has, perhaps, lost a couple of hours
in which he could have picked up something to
make lines out of. It seems bare justice that a
fee for the attendance of casuals at meetings should
be fixed. Of course, if a man goes to a meeting
without a commission it is different; he expects n<.
pay unless his report is inserted.
* * * *
The death-rate amongst country papers in Queens-
land has been heavy this year. Last month the
Gvmpie Mixer, established in 1880, put up the
shutters and went out of the business for good. The
Miner had hardly time to be decently buried before
the Port Denison Times (Bowen), the oldest paper
in North Queensland, established in 1864, flickered
out. In its last two years no less than 13 different
editors had tried to pump oxygen into the dying
paper, amongst them being two well-known Australian
bards —Francis Kenna and E. S. Emerson (“Milky
White”). The last editor, A. Y. Yennard, was only
called in when the patient was gasping. The Ger-
aldton Sentinel, which has been sickly since its
birth, also dropped dead; and that tragedy was fol-
lowed bv the death of the Ravenswood Mining
Journal," whose plant is now being shifted to Pros-
per! ne —one of the districts which “white” sugar is
making to boom.
& * * * *
There doesn’t seem to be much spare room for
“nature writers” in London. A Melbourne “nature
journalist” made inquiries a few weeks ago with a
view to trying his fortunes in the Cold Country.
He has just received a reply to the effect that in
England there are 3000 journalists doing “nature
work” for magazines and newspapers. He has de-
cided to stay in Australia.
* * * *
“Gideon”: Mention in a recent Bulletin of the
fact that a Gippsland man owns a copy of the
Commonwealth Mercury, dated from September 2
to September 9, 1658, and containing an account
of the demise of Oliver Cromwell, reminded me that
I possess a similarly-dated copy of the Gazette,
“comprising the sum of foreign intelligence, with
the affairs now on foot in the three nations of Eng-
land, Scotland and Ireland, for information of the
people.” Referring to Oliver as “a prince, indeed,
among the people of God,” the Gazette sets out
that in foreign parts “he was by good men univer-
sally admired as an extraordinary person raised up
by God, and by them owned as the great protector
and patron of the Evangelical profession. This
being said, and the world itself being witness of it,
I can onely adde that God gave him blessings pro-
portionate to all these vertues, and made him a
blessing to us by his wisdom and valor to secure
our peace and liberty, and revive the ancient re-
nown and reputation of our native country.” The
tea advertisement mentioned in The Bulletin as
appearing in the Mercury, also appears in the
Gazette; but in my opinion a far more interesting
advt. is that announcing the publication of a new
book, entitled “A Few Sighs From Hell, or the
Groans of a Damned Soule being an exposition
of those works in the sixteenth of Luke, concerning
the Rich Man and the Beggar, wherein is discovered
the lamentable state of the damned, their cries,
their desires in their distresses, with the determina-
tion of God upon them. A good warning word to
sinners, both old and young, to take into considera-
tion betimes lest they come into the same place of
torment. Also, a brief discourse touching the pro-
fitableness of the Holy Scriptures by that poor ser-
vant of Jesus Christ, John Bunyan,”
THE DEAR OLD SHEET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Matrimony.—Young Chinese desires acquaintance of
ambitious dark young Lady l , with small capital pre-
ferred, view -above. No triflers. S.S. Oxford-st.
P.O, —S. M. Herald, 9/3/’ll.
The Herald is a kindly sheet;
Observe, for instance, how
It pads about on gouty feet
Matchmaking for a Chow.
It mates the love-sick Chow, and so
For Silver ft may soon
Go gladly seeking high and low
A mate for some baboon.
It keeps wet blankets by for those
Who walk in Labor ways,
But hides Fat from his raging foes
’Neath feather beds of praise.
Of bolsters soft it keeps large stocks
Wherewith to prop up Fat,
And save his nerves from sudden shocks
And thuds he trembles at.
And all of Life’s new-fangled things
It cries “O Lor’!” and wilts;
O’er good hard facts it ever flings
Its grimy patchwork quilts.
It “drats” wild Labor to its face,
And all Fat’s cupboards slams
Whilst working at a dodd’ring pace
At futile pillow-shams.
No soul is wedded, dead or born —
The thing is not complete—
Unless the fact upon some morn
Is mentioned by the Sheet.
A poor unwanted babe or two
Wail ever in its hall;
If you are “kind” there’s one for you.
(ffo questions asked at all).
“A dear old body”—nearly all
Sum up the old thing thus,
Who knits for Fat a woolly shawl
And o’er him makes a fuss.
In slippers felt, it pads about.
As only old things can ;
It carries vessels in and out,
And brings the warming-pan.
By bolster, quilt, and feather bed,
By warming-pan and Chow,
The old identity, ’tis said,
Makes its small living now.
N.S.W.

TERRIBLE SHOOTING PAINS 8 SHORTNESS OF
BREATH-MANY TREATMENTS PROVE USELESS.
m
Mr. MAYOR,
cured by Rile Beane.
THE grave perils of neglecting to attend to
Nature’s warnings that the system is dis=
organised and requires attention is illus=
trated by the painful experience of Mr. Henry
Mayor, of 1048 Hay Street, West Perth, who says :
“ I have led a very active life, and my eagerness in
business pursuits led me to neglect my health, with
disastrous results. By not paying sufficient attention to my
diet nd the condition of my bowels, my system became run
down. I had to pay the full penalty for such carelessness, for
soon I was the victim of that awful complaint—Constipation. I
felt greatly depressed, and a feeling of dread, as if something
was about to happen, would be continually with me, while
terrible pains would shoot through my head from temple to
temple. A fulness under the heart caused me to gasp, and I was
frightened to breathe for fear of this terrible stabbing pain that
would shoot through my body. None of the medicines 1 took
did me any good; in fact, I seemed to be worse afterwards, and life
became a burden. Sleep at night was impossible, and I would
be tossing about in bed for hours. The feeling of suffocation
that used to overtake me was very distressing.
" For a considerable time 1 suffered, taking all sorts of medicines and so-called reme-
dies without any good results. A friend recommended me to take Bile Beans, and, more to
please him than anything, I did so. Bile Beans gave almost Immediate relief, their action
on the bowels being most mild and soothing, with no griping pains like the ordinary
remedies I had been taking. The dizziness and headaches disappeared, and the fulness and
wind around the heart left me. As I continued with Bile Beans my appetite returned, and
the great mental depression was removed. My general health Improved, and after I had
undergone a full course of Bile Beans I was completely cured of all my ills. Now I feel
splendid, being healthy and strong, and able to transact my business and enjoy life.”

Matchless for the Complexion.
Welcome!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WELCOME the children of thunder!
The younglings of battle,
The steeds of the diking,
The horses steel-bridled
And shod with the lightning—
Welcome the aetheling of thunder !
Kings of the nethermost tempest,
Furnaced and furnished,
Battered with hammers,
Braced with grey metal,
Crowned with authority,
These shall come after —
Welcome the sethelings of thunder !
£rrey seeds of history,
Sown on the waters,
The compassing waters,
Pregnant with destiny ;
Lo, to the thunder-call
Speak we your welcoming,
Children of doom !
Visions have smitten us—
Flames of the furnaces
Leap to our bellows-blast;
Thor at the anvil
Batters the furnaced bar;
Ribs of the South Land,
Torn from long darkness,
Ride on the summer wave—
Leaps the loud lightning.
Our gauntlet thrown down !
Death ! We have stricken deep !
Bolts of our hurling,
Red with destruction,
Compass the darkness !
Shudder the mountains,
Tremble the peoples !
What shall the doom be,
Children of flamei?
Welcome the aethelings of thunder.
The younglings of battle,
The vision of lightnings !
Grey seeds of far destiny,
Sown on the waters —
Welcome the sethelitigs of thunder !

DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
Billy’s Mother.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FOUND myself out of work in Welling-
ton, Maoriland, and only £9 10s be-
tween me and starvation: the outlook
was desperate, for Wellington is a dreadful
sort of place, where a man wants to get
£9 10s. per day to make life endurable at
all. When the wind tvasn’t blowing the
mud and stones about the streets it was be-
cause it was raining so hard that the wind
couldn’t get between the drops. I used to
get about four new hats in Wellington every
day—that is, the hats were new to me, but
not fresh from the shops. When one turned
the corner he was certain to lose his hat, un-
less he held it with both hands. When that
misfortune did happen, the loser never chased
the vanished tile, he simply stood still with
his hands stretched out in the manner Moses
is understood to have held his extremities
when a fight was on, and waited till the
wind brought him someone else’s head-gear.
If it fitted he would just cram it on and
start off. If it didn’t fit, lie, would carry
it in his hand till he met another man doing
the same, and they would effect an exchange.
It was awful work tramping round the
offices, seeking employment. Things were bad
in Maoriland in ’93—the country was still
suffering from the effects of the Atkinson
ahti-Socialistic legislation, and the back-
wash of the Australian bank smash had
struck the place. In some of the offices tney
were sympathetic, in others they were too
worried to sympathise with anybody; and
there were a few in which I was greeted
with the information that the managing
director was supposed to have been drowned
in a boating accident off Peucarrow, and had
the key of the safe in his pocket; so that
they would have to ask me to call back in a
day or two, when there was a reasonable
chance of the body having been recovered.
In such cases I didn’t trouble to ask for
employment; a sort of intuition told me it
was not worth while.
A week of that sort of thing caused me to
decide to become a Parasite.
It is one thing to decide to become a Para-
site, and another to find a Host. If you try
and fasten on anyone permanently the brute
soon succeeds in scratching you off, and it
took 24 hours’ hard thinking to decide howto
do the thing properly. Then I took an of-
fice which the last tenant had vacated with-
out paying the rent, and the landlord said
that, as I was just starting, he would charge
me only 2s. per week extra for the use of
the furniture and fittings the other fellow
had left behind him. It was a comfortable
little room, and the furniture was a great
boon, for my capital was running out.
Then I bought a bottle of ink, got some
paper with my name and the additional
words “& Co.” in the corner, laid in a bottle
of whisky, a few cigars and a box of cigar-
ettes, and sat down to wait for Hosts to
come to me. I guessed they would come
when they saw what was written on the
marble slab inside the big front door down-
stairs, about money lent and business of
any sort transacted.
The first Host who came was a tradesman
sent by my landlord, who had *340 of book-
debts to be collected. It was a blow to my
dignity—but I had no friends in Wellington,
and money was scarce, and getting scarcer
rapidly.
* * * *
On my list was one woman who was hard
to find. Her address was Ardrossan House,
Elgin Court. It had an aristocratic sound,
and the fact that she owed £8 for
meat had caused me to look for Ardrossan
House and Elgin Court in the fashionable
qua i ter of the city. The place was not
marked on any map that I could find, and no
one seemed to know its whereabouts. At
last, when taking my clothes to be cleaned, I
caught sight of the name Elgin, half obliter-
ated by rain and rust. This tiny corner of
a Idir.d alley was .Elgin Court, and right at
the end of it a big building with an arch let
through it was marked A/drossan House.
I walked up and tapped at the door. A
faded hag appeared, and glanced at me
wearily. An unkempt head ot a female ot
uncertain age looked over her shoulder.
“Are you Mrs. Albyn?” I asked.
She brought her upper teeth down over
her underlip in a scandalised sort of way :
“oh, noand then, with an evil grin, she
jerked her thumb towards the arch, and shut
the door suddenly. ■_ .
As I stepped down and. walked into the
arch I could see her peeping out of a side
window at the door which she said was Mrs.
Albyn’s.
I knocked moderately. There was a pat-
ter of tiny feet, a baby voice called "Muv-
ver!”' Then the door opened softly, and a
ladv, in faded but fashionably-cut clothes, ap-
peared, holding in her hand a child’s boot,
which she was obviously trying to mend.
She bowed gravely.
“Good-morning! Are you Mrs. Albyn?
She nodded, with the half-guilty smile of ■
person who has been discovered in spite of
herself.
“I suppose you are Mr. Morton’s collec-
tor?” sue sam sadly.
“Yes, mauam, unfortunately.” Then I
stopped; 1 didn't know what to say —the
sight of that ragged little boot took the
words out of my mouth'.
"And you want money?”
“Yes, madam —just whatever you can
spare, you know.”
"To-day I can spare none, for I have
none. Come round next Thursday after-
noon, and I will see what I can do.” And
she gave me a smile that was beautiful and
winning—-and next moment she had boweu
and closed the door.
On Thursday the streets were rivers. The
mud was whirled in sheets by the conflicting
winds that fought for supremacy at every
street corner. An Antarctic blast howled
spasmodically, and froze the flying mixture.
It was awful. But a stern sense of duty,
a fearful anxiety to get that £8 account re-
duced for poor old Morton’s sake,, drove me
up to Elgin Court.
The very wind seemed to shriek derisively
as I waded down that lane, i had to knock
nearly a dozen times before the door opened
slightly, and the pale, shell-like face with
the crown of brown-gold hair peeped through
the aperture.
“Oh, come inside; isn’,t it a dreadful day.
I had almost concluded that you wouldn’t
come. Your feet must be soaked. Oh, you
have gum boots, what an excellent idea. I
wish I could get a pair of those —I have to
go abroad in all weathers.”
“Yes, it is wet,” I said, as I entered.
“Take oil' your coat and come and sit
down till the rain ceases—it is too dreadful
io be out. It was good of you to call, and
such a day, too.” And then the dingy,
gloomy hall was lit up by the glory of her
smile..
“Now, what kind of creature is this at
all, who asks a dun in to dry ?” I asked my-
self, as she helped me off with my coat.
I followed her into a tiny drawing-room.
The furniture was very old, but heroic at-
tempts had been made to polish it; and there
was an atmosphere of refinement about the
place. I sat down before the tiny scrap of
fire in the register grate, and a beautiful
child of about four looked up at me with
wide, wondering eyes of lustrous brown.
“Is this your baby?” I asked, stroking the
child's brown ringlets.
“That’s my littlest baby—God help me,
they are all babies, the Whole seven of them.
Rosalind and her twin brother are the eldest,
and they are not much more tnan babies.
They are at school, and will be home as
soon as the storm will allow them.”
“Have they far to come?”
“Yes, it is a good step, and the streets are
so muddy. I couldn’t let them go last week,
so they have to go all weathers this week to
make up their time or I should be fined.
Billy’s at home this week.”
“Are you a widow, Mrs. Albyn?”
“Yes, sad to say»—will you have a cup of
cocoa?”
Outside the rain had stopped. A scrap of
sunshine gleamed faintly for a moment and
then gave way to greyish gloom. She rose
hastily and hurried out. I sat watching the j
door she had vanished through, and while I j
watched, a little boy, who might have been j
the original of Bubbles, peeped in.
I beckoned him over. He came gingerly, ,
and his little feet were bare.
“Are you Billy?”
He nodded and whispered, “Yes.”
“Why aren’t you at school, Billy?”
“Haven’t got no boots, an’ I catch cold
when I go wivout boots, don’t you?”
“Yes, Billy. Do you like stickjaw?”
“Got any?” he said, with a gurgle of de-
light.
“No, but I’ll bring you some when I come
again.”
His face fell—his lip stuck out; I thought
he would have cried.
“Perhaps mamma will let me carry yon
down to the shop at the corner and buy you
some when I go.”
“You ask her; she’d spank me if I did”;
he said knowingly, as though he always got
on well with debt collectors.
Then his mother returned with two cups
of cocoa, and some thin bread dotted here
and there with butter. She bundled Billy
out with great suddenness.
Then the baby that had been sitting so
quietly on the hearth climbed suddenly on to
my knee. The mother snatched it away
hastily.
There was a knock at the front door, the
children came in from school, and received
small slices of bread and butter. They were j
a beautiful family, boys and girls all had i
the crowning gift, or curse, of almost an-
srelic features. The woman had recovered
her composure, and talked in a quiet, ra-
tional manner. She was pleasant, well-
read, and full of interesting ideas.
* * * *
It was 5 p.m., and after dark, and the
woman’s face was only a shadow when I rose
to go, without having asked for anything on
account. I asked if I might carry Billy on
my shoulder down to the stickjaw shop round
the corner of the lane. She demurred ; it
was very kind, but I would find that Billy’s
weight trebled every two yards. She knew.
I asked as a special favor. She asked me
to .wait till she put stockings on him. And
when Billy’s feet had been covered I put him
on my shoulder and trotted him down to the
stickjaw emporium, and then to the boot
shop across the way, and bought him a pair
with bright, burnished copper toes, which he

He walked back, and I lifted him across
the muddy places till we reached the door,
where I put him on the step, banged the
knocker, and hurried off. A faint light in
the Court showed the door open, showed her
bend down over Billy, who held his feet up
alternately. Then she put her arms round
him and took him inside.
* * * *
I had a faint, indefinite idea that I was
being cajoled. But one thing I felt abso-
lutely certain of, and that was that in that
distressful period no woman who had seven
children to support could be in a position to
work off £8 worth of dead horseflesh.
Next morning Morton called in. He was
mightily pleased with my success.
“Did you find Mrs. Albyn yet?”
“Yes, found her yesterday.”
' She’s a bad ’un, that. I dursen’t go nigh
her at all. If I go round with a summons
in me pocket, dang me if she don’t just look
at me so sweet like, and them darned kids
with their angel faces comes round, and in-
stead of serving her with the summons I
alius comes away and sends her a jint or
two. They’re a domned sight too good-
looking—oh, she’s a bad ’un. You swear at
her, and scare her—l can’t; but you’re a
harder man than me, and you just bullyrag
her.”
“I’ll see you d —d first —-she’s a widow
and got seven children. Where’s your hu-
manity?”
“Mostly gone in jints, and rump-steak
when she was ill”; and he pocketed his
money and toddled out.
* * * *
Next time I called she received me gravely,
and without the rush of effusive language.
She was almost reserved, and conversation
was rather difficult, till she suddenly lifted
her eyes with a sad, expressive smile:
“I don’t know how to thank you for Billy’s
boots —it was very kind, but, but” — Then,
ivhen I saw how happy she was, I felt unut-
terable things. And again that demure,
quizzical look straight into the eyes, which
i could not interpret properly, and was
afraid of misunderstanding.
I felt a mighty longing to take her in my
arms —but had still some glimmerings of rea-
son left; and commenced to discuss the
political situation. I discussed it for two
hours, and came away once more without
having mentioned Morton’s account. Never-
theless I entered tip in his book that Mrs.
Albyn had paid 2s. 6d. on account; an entry
which moved the old butcher-man to wild
hilarity when he read it.
I felt that Mrs. Albyn was growing on me
rapidly. I began to drift up to Ardrossan
House in the evenings, but was shoo’d out
religiously at 9 p.m. I had asked her re-
peatedly to come to dinner at the Trocadero,
but she had always declined. Then I solved
it another way. I got a cab and took the
dinner to Elgin Court, and managed to get
it in without attracting a crowd either.
For once this strange woman let herself
go. With a petunia blossom in her hair,
across a French salad and burgundy I saw
her eyes—they were the tint of the topaz—
flash with the joy of life. Outside the wild
Wellington weather howled and shrieked, and
its tumult made the room cosy and delight-
ful by comparison. The children were
asleep upstairs, and there seemed to be no
one in the world but we two. Everybody
else so far away.
The fire burned red and tranquilly in the
grate. I blew a cloud of smoke towards the
ceiling, and turned to look at her. That
curious, demure, but magnetic glance came
from her eyes, and
I felt my arm round her neck, felt the
warm, delicious touch of her lips—for a mo-
ment. Then she seemed to awake from a
trance, and, wrenching herself free, fled out
of the room.
I waited an hour, but she did not come
back. Then I got my coat, hat and um-
brella. and let myself out.
* * * *
Brief as it was, that touch of her lips in-
toxicated me. I kept to myself as much as
possible for a couple of days, and tried to
recall the sensation. It had something of
the nature of opium about it, and I wanted
some more. I thought a couple of days ot
hard work might cure me, but instead it
drove me frantic, and I got hungrier and
hungrier. Seven children were a handicap,
certainly, but they were all very beautiful
children, and there were some magnificent
swindles to be worked even in gloomy, hor-
rible Wellington. I felt that the only way
to make life endurable was to marry Mrs.
iUbyn.
While I was in this frame of mind Morton
called in, just to see if I had any dollars for
him. I gave him a few, and when he had
signed the receipt he' glanced down the list
once more.
"I see you can’t do no better nor the rest
of ’em with that there beautiful she-devil —
she’s a bad ’un, she is!”
“Look here,” I said fiercely, “don t talk
about Mrs. Albyn like that. She’s a lady,
and her inability to pay should command
our sympathy.”
Morton took one quick glance at me under
his bushy eyebrows.
“Come and have a drink!” he said in a
soothing tone, and led the way to the Stag
VVith Two Heads.
When I got back to the office I carefully
counted my cash. I found that I could just
pay Mrs. Albyn’s account, so it went into the
cash book as paid, and I set the money aside
carefully. Then I made out a receipt, and
enclosed it in a nice sheet of scented paper
with a soulful request to write and let me
know when I could see her again.
This left me short, so I went out to look
for someone to fleece, and made the great
mistake of trying to make a victim of
McGuire, the solicitor. He was so stag-
gered at my monumental impudence in try-
ing to defraud him that he became a sort of
a friend. He said that a fellow with the
cheek I had would unscrew the devil’s tail
when he wasn’t looking, and pawn it.
* * * *
Next day I was down with influenza, and
my luck was right out. That was the day
when I had intended to propose to Mrs.
Albyn. and had arranged for her to fall into
my arms, and say “Yes” in a whisper, while
I breathed into her glorious back hair; and
instead of all this I lay and dozed in my
dreary lodgings, and my head seemed the
size of ten, and outside it rained miserably
and blew horribly. And Mrs. Albyn didn’t
write.
I was in bed a whole week. The first day
I was able I dashed off a long letter to her,
and waited a week for an answer. None
came. I wrote again. But she never le-
plied. Then at last, unable to stand it any
longer, I fortified myself with many strange
drinks, and, getting into a cab, drove up to
Ardrossan House, Elgin Court. The an-
cient hag who had directed me to it in the
first place, told me Mrs. Albyn had shifted
to Tinakori-road ; and she laughed a laugh
of horrible amusement. She didn’t know
the number, but it was the third house past
the poplars at the corner, on the left-hand
side. Now, Tinakori-road is an aristocratic
and expensive place, and I came away be-
wildered. I called in at the office on the
wav back, to cool my head (which still
seemed too large) against the mantel, nd
found Morton’s son awaiting me to collect
any cash.
•‘Don’t suppose you’ve got very much,
bein’ bad.”
“Well, I’ve got all Mrs. Albyn's bill, and
that’s a fair amount in itself,” I said.
“Mrs. Albyn’s” Then he stopped
suddenly and grinned all round his face.
“When did you get it?” he asked, when he
recovered the use of his countenance.
“What the devil are you grinning at?” I
demanded in a burst of passion. “I collected
it yesterday by the exercise of < pressure
which almost amounted to cruelty.”
“I ain’t grinnin’ at anything—l was
tliinkin’ of somethin’!”
Then he took the cash and got out quickly
McGuire came in immediately afterwards, to
tell me in a friendly way some new aspects
of his bad opinion about me.
“Some silly ass has taken a fit on the
stairs, I think ” he said. “A weedy youth
in a black overcoat. He’s laughing like a
fool and kicking himself.”
“That must be young Morton —I think
there’s a strain of madness in that crowd.
Look here, I’m going round to Tinakori-
road. I’ll be there the rest of the day. Just
postpone any bad language.”
* * * *
I found the third house without difficulty.
Billy, in a brand-new suit, but still wearing
the boots I bought him, was playing in a
bright garden—that is, as bright a garden
as could exist in Wellington.
I put him on my shoulder to carry him to
the door —but soon lowered him again. The
’flu’ does pull one down. He ran on in
front, and, opening the door, led me into a
gaily-furnished drawing-room.
“H’m, evidently a boarding house; not
much chance of getting the dear creature by
herself here,” I murmured to myself. Billy
was shouting “Muvver, muvver, here’s some-
body to see us.”
There was a quick light step in the hall,
and next moment she swept in. It was the
first time I’d seen her dressed properly, and
she was a dream. The sight of her was too
much. I held out my arms and stepped to-
wards her, and said, “My darling.”
Then the door opened suddenly, and a
heavy hand was laid on my collar, and the
voice of old Morton, tremulous with rage,
burst out:
“You d —d scoundrel! Take your hands
off me wife. Get outer me house.”
Then he hustled me violently down tne
front steps on to the gravel path, and fe
on me heavily. When we got up 1 atme
lor his point. He stepped back, and i wa»
too weak and shaken to follow him.
“You jest get back and square up nay
counts, young man. I’ve two minds to hat e
the law on yer, fer this outrage.’
Then he went up the steps and slammed
the door, leaving me too wrecked for words.
* * * *
At an hotel near Mrs. Albyn’s old quarters
—an hotel where I was well and unfavorably
known——l gathered information. Morton,
dissatisfied with the 2s. 6d. he had received
on account, had gone round himselt to try
and collect at least half a sovereign, and the
widow had made a bold stroke, and. had col-
lected him bodilv. What she told him about
me I don’t know, but it didn’t seem to have
been favorable. .
Anyhow, Morton denounced me round aie
town till my business fell away to nothing.
I tried to get McGuire to sue him for libel,
and pay himself out of the costs when he
collected them, but McGuire said he reckoned
it would be cheaper to pay my passage to
Sydney. ,
It was a grev, sodden day w ten 1 sailed,
nrnl tlm wind blew like an unchained devil,
and Morton’s son was in convulsions ol
mirth again on the wharf.
C. A. JEFFRIES.
An Ebb-Tide.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OUT of the south, out of the hollow night,
Roareth the wind under a racing moon.
Bidding the black grotesques on a world
of white
Leap to his giant tune.
Window and door groan, and the writhen boughs
Shudder and groan, toss, and aloud complain,
Threaten, implore, clutch at the shaken house,
Lift, and are bowed again.
Hidden here in the dim, the sheltered room
No wind soundeth ; only a distant sea.
Level, rhythmic, pouring by in the gloom,
Filleth the ears of me.
Limbs relaxed, senses, importunate will.
Hushed, and all the life of me far withdrawn.
Slowly I breathe, slowly, slowlier still
Soul, thou art loosed. Begone !
Is it I, there on the narrow bed ?
Glimmering out of the shadow my white face ?
Is it I, drifting and strangely sped,
Silent by silent ways !
What is the leaping hearth-fire ruddy and warm,
Eyes that meet, hand in the clasp of hand,
What the echoing spaces of night and storm
Here in this silent land ?
Here is only I and a pulsing tide,
Only I and the spell of a spirit that knows.
Bid me no more return ! Unto thy side
Draw me at length—and close !
TASMANIA.
A. W.
